﻿RINGS OF DUST

Chapter Three

A Thousand Thrills

A small A-frame cabin sat nestled amongst White Fir and Sugar Pine trees at the
edge of Sequoia National Park. It was at the end of a dirt forest service road
and used to belong to Ulysses Nova, who had used it for hunting. Upon his
passing he left it to his favorite nephew Cole, who rarely visited. Cole would
check on the cabin between missions to see if the roof had caved in. He often
thought about selling it. It was a long drive from his apartment in San Diego, a
place he referred to as 'The Hotel Terra Firma,' since he rarely slept there.

After his last mission to Saturn, he could barely stand to be around people. He
never imagined that he would ever call the cabin home, or that his roommate
would be a six-limbed science project with bad gas.

Cole had just finished his weekly shower and as he entered the bedroom a picture
frame caught his eye. It was a group of classmates from the Academy. Corizon,
Phillips, Hadley. For some reason it triggered memories farther back into his
childhood. A jingle played in his head as he saw the commercial play out in his
mind.

'A Thousand Thrills.' He saw kids going down waterslides, racing go-karts. They
blissfully stuffed their mouths with cotton candy and funnel cakes. 'A Thousand
Thrills' was a theme park about three hours away from his home in Pasadena. He
remembered thinking that has to be heaven on earth. I have to experience it.

Cole would plead with his parents every weekend. It's only three hours away.
I'll pay for my own ticket and I'll even pay for gas.

At first they offered excuses. We've got a lot going on. Maybe next weekend.

When it became obvious that this wasn't just a phase he was going through, his
parents were irritated and blunt. We don't have the money. That would break the
bank.

These deflections worked. Cole felt deeply troubled by the idea that his family
was so financially fragile, so close to the edge. He began saving money from his
paper route and lawn mowing jobs, afraid that they might not have enough for
food or electricity. One day Cole's dad came home with a brand new Harley
Davidson motorcycle. Cole thought they were broke, yet here was his dad blowing
money on a toy.

Cole noticed that in spite of this purchase, the family fared just fine. They
weren't evicted. They didn't go hungry. They even kept buying ice cream. Cole
felt duped. What else were they lying about?

What Cole didn't understand then, and wouldn't understand for another year, was
what that Harley actually meant. His father hadn't bought a toy. His father had
bought a last thing. The motorcycle was a man making a list of everything he
still wanted to feel before he couldn't feel it anymore " wind, speed, the
particular loneliness of a highway at six in the morning. A man who had just
been handed a piece of paper in a doctor's office and was trying to decide how
much of it to believe.

In the evenings, Cole would hear his parents talking in the kitchen with the
door mostly closed. The conversations had a weight to them now that he couldn't
name. He would hear his mother's voice strain at the edges. You have to tell
them. And his father's voice, quieter, later. Not yet.

Cole spent those months planning his escape instead of listening. As he got
closer to his sixteenth birthday he set a goal to buy his own car. He spotted a
gray Toyota Tercel parked on the street a couple of blocks away. They were only
asking $1,500. That was doable.

He talked to his friend Marcus, and together they hatched a plan to stay in
Culver City over the summer. Marcus had an older cousin with his own apartment.
They could split rent and share a room. 'A Thousand Thrills' was only five
minutes away. They could spend three months not only enjoying the park but
getting paid as employees. Other kids at school had friends and relatives who
worked there, and they would push Cole's and Marcus' applications to the front
of the line. The plan was flawless.

The only family member Cole shared this plan with was Emily. She was a couple of
years older and his only sibling. Cole trusted her. She was the most loyal
person he knew.

Cole dove into work, eager to get his car and even more eager to get to Culver
City. Finally, after over a year, the day came. He finished Mrs. Weber's lawn
and ran home with the last twenty dollars he needed. He spread all the cash on
his bed and double-counted the piles. He gathered the money, stuffed it into a
large zip-lock bag, walked to his neighbor's door, and presented what seemed to
Cole a huge sum. He signed the bill of sale.

He stood in the driveway with the keys in his hand, sixteen years old, holding
everything he had earned. Fifteen hundred dollars. To anyone else it was a used
car. To Cole it was a fortune " the kind that takes a year and a half of cold
mornings and other people's lawns to build, dollar by dollar, the way you stack
bricks. He had amassed something. He felt the weight of it the way you feel the
weight of a first house, a first real thing that is yours and nobody gave it to
you. He didn't know yet that he was about to give it all away.

With the keys in hand, he opened the car and was greeted by the smell of old
sun-faded plastic and seat cushions. He could feel the memory of a thousand road
trips when he touched the steering wheel. He also discovered something he hadn't
planned on: it was a manual. After sheepishly knocking back on the neighbor's
door and getting a short lesson in how to release the clutch at just the right
pressure, he lurched the car a few blocks home.

His parents were surprised by the car. He wasn't going to tell them about Culver
City until the night before. The applications were filled out, the interviews
scheduled for the day after school let out. He already had his clothes separated
into two piles: what to take, and what to leave. He even had a small pile of
stuff he would allow Emily to use for the summer.

A week before his departure, Cole's mom and dad sat him and Emily down on the
couch. Cole felt the cushions go cold against his back.

His father did not look like a man who had hidden anything. He looked like a man
who was tired of hiding it.

The word was cancer. It had been cancer for eleven months.

Cole's mother held his father's hand and looked at the middle distance between
them and the wall. Emily's face, which usually beamed with happiness, was
crushed inward and silent tears ran down her cheeks. He broke for her. He could
not watch her cry without breaking.

In the following days it became clear what the summer would actually be. His
father would need more appointments. His mother would need to work more hours.
Emily was barely holding herself together. His dad moved through the house
slowly now, as if he had been given permission to stop performing. The Harley
sat in the driveway and nobody rode it.

Cole dreaded telling Marcus. In a school stairwell he said it plain: I can't go
to Culver.

What? Marcus' face went red. You're the one with the car. This was your idea.

I know. I'm sorry. He searched for the words. My dad is sick. Really sick.

Marcus stared at him for a long moment. So we just throw the whole summer away?

Cole looked at him and understood, in that moment, something he would spend the
rest of his life turning over. The look on Marcus' face was not grief. It was
arithmetic. He was calculating what he had lost and whether Cole's family
emergency was a sufficient explanation. He decided it was not.

School let out. Marcus found another ride to Culver City. When he did, he left
Cole a note under the front porch mat. It read: Find yourself a new best friend.
I will NEVER forget how you screwed me over. Marcus.

Cole had not been surprised by that word — forget. Marcus had always been the
kind of person who kept a ledger. He had been keeping one on Emily since middle
school. Cole remembered a hallway, Marcus and two of his friends with Cole
against the lockers, and then Emily rounding the corner. She was maybe fourteen.
She grabbed Marcus by the collar with both hands and swung him sideways into the
lockers hard enough that three classroom doors opened. She held him there,
looked at him, said one sentence, and let go. Marcus had gotten up off the floor
in front of his friends and not said a single word. That was worse, for him,
than anything else that could have happened. Cole knew, from that afternoon,
that Marcus had not forgotten it. That was the thing Cole had understood
from the floor — Marcus never forgot anything. He simply decided when to
use it.

Cole ended up delivering pizzas. Every time he heard stories about Marcus having
the summer of his life at the park, he sat with something complicated " a
jealousy he couldn't justify and a steadiness he couldn't explain. He had chosen
to stay. He would choose it again.

His father died that November. Cole was seventeen. The hospital corridor where
he waited was the quietest room he had ever stood in. Sound stopped at the edges
of it. He sat in a plastic chair for six hours and the only thing he could hear
was his own blood moving. When the doctor came out to speak to his mother, Cole
looked at the closed door and understood for the first time that some rooms you
do not get to enter.

The drive home was the longest drive of his life. Nobody spoke. His mother held
the steering wheel at ten and two the whole way and Cole watched the yellow
lines come and go in the dark and thought: this is it now. This is the world
without him in it.

The weeks after were a fog of casseroles left at the door, relatives who didn't
know what to say, and the particular cruelty of paperwork. His father's reading
glasses stayed on the nightstand for two weeks before anyone touched them. His
shoes were by the front door for a week before anyone moved them. Cole moved
them. He didn't tell anyone. He just picked them up and put them on the shelf in
the closet and stood there for a minute with the door open, and then he closed
it and went back to the kitchen table.

After a summer of grief and a fall of worse, Cole promised himself that he would
not be trapped in one place again. He started looking into the Space Academy,
but when he learned about the high-level classes required, he let himself be let
down for exactly one evening. Then Emily knocked on his bedroom door.

Hey. What's wrong? She sat on the bed next to him.

The Academy only accepts straight-A students. I'm not that.

No you're not, she said, and you're going to be. Look at me.

He looked up from the floor. She looked at him with a certainty that he had not
earned yet.

You can do anything you want. She let out a small laugh. It was the first time
he had heard her laugh since the hospital. But you're going to have to work
twice as hard as everyone else. She said it matter-of-factly, like a weather
report. Come on, poopy pants. I know you're not afraid of hard work.

I'm barely getting Cs, he said.

I'll help you. Every night. I've already aced every class on your schedule for
next year. She tilted her head. Or are you going to be a little bitch about it?

Cole wasn't used to hearing his sister curse. It shocked him into laughing. He
was convinced that with her help it might just be possible. What other options
did he have? Deliver more pizzas?

Every day before and after work, books and papers littered the kitchen table.
Emily and Cole filled out diagrams and scribbled in notebooks. Their mother
began sneaking in to watch them work. At first she would need a drink of water
or check if the oven had been left on. Eventually she became a regular fixture
in the study group. It turned out that she had been a real nerd in college.

What were you going to study? Emily asked her one night.

Chemical engineering. She sounded like she was admitting something. I always
found it fascinating that small changes could transform a substance. Even shape
a society.

Like coffee? Cole offered.

She smiled at him the way she almost never did anymore. I built a whole life for
another person, she said. Every choice I made, I made it thinking about what a
good wife does, what a good mother does. And then the person you built it for is
gone, and you're standing in your own life like a stranger in someone else's
house. She let the silence sit for a moment. Build your life for yourself.
That's the only life that survives losing someone. You never know when a person
could be gone. Her voice sounded strained in a way that wasn't sadness exactly.
More like something she had arrived at too late and was determined they would
arrive at sooner.

They got back to work with a greater sense of who she was. By the end of his
senior year, Cole had pulled himself to A's and B's.

The day he, Emily, and his mother drove down to San Diego for his Academy
entrance exam, the space ships were there waiting. Their presence stirred
something in him that he could not account for " something familiar, something
that felt like an answer to a question he had not known how to ask. Up there,
his only responsibility would be to his crewmates and the mission. No grief. No
kitchen table full of bills. No Harley sitting in the rain.

That day, he knew that if the Academy would give him a chance, he would not rest
until he was the captain of his own ship.

Captain Nova. He liked the sound of it.

A loud glugging noise from the other room broke the spell.

Oh no. Not again. Cole groaned.

════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

﻿RINGS OF DUST

Chapter Four

The morning light filtered through the tall pines, casting dappled light on the
forest floor. Cole hefted his old ax over his head and split the last log round.
The splitting crack was subdued due to the early spring moisture. Cole's breath
clouded in the crisp air. The soft rustle of leaves and the distant call of a
mountain jay seemed unbothered by this early morning habit. He paused,
straightening to scan the treeline. Warm light burst through a gently swaying
thicket of pine needles.

The low sun cast a hard, angular light as it peeked over the granite peak just
east of his cabin. His eyes were intense and watchful. His age, or was it his
pain, was increasingly apparent. As he approached the cabin where old wood met
new tech, he rubbed his hands together and let out a sudden and glorious howl.
Then, just as before, he continued to the comfort of home.

The door creaked open, and Bitboy groaned. The firelight from inside flickered
on Bitboy's brown and white fur, catching the streaks of auburn as he scurried
over to Cole. His mechanical limbs hummed softly as he reached around Cole's
thighs, and the poor monkey sighed in obvious discomfort. One of Bitboy's arms
reached out, tugging at Cole's sleeve, guiding him to the warmth.

Cole knelt by the fireplace. Bitboy watched his friend add a freshly cut log to
the fire through his delirium. The sap began to boil, and the moisture hissed
from the wood until it was dry enough to burn. Bitboy's eyes reflected the
dancing flames. The scent of pine mingled with the earthy aroma of the fire,
filling the cabin with a comforting fragrance. Cole's fingers brushed against
Bitboy's fur.

The warmth of the fire seeped into his skin, a stark contrast to the morning
chill. Outside, the high Sierra Nevadas loomed. Cole softened as he looked at
Bitboy. He felt deep gratitude for the unlikely companionship. The morning
light, the serene forest, and the warmth of the fire blended into a moment of
quiet reflection.

"You little bastard," Cole said. "What do you have?"

As if dropping a one-gallon glass bottle of ethanol next to an open fire would
obliterate his wrongdoing, Bitboy did just that: rolled on his back and forced
all six limbs into the air like a dead bug. With a brilliant puff, Cole's
expectation of a relaxing day went up in flames, along with the lingering fumes
from a well-finished bottle that should have lasted the damned monkey at least a
few days.

Cole blamed the Federation. Their quest to re-engineer life had ended in almost
complete failure. Other than Bitboy, no other monsters had made it past a year.
It turned out that attempting to craft a weapon with the learning capacity of a
human but devoid of moral complexities—a creature entirely expendable—was a
terrible idea. Now Cole was the unwilling father of a junky. A junky with
forearms containing a network of metal rods, which themselves acted as the
unholy foundation for retractable steel blades.

Bitboy's upgraded form required ethanol not as a vice but as a vital power
source for these and other mechanical augmentations. Even with its enhancements,
his primal brain lacked the discipline to moderate. Ethanol was akin to ambrosia
to him—fueling his strength, sharpening his intellect, and enhancing his
agility, making it irresistible. His inability to self-regulate his consumption
posed a risk of overindulgence to the point of shutdown.

Meanwhile, the world outside grappled with a crisis of its own. The airwaves
carried the manufactured accent belonging only to news anchors.

"Good morning, California. This is Rachel Vayes for Public Square News. The
energy crisis, which has left millions struggling from resource scarcity and
power outages, may finally be seeing a solution. During the opening day of
Defying Gravity 2041: Innovations in Combating the Energy Crisis, the tech titan
Alpha Corporation promised a long-term solution. Although initial
disappointments stemmed from the keynote speaker's disappearance, Alpha defied
the gravity of the situation by announcing that new energy-efficient implants
would be available on a subscription basis. More on the disappearance of
43-year-old Dr. Stephen Conrad tonight at ten."

Cole reached for the remote. Rachel Vayes. She had been running Alpha's
preferred version of the energy shortage for three years — the one where the
crisis was complex, the solution was coming, and Alpha happened to be the people
working on it. He had never once heard her question the premise. The vibrant
escapism of silence. Bitboy returned
from a meandering trip to the kitchen and, with snacks in hand, sat in wonder at
the crackling fire and the boiling, spitting sap. Cole cracked open a beer on
his way to the medicine cabinet. If selling second-hand Federation tech on the
black market had been a rich man's game, Bitboy's thirst and the price of
Naloxone-E kept Cole distant from a golden throne. While walking back to the
warmth of the living room, Cole pulled his phone from his pocket and searched
for "Heavy Hitter" in his contact list.

"I need you to walk me through it again. I have the shot, but what's the rest?"
Cole asked.

"It's a good thing I'm about there then. Isn't it?" Ricardo replied.

“Hell will freeze over. Sharon will marry me. Heck, the lights will stay on past
midnight — all before you drag your ass out here after seven years,” Cole said.

"I know. I know," Ricardo replied. "I'm under strict orders to keep this
conversation analog. I'll be there in about an hour."

"Orders? And here I thought you were coming to visit an old friend or, better
yet, to pick up your damn pet."

"I have a 30-rack of Natty Light," Ricardo said.

"That's a start," Cole replied. "But Bitboy and I don't drink anymore. We're
watching our figures."

"Come on, man," Ricardo insisted. "Just one beer."

"You're trouble, Hitter," Cole said. "Just one beer!"

With the Naloxone-E in hand, Cole turned his attention from the phone to Bitboy,
who was lost in the dance of the flames and snacks. The glow of the fire cast
shadows across Cole's aging face. He knew what came next was a delicate balance
between saving Bitboy and pushing him to the brink.

"all right, buddy," Cole said. "Just a short prick."

Bitboy, sensing the shift in Cole's demeanor, turned, his eyes narrowing in
confusion and then widening in understanding. An unwelcome whirr came from
Bitboy's arms, and the blades peeked out. There was a moment of understanding
even a junky monkey could grasp, and Bitboy fell willingly, yet tense, into
Cole's arms. With a gentle touch, Cole injected the Naloxone-E. The room held
its breath as the solution coursed through Bitboy's veins. His breathing
steadied to a rhythm reclaimed from chaos. Cole let out a breath he didn't
realize he was holding, and relief washed over him as Bitboy's eyes flickered
with clarity.

"I'm going to just toss you out in the snow next time," Cole said. "Do you
understand me, Monkey?"

A quiet settled between them, broken only by the occasional pop and hiss from
the fire. Bitboy, lucid but weakened, leaned into Cole, and Cole draped an arm
around Bitboy. As they sat in the warmth of the living room, Cole's gaze drifted
into the fire.

The crisp air filled Ricardo's government-issued SUV with a scent of pine and
dust. A nearby stream babbled its way through the valley. He handled the winding
country road carelessly like only a pilot could. The unmistakable sound of tires
on a dirt road was amplified for miles around the cabin. The sound of a single
car was a rarity. The rumbling of the two approaching the cabin was cause for
concern.

Gravel crunched under the tires as Ricardo pulled into the driveway. Before him
stood a single A-frame cabin. Ricardo chugged the last of a lukewarm energy
drink.

Cole cracked the front door and winced at the bright sunlight dazzling across
the numerous pines with their dusting of snow. His eyes scanned the environment
before settling his sight on his visitor.

"Hey, Nova, I hope you don't mind. I let myself past the gate," Ricardo said.

Ricardo was the embodiment of the world Cole left behind. A Federation man
through and through. With his height and heft, he filled his fatigues just
enough to accentuate massive arms and shoulders while leaving room in the
midsection to hide his growing gut. Next to his large body, he carried a heavy
black briefcase, and he looked like a 4th grader with a lunch pail.

"You didn't get past anything," Cole said. "I clocked you on the gate's camera
and buzzed you in."

"Always have to be the winner, eh, Nova?"

"Just works out that way, Hitter. My sister and girlfriend will be here soon.
Behave."

Ricardo joined Cole on the wraparound porch and clasped his large hands around
Cole's shoulders. The two men looked into each other for only a moment, and the
years between them melted away. Cole grunted and patted Ricardo away from their
friendly embrace.

"Bitboy is by the fireplace," Cole said. "Go on and take a look. I'm going to
wait for the girls."

Ricardo pulled a beer from the pouch pocket on his jacket and cracked it open
with a crispness that echoed off the granite walls around them. Then, from
another pocket, he retrieved another and tossed it to Cole before disappearing
into the cabin.

The sound of another vehicle crept closer and closer. Cole waved them over when
Sharon and Emily finally arrived in a puff of dust. Both women jumped from the
van to stretch the long drive from their slender bodies. Cole slowly opened his
beer with a hiss instead of a crack and quickly drank a giant's share. He
watched Sharon and sent an effortlessly booming whistle through the quiet valley
around them.

Sharon stepped out of the van and stretched and looked up at the porch and had,
for approximately two seconds, the particular sensation of a memory reassembling
itself from the parts she'd filed in the wrong drawer. A transit bay at Port
Authority Fourteen. A manifest dispute that was not his problem. A port official
who had been publicly wrong, and a man who could not help making that his
problem anyway — had walked forty feet out of his way, involved a stranger,
cited the exact weight classification code from memory, and then looked at her
with an expression that was not gratitude and not satisfaction but something
quieter and more specific. The look of a person who had been right about
something and wanted nothing from it. She had been thinking about that look, off
and on, for four years. She had not expected it to be on a porch in the Sierra
Nevadas holding a beer.

"Hold your damn horses," Sharon said. "Some of us give our bodies love from time
to time."

"Put on a few pounds," Ricardo's voice carried from inside the house, "and I'd
take care of that for you."

Emily turned red and wide-eyed, which brought out her innocent beauty. Cole, on
the other hand, closed the door.

"Girls, we have a visitor," Cole said. "I'd like you to meet Bitboy's deadbeat
dad. This disgusting pervert is Heavy Hitter."

Still outside, the three embraced until Emily squirmed out to allow Cole and
Sharon a moment of affection. Cole and Sharon absorbed each other. Cole's hands
wrapped around Sharon's lower back, and he lifted her into a longing kiss. With
her back turned, Emily did her best to pretend she wasn't intruding at that
moment.

"How'd she do?" Cole asked in a soft tone that sounded like a secret.

Emily rounded the corner and continued to pretend she wasn't there. Through a
lifetime of practice, she had become skilled at feeling invisible.

"Cole, honey," Sharon looked up into his eyes, "she was incredible. She'll rule
the world once she figures out how capable she is."

"I'm glad to hear it," Cole said. "But what happened to the guy if it went so
well?"

"The guy?" Emily said, popping her head around a nearby corner. "What, uh, what
guy?"

"Your contact, E," Cole said. "You obviously got the tech. But what happened to
Dr. Conrad?"

"Babe," Sharon said, "you must be more specific. Stephen was a bit of a mess. He
gave us this whole doom prediction and this memory stick. Other than that, it
mostly went down as expected."

Sharon handed Cole the van keys, and both women stood silent. Cole held the
memory drive, rubbing his thumb across the hull of the Enterprise. The
repetitive motion left a dark silver stain on his skin.

He didn't think about why he kept rubbing it. He'd been doing it for forty
seconds before he noticed, and another twenty before he stopped.

Seven years in the cabin, and the thing he'd never told anyone — not Ricardo,
not Emily, not Sharon — was that the part of the Polaris he missed most was not
his crew. He missed his crew in the full human way, the way that came with faces
and voices and specific remembered moments he couldn't close his eyes without
seeing. That grief had its own room.

This was a different room.

Gracie had been in the ship. She'd been in the ship's systems, and the ship was
gone, and the morning after he'd made it back, he'd opened the app out of habit
and gotten nothing. Not an error. Nothing. The way a phone number simply doesn't
exist anymore. He hadn't told anyone about that part. There was no language for
it. She wasn't a person. There was no paperwork for that kind of loss. You
couldn't put it on the memorial placard.

Ricardo, drunk on Natty Light two winters ago, had supplied a word for it.

Space divorce, Ricardo had said, with the solemnity of a man who believed he'd
said something hilarious. You're a space divorcée, Nova. Seven years in a cabin
in the woods. Classic post-space-divorce behavior. I've seen it a hundred times.

You've seen it zero times, Cole had said.

I've seen it in you one time, and that's a hundred percent of my data.

Cole had laughed. He'd laughed because Ricardo was right and because laughing
was easier than the thing underneath laughing, which was that Gracie had been
the one who noticed when he hadn't slept and the one who catalogued his silences
without comment and the one who knew, without asking, when he was running the
Polaris incident in his head again. And she had been gone since 2034. And in the
years since, without anyone filling that function —

He had looked up one afternoon and Sharon was sitting across from him at the
table.

She had driven three hours without telling him she was coming. She had brought
blackberry preserves from the farmer's market and a road atlas she claimed was
for a road trip she was planning, and she'd sat down and said absolutely nothing
about why she was there, and she'd been there for four days, and she'd noticed
every single thing she noticed without saying a word about it.

He had not said anything about it either.

It wasn't the same thing. Sharon was a person. Gracie had been something else.
But the function of it. The particular being-attended-to. Cole had spent
thirty-two years not knowing he needed it, four years learning to take it for
granted, and three years in a cabin learning what the absence felt like.

He rubbed his thumb across the hull of the Enterprise one more time and looked
at the stain on his skin.

Lock for every key, Conrad had apparently said.

Cole looked at the silver mark on his thumb and did not yet know what to do with
that.

"He was weird, okay," Emily said, "but nice too. He said to tell you there is a
lock for every key," raising her hands in confusion.

"So neither of you know where he went?"

The women looked at each other and shook their heads.

"Okay. Well, he's missing," Cole said. "Didn't even show up for his speech.
Thought maybe you had all run off together."

"How should we know where he went?" Emily said. "Is that something we should
know?"

Cole shook his head and opened the door. The smell of bacon and pancakes
caressed them inside.

"Who's hungry?" Ricardo said. "Looks like these degens have been partying all
night...for, like, years. And from the van and sweatpants, I'd bet you girls
could use a meal."

Bitboy lay in agony next to the dying fire. Ricardo's case sat open on the table
next to several accouterments. Sharon added a small jar between the butter and a
container of organic maple syrup. Cole set down his empty beer can and reached
for the case Ricardo had brought with him. He unpacked the stomach pump from
within it and saw Emily holding Bitboy with the care of a nurturing mother. Cole
wondered where she learned that as he tiptoed toward the two. He kneeled and
nodded to Emily. They transferred Bitboy to Cole's firm embrace, and Bitboy
nuzzled into Cole's chest.

Emily slid the tip of her finger under Bitboy's chin. She gently pressed up
until their eyes met, and Bitboy went limp with safety. Cole gently slid the
long tube down the macaque's throat as the poor animal twitched in discomfort
layered on pain. The device hummed to life, pulling putrid sludge up with every
beat. As it worked, Cole glanced at the empty can.

"Hey, Hitter," Cole said while leaning over Bitboy, "beer me."

"You have the grace of a peasant," Sharon said.

The machine emitted a steady hum, purging the toxins from Bitboy's system.
Emily, her face set with determination, carefully removed the tube from Bitboy's
mouth. She moved to the sink, the sound of running water mingling with the
crackle of the fire. She scrubbed her hands and glanced over her shoulder,
noting Ricardo's handiwork. The kitchen was a scene of organized chaos: plates
piled high with bacon and a mountain of pancakes, while six empty beer cans
stood sentinel on the counter.

"A girl could get used to this," she said, her smile widening as she caught
Ricardo's eye.

Ricardo leaned against the counter with a beer in hand and wearing a lopsided
grin. "Shit, sis, this place is stocked like a bomb shelter. So, you know, I
could get used to this too."

Across the room, Cole knelt beside Bitboy, his rough hands surprisingly gentle
as he caressed the small creature. Lulled by the fire's warmth and rhythmic
crackling, Bitboy's eyes fluttered shut. Cole watched as Bitboy's breathing
steadied and deepened. He then carefully laid Bitboy in a plush dog bed
positioned just within the fire's glow. The front door creaked behind, and
Sharon stepped in, boasting a devilish grin commanding attention. She moved with
deliberate grace, her steps quiet but assured. She placed a hand on Cole's
shoulder and drew his gaze away from Bitboy.

"I have a surprise for you," she said. "Come sit with me and close your eyes."

Cole raised an eyebrow, but he complied. Sharon took his hand, her touch light
but insistent, and led him towards the kitchen table. He settled into the chair,
fingers splayed over his eyes, peeking through them. Sharon gently guided his
hand away from his face and pressed it to her chest. He could feel her heart
flutter beneath his palm.

"Okay, open them," she whispered.

He blinked against the light and saw a dark purple jar nestled beside a stack of
steaming pancakes, promising something special inside. Cole's eyes softened, and
a smile spread across his face.

"You spoil me," he said, his voice a mix of affection and awe.

"Only because you deserve it," Sharon replied, kissing his forehead. The kiss
lingered.

As the mid-morning light filtered through the kitchen windows, Emily and Ricardo
shared a quiet laugh by the sink. The fire crackled, casting a warm glow over
the room, while the tall pines stood guard outside. They found a fleeting moment
of peace within these walls amidst the aroma of bacon and pancakes. In their own
ways, each of them felt at home.

"Are you real? I don't deserve you." Cole said, his hand still resting on her
heart.

Sharon smiled, squeezing his hand tighter against her chest. "I got this at the
farmer's market. The last blackberry preserves they had!"

"Mmm! Quick, open it for me," Ricardo interjected, eager as ever.

Cole opened the jar with a satisfying pop. Sharon dipped her finger into the
bittersweet jam and then into Cole's mouth. He melted into his chair, savoring
the taste, his lips lingering on her finger as she slowly pulled it away.

"Jackpot," Ricardo said, a spreading mischievous grin. "I haven't seen you this
happy since our first time behind the stick. Let me try that moon goo. Maybe
later, we can zip around in whatever plane you have hidden here."

"I'm not flying," Cole said, standing abruptly. "Not later. Not ever."

Ricardo's smile faded. "Then you're not going to like why I'm here," he said,
his tone becoming serious. "Although I don't entirely know myself."

Sharon fixed her most penetrative glare on Ricardo. "Did you know she's involved
with those crackpot Collectivists?" Sharon asked.

Ricardo's face fell. "Yes, I knew."

"And you didn't think to tell me?" Cole asked, his voice rising.

"It's not my place, Cole. She's an adult and makes her own decisions," Ricardo
replied calmly.

Emily, who had been quietly listening, suddenly felt the weight of the
conversation bearing down on her. She set her fork down on the table, her eyes
watering. She stood up without a word and walked to the kitchen sink, her hands
moving automatically as she touched the empty plates, scratching the crumbs off
pans.

She was unconsciously distracting herself from the hot tears burning her face,
the fear of the energy crisis, Cole's unrelenting sense of duty, and a room full
of people who should mind their own business. Emily grasped the sides of her
head, let out a silent scream, and returned to the living room—her face red but
otherwise composed. She sat on the couch, facing away from her brother.

Cole said nothing and continued to pet Bitboy, who had joined him on the sofa.
His mind is fixated on the letter, and his thumb drives on the table. Until this
moment, his thoughts had been on the monkey, pancakes, and the metric volume of
fuel needed to reach Mars—anything other than what these items held for him.

"And burn my apartment down when it self-destructs? No thanks," Ricardo said,
shaking his head. "I don't know what's in there any more than I'd know how many
lives I've saved via lovemaking."

In the following pause, Cole scanned their faces, searching for answers and
finding only blank stares. Resentment began to simmer in his mind. Emily's gaze
wandered around the log cabin, taking in the sight of dirty laundry mounds and
empty cans that littered the floor. Sharon and Ricardo looked back at Cole,
their expressions unreadable.

"They can say or do whatever they want. It's been seven years," Cole said, his
frustration boiling over. "Seven years! Do they think they can put a leash on me
and run me around like a dog?"

Emily glanced at him, her voice softening. "I don't know if I should feel like
I'm in danger or satisfied."

Cole's heart raced. "How do you mean?"

"That's the first real emotion I've seen out of you in months," Emily said with
a smile playing on her lips.

"Tell us you're ready," Sharon demanded, eyes locking onto Cole's.

Bitboy turned to look at Cole. The metal implements in his arms twined
unnaturally around the muscles. Cole's mind reeled, wondering what secrets the
envelope held. At the dining table, Ricardo tried to get Bitboy's attention.
Still, the monkey ignored him, perhaps because of Ricardo's earlier abandonment
or simply because Bitboy had forgotten him. Instead, Bitboy's eyes remained
locked on Cole, his mechanical body whirring and tensing in sync with his
master's agitation.

Ricardo stood, sighing as he walked from the dining table to the couch, the
envelope held out in front of him. He extended the envelope toward Cole, who
snatched it from Ricardo's fingers. Cole glanced at Emily and Sharon, then
shrugged and extracted crisp white papers from within. Two small captain
insignia tumbled out and landed on his lap as he did. His breath caught, and for
a moment, he stared at them, the gleaming symbols of authority and recognition.
His fingers hovered over the insignia, eyes widening with longing and disbelief.
He picked up the insignia, feeling their cold metal against his skin. He had
imagined this moment countless times, but this felt like a cruel bribe. His mind
flashed back to the lives lost under his command. The insignia dug into his
skin. He tossed them on the table, feigning disinterest, and moved on.

The Federation's emblem in the upper left-hand corner of the letter sent a
shudder down his spine. Sharon rested her head on her hand, arm draped over the
back of the sofa. Her eyes were fixed intently on Cole as his gaze darted across
the page, index finger trembling as it followed each line. He began to read
aloud, his voice unsteady, the weight of the words sinking in with every
syllable:

From the Office of Federation Chancellor Geoffrey H. DeMint

Dear Mr. Nova,

The Federation now stands at a critical juncture, and your unparalleled
expertise is urgently required. After extensive deliberation, the Council has
concluded that your unique qualifications are essential to our mission. Enclosed
is a copy of a legally binding document with your signature dated September 12,
2021, which waives your rights as a United States citizen in exchange for your
service in the Stellar Council Forces. Your duty to the Council remains in
effect and paramount.

We regret to inform you of the escalating energy shortage crisis, which
threatens the very fabric of our society. The severity of this condition is
unprecedented, and we seek your immediate involvement with utmost urgency. Your
skills and experience make you uniquely positioned to address this dire
situation.

You are hereby ordered to prepare for immediate deployment. Report to Federal
Space Command Headquarters. Be advised that your movements are under constant
surveillance. The Federation relies on your swift and unquestioning compliance.
The stakes have never been higher, and the survival of humanity rests on your
shoulders.

This is your moment, Mr. Nova. The world is watching. We need the old Cole
Nova—the leader, the hero, the man who can turn the tide. Your legacy awaits.

Sincerely,

Geoffrey H. DeMint, Federation Chancellor

His face contorted angrily; Cole crumpled the letter and hurled it into a steel
garbage pail. "Bastards! I need more time," he growled, his voice echoing with
frustration. The sudden clatter of dishes from the dining area caught his
attention. "Where's Bitboy?"

The macaque perched on the table with a stack of pancakes the size of a tall top
hat, clutched in his tiny hands. He grinned sheepishly, crumbs littering his
fur. Cole and Emily shared a laugh bubbling up from their mutual despair. Sharon
and Ricardo joined in, their laughter tinged with nervousness. Sharon's mirth
quickly turned to rage as she noticed a blue trail of stains leading to Bitboy's
many fingers and the now-empty jar of blackberry preserves. The juice had turned
his steel claws a dark blue, with a hint of the color marking his lips and
tongue.

"You dirty ape! Is nothing sacred to you?" she cried, her voice rising in
frustration.

Cole turned away from the scene, his smile fading. He strode purposefully to a
cluttered desk and jammed the thumb drive into the side of an old laptop. The
screen flickered to life. Files opened frenetically across the display in a
chaotic frenzy of data. He leaned in, scanning the information with growing
unease.

Ricardo walked over and peered over Cole's shoulder. The data scrolling on the
monitor included information on Cole that he had long buried in the recesses of
his memory: missions, failures, and personal details he wished to forget. Dirt
on Ricardo that could bury him twice over, both scandalous and damning. Notes on
Emily that reduced her fears to a mere footnote in a much larger, darker
narrative of corruption and cover-ups.

But one encrypted file, tagged "Dark Matter Research," lured Cole in like a dark
siren call. Sharon, her curiosity piqued, leaned closer, her breath steady
against the back of his neck, eyes locked on the screen. The weight of what they
might uncover hung heavily in the air.

"Bitboy, play," Cole commanded, his voice a mixture of resolve and dread. The
monkey, driven by complex programming, scampered to the laptop. His mechanical
fingers moved with precise efficiency, tapping away at the keyboard. Bitboy's
eyes glowed with a soft light as he interfaced with the encryption, his body
humming with a low mechanical whirr.

As Bitboy worked, Cole's mind raced. What could be so important that it was
hidden behind layers of encryption? The seconds felt like hours as the monkey's
limbs moved in a blur of activity. Unable to stay still, Ricardo paced the room,
his footsteps adding to the tension. Emily stepped closer, her eyes wide with
concern.

Finally, the screen blinked, and the files spilled their secrets, line after
line of data unfurling before them. Bitboy, his task complete, returned to his
feast of pancakes, the glow in his eyes dimming as he disconnected. Cole's eyes
darted over the data, absorbing its implications. Although much of it was
unintelligible without careful analysis, the narrative suggested the
unthinkable: Cole's reckless dance with disaster  wasn't just a personal
folly—it might have birthed the energy crisis. The realization hit him like a
gut punch, a cosmic joke with Cole as the punchline. He felt Sharon's hand
tighten on his shoulder, her grip a silent affirmation of their shared burden.

Cole scrolled through the documents, his face grimmer with each line he read.
Detailed logs chronicled Alpha's secretive experiments, run through the
Federation as a shield. Early trials, initially promising, had shown the
potential to harness Dark Matter as a nearly infinite energy source. But the
risks had been monumental. One log highlighted a pivotal experiment conducted by
Dr. Stephen Conrad. The document described how the experiment had gone awry,
destabilizing the global energy grid and triggering catastrophic energy surges.

Emily leaned over Cole's other shoulder, her eyes scanning the text. "That's why
the world's in chaos," she murmured. "They caused it."

Cole nodded, his jaw set. The logs detailed how the energy crisis was no mere
accident but a result of Alpha's relentless pursuit of power—their
incestuous relationship with the Federation used as cover, the blind ambition of
one feeding the blind compliance of the other. Dr. Conrad's disappearance now
made sense—it was a cover-up, an attempt to bury the truth of the failed
experiment that had plunged the world into darkness.

As they delved deeper, surveillance reports began to surface. Cole's name
appeared frequently. Alpha had been watching him through their Federation
assets, aware of his expertise and the potential threat he posed. The reports
suggested they had planned to leverage his skills, pulling Federation strings to
bring him back into their orbit to mitigate the disaster they had created.

Sharon's grip on Cole's shoulder tightened. "They've been playing us all along,"
she whispered, her voice thick with anger.

Cole looked at his team and his family and felt a surge of hope. "We can do
this," he said firmly. "Together, we can stop them."

The firelight cast dancing shadows on the walls, illuminating their determined
faces. Cole turned to Bitboy, who had returned to his makeshift bed by the fire,
his mechanical limbs finally at rest. Emily's face, now composed, joined them,
her eyes reflecting a newfound resolve. Standing by the kitchen counter, Ricardo
finished his beer and crushed the can with a loud pop, breaking the silence.

"So, what's the plan, Nova?" Ricardo asked, his voice steady but tinged with the
gravity of the situation.

"We need to understand everything in these files," Cole said. "We have to know
what we're dealing with. Then, we can figure out how to stop them."

Emily spoke up, her voice strong and clear. "I've been involved with the
Collective. I know people who can help."

Ricardo looked at her with a mix of surprise and respect. "You really got
involved with those Collectivists, huh?"

Emily nodded. "They're not all crackpots. Some of them are actually trying to
make a difference."

Cole felt a surge of pride mixed with worry. "We'll need all the help we can
get."

Sharon stood up, her face set with determination. "Let's get to work. We've got
a lot to do and not much time to do it."

As they gathered around the laptop, the fire crackling behind them, the cabin
seemed to pulse with a new energy. The quiet refuge had transformed into a war
room, and they were ready to face whatever came next. Alpha might have
thought they could control Cole Nova. Still, they had underestimated the
strength and resolve of those he held dear.

Cole glanced at them, seeing the same fire in their eyes that he felt in his
own. "Together," he said, his voice firm. "We do this together."

Bitboy, his mechanical fingers poised over the keyboard, began working on the
decryption. The room fell silent, and each group member focused on their task.
As the files started to reveal their secrets, the scope of Alpha's ambition
became clear.

"Look at this," Emily said, pointing to a series of documents. "They've been
experimenting with Dark Matter for years, using it to create energy-efficient
implants and other technologies. But the side effects...they're catastrophic."

Ricardo added, "They knew the risks and proceeded anyway. The energy crisis was
a direct result of their experiments."

Sharon's face paled as she read through the data. "If we don't stop them, the
consequences will be unimaginable."

Cole looked at his team and his family and felt a surge of hope. "We can do
this," he said firmly. "Together, we can stop them."

They set their plan into motion, ready to face whatever challenges lay ahead.
Alpha might have thought they could control Cole Nova. Still, they had
underestimated the strength and resolve of those he held dear. The fight for the
future of humanity had just begun.

MERIDIAN II — FIVE DAYS AFTER COLE ACCEPTS
Slot: after Chapter Four

Dayo read the relay intercept three times.

It wasn't the content — the content was vague in the way that
long-distance intelligence usually was, the shape of a thing but
not its texture. Emily Nova had gone to a cabin in Sequoia with
the Enterprise figurine. The cabin was confirmed as Cole Nova's
address for the last five years.

"She got him," said Loa from the engineering station, not looking
up.

"She was always going to get him," Dayo said.

"You don't know that."

"I know she's the only person in his network with a clear enough
picture of what's at stake and enough of him to make it matter.
Of course she got him."

Petyr had found his droplet by now. It was on the inside of his
left wrist — a small fabric oval, a water droplet in gunmetal
thread on navy. He kept turning his wrist over to look at it.

The nav station ran three degrees colder than the rest of the
ship — a quirk of the cooling architecture that Loa had
catalogued and declined to fix. Petyr kept rubbing his left
wrist without noticing. Not the droplet, exactly. The cold.

"Can I ask something," Petyr said.

"You're going to either way," Loa said.

"What's a droplet mean. Like, actually. I read the brief but the
brief was—"

"Spare," Kate said.

"It said I had demonstrated awareness and willingness. Which is a
pretty low bar for a clandestine network."

Dayo set down his coffee. "What would a higher bar look like to
you."

Petyr thought about it. "Demonstrated capability. Demonstrated
commitment. Something you do rather than just — something—"

"A droplet means you're wet," Dayo said. "You've gotten close
enough to something true that you can't entirely go back to how
you saw things before. You've seen the lock on the door. You know
there's a room. You don't earn a droplet. You receive one because
someone assessed that you were already carrying it without knowing
what it was."

Petyr looked at his wrist. "Oh."

"Between a droplet and a compass, there are others. A scalpel
means you operate — you cut what needs cutting, you move through
resistance with precision. A scalpel doesn't wait for the right
conditions. A wrench means you build and connect — the people who
wire the network together so everyone else can use it. A
sunglasses means you see what you're not supposed to see, and
you carry that without breaking cover." He paused. "A compass
means you navigate. You move when the network moves. You give
direction as well as receive it."

"What's above a compass?"

Kate and Loa exchanged a look.

"We don't put them in order," Kate said.

"Why not?"

"Because the network doesn't run vertically." She turned back to
her panel. "It runs in all directions at once. That's the whole
point."

Petyr looked at his wrist again. He seemed to find this both
clarifying and unsatisfying, which Dayo privately thought was
the correct response to most true things.

"Cole Nova launches in approximately seventy-two hours," Dayo
said. "Kate. Continuous passive intercept on the Golden Dawn's
outbound relay bands. Everything."

"Already running," she said. "Have been for a week."

He had known she would be. A sunglasses didn't need to be told
what to watch for.
