﻿RINGS OF DUST

Chapter Five

It had started as laziness.

The protocol was clear: submit for containment within twenty-four hours of
crystallization. That window had closed ten days ago.

Max had converted the batch on a Tuesday — standard process, salt compounds and
liquid nitrogen in a negative-pressure chamber until the DarkX plasma locked
into solid form. Beautiful crystals. Dense, dark, faintly luminous at the edges.
The kind of thing that took three hours to make and thirty seconds to ruin. The
kind of thing a junior researcher had no business holding onto without
authorization.

The official containment unit was across campus. Getting there meant logging the
transfer, signing through two security checkpoints, and explaining why a junior
researcher had an unauthorized batch of Darker Matter crystals at all. Max
looked at the crystals. He looked at the clock. He looked at the spare fuel cell
sitting on the shelf behind him.

He packed the crystals inside it, sealed the cell under negative pressure,
topped it off with nitrogen to keep things stable, and shoved it in his locker.
*I'll deal with it tomorrow.*

Tomorrow became the next day. The next day became a week. A week became ten
days, the mandatory turnover window long gone, the crystals now approaching
reversion on their own schedule whether he was ready or not. Each morning he
stood at the locker for a full minute before he could make himself open it.

Max slumped over his workstation, eyes strained from hours of staring at data
screens. The fluorescent lights above cast a sterile, cold glare on the polished
floor. His mind was a storm of thoughts, still reeling from what he'd seen. The
fuel cell—his impulsive shortcut—hadn't just held; it had transformed.

Sitting hadn't worked. Relaxation is a distant bitch. A double-edged sword. His
feet wanted to run in any direction other than the one he forced his mutinous
legs to continue. He rounded the corner, his pulse quickening. The locker loomed
ahead, a secret buried beneath anxiety and cautious hope. His fingers fumbled
with the lock, the combination momentarily escaping him as nerves tangled his
thoughts. When the door finally swung open, his breath caught in his throat.

The fuel cell was glowing. A soft, eerie light pulsed from the panel, a
heartbeat in the silence. He lifted it carefully, hands trembling as he peered
through the viewport. The vapor inside was dense, swirling lazily like a storm
caught in a jar.

"No way," he muttered, almost dropping the cell in shock. The crystals he'd
sealed there were gone, replaced by something entirely different. His mind
raced, a dozen explanations swirling like the mist in the cell, none making
sense. He should have reported it immediately, but the fear of repercussions—of
losing everything—held him back.

Instead, he tucked the cell back into the locker, slammed the door, and forced
himself to breathe. He needed time, needed to think. The lab was a maze of risks
and rewards, and Max knew better than most how quickly those scales could tip.

The following weeks were a blur of late nights and whispered conversations, the
fuel cell hidden away like contraband. Max monitored it obsessively, his heart
pounding every time the lab door creaked open. Each day, the cell's charge grew,
the vapor inside thickening, alive with a strange, magnetic energy. He was on
the edge of something huge that could change everything.

But when the 11th day came, and the mist cleared, revealing a cluster of
delicate crystals clinging to the outside of the cell's base, one word escaped
him before he could stop it.

"Jackpot."

He said it the way a man says something he didn't know he was going to
say—quietly, to no one, to the empty lab, to the crystals themselves. Then the
dread moved in behind the word and filled all the space the triumph had just
vacated. He knew what this meant. This wasn't just a fluke; it was a
breakthrough—a dangerous one. He scribbled notes furiously, documenting every
detail, his thoughts a chaotic jumble of triumph and fear.

Then came the decision he couldn't put off any longer. He had to tell someone,
had to share what he'd found. He chose his department head, hoping that his
discovery's potential would outweigh the protocol breach.

It didn't.

Security was on him within minutes, their hands rough as they stripped him of
his badge and lab coat. Max barely registered the words—the accusations, the
threats. He was too stunned, his mind still fixated on the image of those
crystals, their potential.

"You'll keep quiet about this, or we'll make sure you never work again.
Anywhere," one of the guards snapped, his voice a low growl. Max blinked, the
weight of the threat slowly sinking in. They weren't just talking about his
career. This was bigger, more dangerous than he'd realized.

Outside, the night air was sharp against his skin, the chill cutting through the
shock. He stood in the parking lot, staring at the looming silhouette of the
research complex. It felt like he'd been expelled from some exclusive club, and
the doors slammed shut behind him.

At that moment, he knew that Alpha would bury his discovery and lock it
away behind layers of corporate secrecy. But he couldn't forget what he'd seen.
Couldn't let it go.

Back inside, Alpha's upper echelons were already dissecting his research.
The data was clear: the combination of nitrogen, negative pressure, and the fuel
cell circuits had triggered a reaction that turned Dark Matter crystals back
into its vapor state, generating massive energy. They added liquid nitrogen,
accelerating the formation of the crystals and cutting the cycle down to 11
days.

The process was erratic. Some cells bloomed with crystallites, others failed
spectacularly. Researchers started betting on which cells would produce, a
twisted game of chance in a world where control was everything. The "gardens,"
as they called the fuel cells, became a grim parody of hope—a hope that could
only thrive in secret, behind locked doors.

Every 11 days, they harvested what they could, the crystals growing more
reliable but still volatile. The cells themselves deteriorated under the
constant strain, leaking energy and sometimes exploding. Any remnants were
transferred to fresh cells, and the losses""tailings," they called them—were
painfully significant.

They knew what they had, though. The data was buried deep, accessible only to a
select few. Stock prices soared on speculation and rumor, the true nature of
their discovery hidden beneath layers of corporate bureaucracy.

"  "- "

Meanwhile, at Federation Headquarters, the crisis deepened. Chancellor DeMint
stared at the holographic display, the red zones creeping across the globe like
a spreading disease. The energy crisis was a noose tightening around their
necks, and the room buzzed with the grim knowledge that time was running out.

"We're looking at six months, maybe less," Dr. Salazar said, her voice cutting
through the tension. "Without the new fuel cells, we're facing catastrophic
blackouts."

DeMint's jaw clenched. "What about production? Can we ramp it up?"

"Not without more DarkX," Taylor interjected, his tone clipped. "The
stabilization period is still 90 days, and our reserves are almost gone. We're
scraping the bottom of the barrel."

Sam, a data technician who had been silent until now, leaned forward. "We've
been experimenting with the accumulations on the cells. We can extend the
stability for a few more days, but it's not enough. We need their data."

Taylor turned toward her for the first time. "Samantha." He said it the way
you say a word that isn't a greeting — flat, first name, in a room where
everyone used rank or last name. A small and specific violation. Her jaw
tightened once and then didn't again. She held his eyes and said nothing,
which was its own answer. He turned back to the room. "Extending accumulation
cycles based on experimental intuition isn't a strategy. It's a feeling. And
feelings don't hold up at a briefing table." A half-beat pause. "Or in the
field."

Sam's eyes stayed on her screen. "Might want to patch that," she said. "Your
firmware's running a few versions behind." She pulled up a new readout and
turned it toward Dr. Salazar without looking at Taylor once. "The numbers are
right here."

Dr. Salazar's expression didn't change, her gaze steady and unyielding. "Alpha
Corp has already shared everything they can. The proprietary information is
classified."

Sam's hands curled into fists. "We're all fighting the same battle. The least
they could do is give us a chance."

Taylor's voice was low, almost apologetic. "It's not that simple."

"Isn't it?" Sam snapped. "They hold all the cards, and we're just supposed to
wait until they decide to deal?"

The room fell silent, the weight of the unspoken truth settling over them. Alpha
Corp controlled the future, and without them, the Federation ran out of options.

--- --- ---

Back in his apartment, Max sat at his kitchen table, staring at the stolen fuel
cell. The crystals inside glowed faintly, a promise of power just out of reach.
He knew what he had to do. He couldn't let this go or let Alpha bury his
work.

He would go underground and find a way to continue his research outside their
control. He knew the risks, knew he was playing with fire, but he also knew that
if he could crack this, he could change everything.

Max took a deep breath, the decision settling like a stone in his gut. There was
no turning back now. He had to see this through and find a way to get this
information into the right hands.

════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

﻿RINGS OF DUST

Chapter Six

The sun dipped behind the towering Sierras, casting the rugged landscape in a
cascade of oranges and reds. Evening's approach brought a cool, gentle respite,
allowing the land to exhale in whispers that rustled through the pine needles
and brushed against the cabin's wooden and glass facade. The golden light
filtered through the windows, dancing across the piles of luggage compiled
throughout the day.

Cole, Ricardo, and Bitboy moved with muted urgency, their actions tempered by
the gravity of the day's unfolding revelations. The evening light enveloped them
in a scene reminiscent of a painting, where every brushstroke was part of a
memento mori.

Bitboy climbed into the back seat out of habit. Cole and Ricardo swung the
luggage into the SUV's open hatch. They stood across from one another, Cole on
the passenger side and Ricardo on the driver's.

"You know, you and Sharon make a great couple," Ricardo remarked, his voice
tinged with sincerity and envy. "You two remind me of the people in picture
frames—you know, the ones that come in the package—the ones you throw out and
replace with people you care about."

"We'll take that as a compliment. I guess?" Cole replied, a small smile playing
on his lips.

Sharon joined Cole at his side, pulled his face towards hers with both hands,
and delivered a perfectly timed, fully committed kiss.

"Those picture frame models don't come close to our hotness," she said. "Isn't
that right?"

"You know it!" Cole said, feeling a surge of affection and gratitude.

Sharon seemed composed, yet Cole sensed the tension beneath her calm exterior.
The connection between them, tangible and electric, surged through him,
grounding him in the moment. Cole found momentary peace in the sanctuary of
Sharon's presence, with his arm wrapped securely around her. The warmth of her
body seeped into him as she leaned in close. Bitboy mimicked their affection in
his own quirky way, rubbing his palms together and making kissing sounds from
the back seat. Meanwhile, Ricardo seemed caught off guard by the display, his
frustration manifesting in a noticeable flush across his forehead.

"Been training at all? What's your cholesterol like?" Ricardo said while he
cracked open a tall energy drink with a single hand. "Can you even fit into a
flight suit?"

"Let's get that head rush," Cole said in his best Astro jargon. "If the payload
doesn't make it home, ain't nobody gettin' paid! We'll be home in bed while the
thrusters are still hot enough to fry an egg."

Sharon leaned back with wide eyes, shaking her head. "You boys have fun with
your 'Astros only' cult."

Cole spotted a hummingbird feeder hanging near the porch and quickly strode
over, ripping a length of copper wire from it. He picked up a small pebble from
the road, its surface smooth and weathered. He twisted the wire around his pinky
with nimble fingers, securing the pebble at the top and fashioning a makeshift
ring. He returned to Sharon, who watched him with curiosity.

"For the first time, I want to give you something to hold onto," Cole said,
placing the makeshift ring in her hand. "I'll bring back something extraordinary
to replace this. Keep it safe for me?"

Sharon's eyes softened, and she closed her fingers around the ring. "I will,"
she whispered. "You'd better come back to me, Cole Nova."

Emily patiently waited on the porch swing for her turn to speak to Cole. She
watched him hold Sharon, and Sharon gripped him in return. They held one another
with simple determination.

"Take E to deliver the crystals; they're only stable for six more days," Cole
said. "It's the same spot as always. Leave the van and take the train back."

Cole looked up from the nest it had made in Sharon's hair along her neckline and
saw Emily gently swinging, wrapped in one of their mother's god-awful knitted
blankets.

"Guiding light in my life," Emily said, a tear streaming down her cheek.
"Distance and time mean nothing against the bond we share. Look to the ground as
I will always be there waiting for you, guiding you. Keep our dreams alive, and
remember, you're always in my heart. I love you, big brother."

"Okay, nerd. I will if you will," Cole said, taking his sister in his arms and
softly shushing her quiet sobs. "Leave the hero stuff to me. Take care of
yourself, okay? I'll see you when I get back. I promise."

Ricardo got in the driver's seat. As he pulled away, a dust cloud flew around
the rental car. Cole watched Emily and Sharon in the rearview mirror until they
were too small to recognize.

"It's already cold. Doesn't the heater work?" Cole complained.

"Fine, fine. I'll turn up the heater just for you, princess," Ricardo said.
"They've had me shuttling supplies to the asteroid miners every other year.
Short jumps to orbit stations through meteor storms and everything."

Cole watched the trees pass—the silhouette of peaks and valleys formed against
the night sky and sleepy mountains.

"Man, you had to be there this one time. Asteroids hit my wings like buckshot.
It was rad. The tail almost fell off about a thousand feet before I hit the
runway, but my bird held together. Hah! Man, you should've seen it."

Cole shut his eyes tightly and tried to breathe through the rising, ominous
visions before they could fully overtake him. With a slow inhale and a long
exhale, he brought his awareness back to the present moment; his mood now
soured.

"Can we not talk about accidents?"

"I should have retired years ago. How was retirement?"

"Was?"

"Hate to break it to you, silly goose," Ricardo said, patting Cole on the
shoulder. "But retirement was over the moment you stepped into this SUV."

"Listen, man, I don't get a lot of human interaction, and that's just how I like
it. It's just been the girls and Bitboy for the last half-decade." The chimp
grunted at his name and popped his head between the front seats.

"You forget we're brothers, Nova." Ricardo's smile faded briefly, then returned
as he turned to look at Cole. "As your brother, it's my duty to annoy the
ever-living shit out of you until you lighten the fuck up."

"Keep your damn eyes on the road, bro," Cole said.

Ricardo hissed dryly like a steam engine running out of water. The dry cough
conveniently became a tool for breaking the awkward moment before it could
stretch into oblivion. Or maybe he just realized how thirsty he was. He reached
for his energy drink, which was no longer in the center console. He looked at
the empty cup holder.

For the mere span of two heartbeats, his eyes left the road, hands released from
the steering wheel, and the vehicle chose to do its own thing. Ricardo calmly
but nonchalantly regained control of the wheel and eventually returned to the
original lane it had drifted from.

"Are you testing me?" Cole asked.

"Just having some fun, mountain man," Ricardo said. "You do remember what that
is, right?"

"No," Cole answered without a hint of amusement. "What were you looking for?
I'll get it so you can focus."

Ricardo let that land, then let it go. He drove for a moment in silence, then
spoke to the windshield like he was telling the story to himself.

"You remember Kowalski?"

Cole said nothing.

"Titan Station. The customs inspector with the lazy eye — the one who'd hold
your paperwork up just to watch you sweat." Ricardo shook his head slowly,
already fighting the grin. "We're looking at a thirty-hour hold, mandatory cargo
inspection, the whole thing. I'm doing the math on how bad this is gonna be. And
Kowalski — without a word, without a single word — reaches into his chest pocket
and sets a hard-boiled egg on the man's desk. Just sets it there. Folds his
hands. Looks at him."

Cole stared out the window.

"Inspector looked at the egg. Looked at Kowalski. Stamped our clearance and told
us to have a good day." Ricardo exhaled through his nose. "We asked him about it
on the ride back. You know what he said? He said, 'An egg on a desk confuses any
bureaucrat long enough to reset the situation.' Said it worked every time. Four
stations, never failed."

Something shifted almost imperceptibly at the corner of Cole's mouth. Not a
smile. Just the ghost of one, passing through.

"Dumbest thing I ever saw," Ricardo said quietly. "Worked perfect."

"My energy drink, or whatever's left of it."

Cole glanced to the back seat to catch Bitboy tilting the can upside down above
his gaping maw, which had protruding razor-sharp fangs.

"We have maybe five minutes," Cole buried his face in his hands. "You have to
keep anything he can use for energy on lock, man; you know this: sugar,
caffeine, ethanol."

"Hey, I think we're being followed."

A black SUV similar to their own quickly approached from behind them. It sped up
suddenly, pulling alongside them, then roared ahead and swung in front of them.

Ricardo stared back at Bitboy through the rearview mirror. For no particular
logical reason, Bitboy utilized each of his four arms and two legs to shoot
obscene gestures at Ricardo with a toothy smile. Redirecting the high beams
shining from the car approaching behind them, Ricardo blasted Bitboy with a
concentrated shot of light by cleverly repositioning the mirror.

"What's got you spooked?" Cole asked. "We're going to see another car from time
to time. Bitboy is cracked out; you'll rile him up if you don't chill."

"If they were just looking to pass, why slow down after?" Ricardo said. "Bitboy,
buckle up. Cole, do whatever you'd like, but there's an automatic 9mm under your
seat if shit hits the fan."

The LED brake lights ahead of them shone an intense red as the vehicle dropped
in speed. Cole gripped the overhead handle and braced himself. Ricardo hit the
brakes hard, redirected, then hit the gas. He swerved left around the other
vehicle in a shower of gravel and dirt.

Still looking entirely too calm, Ricardo jested, "Somebody doesn't like you,
Cole. And please handle your monkey."

The SUV was in the rearview again. Bitboy did not buckle up. Instead, he began
to deploy and retract the sleek blades built into his forearms. With each
deployment, Bitboy released a lion's bark. As the car behind them again grew
nearer, it filled the cabin with light, creating a shadow puppet show of the
monkey as he jumped around in the back seat. He shredded the cushions and raked
the ceiling open with his claws.

"All right, Jackpot. Call the cops," Ricardo ordered.

Cole hastily located his phone and dialed.

"9-1-1, please state your name and the location of your emergency."

"This is Cole Nova. I'm located at mile marker, uh, thirty-three on Forest
Service Road 22. An aggressive driver is tailing me. They're trying to run us
off the road."

There was a brief pause before the operator responded. "I'm sorry, sir. We don't
have any units in that area. Can you get away from them?" Her voice was calm,
too calm, with an unsettling smoothness.

"We're trying," Cole said, his voice strained, eyes darting between the phone
and the road. "But they keep ramming us."

"Who else is with you?" the operator asked, her tone oddly clinical.

"Ricardo Corazon and a monkey," Cole replied, the absurdity of the situation
hitting him even in the crisis. There was another pause on the line, longer this
time, enough to make Cole's anxiety spike.

"Is this a prank call?" the operator demanded, her voice now edged with a
strange irritation. "Are there any females in the vehicle with you?"

"This isn't a prank, ma'am!" Cole insisted, frustration seeping into his tone.
"No, just us."

"We have no units in that area. Your report has been logged. Sorry, sir. Good
luck." The line went dead abruptly, leaving Cole staring at his phone in
disbelief.

Bitboy's shrieks filled the cabin as he dismantled the back seat, his fury
palpable. He clambered onto the duffle bag in the trunk, eyes gleaming with a
wild light. The Escalade behind them rammed their bumper again, sending them
skidding onto the rocky shoulder.

Ricardo's voice was terse. "The gun?"

Cole shook his head, his voice barely a whisper. "I've done enough killing.
Bitboy, attention!"

Ricardo sucked his teeth in frustration. Bitboy, illuminated by the dashboard
lights, turned his murderous gaze toward Cole. Ricardo maneuvered the SUV back
onto the road, accelerating to match their aggressors' speed.

"Bitboy," Cole said with steely resolve, "bad men. Initiate option Sierra-4."

Bitboy turned to face the attacking vehicle, his muscles coiling with tension.
With a savage roar, he launched himself through the shattered rear window, glass
shards glittering in the night. He landed on the hood of the pursuing SUV with a
metallic thud, his blades slicing into the windshield. The vehicle swerved
violently as Bitboy disappeared inside.

Gunfire erupted from within the SUV, each shot a violent burst of chaos. Ricardo
pulled their car to a stop about 40 yards away, grabbing an assault rifle from
under the rear seat. Cole stumbled out, running towards the wreckage, heart
pounding so loud it drowned out the noise. More shots rang out, each one making
him flinch. The shrieking of the chimpanzee drowned out the terrified screams of
the men inside.

Then, silence.

Cole reached the SUV, breath ragged and heart racing. A bloodied, unconscious
man in a sports coat and tee shirt occupied the driver's seat. On the passenger
side floor lay an empty hamburger bag and a pistol. Bitboy squatted in the
passenger seat, sucking on the straw of the man's soda with a satisfied grin.
The rear cabin was a gruesome scene of dismembered body parts, blood splattered
everywhere. Cole couldn't discern how many attackers there were, but their
private attire and weaponry were unmistakable.

"Bitboy. Attention! Threat neutralized."

Cole pulled the driver's wallet from the inside pocket of his sports coat, hands
shaking as he took a photo of his license. He grabbed the gun and then scooped
sand from the roadside. He emptied the remaining bullets from the magazine, then
cocked the gun, pouring sand into the slide until it jammed. He tossed it into
the bushes.

"Let's go. Let's go!"

Cole and Bitboy rushed back to the car, jumping in beside Ricardo. They sped off
into the night, the tension still thick.

"I got a photo of one of their IDs," Cole said, breathing heavily. "We'll file a
report when we get to The Federation."

"Holy shit Cole, what have you been training this monkey to do up there in the
mountains?" Ricardo said, his voice a mix of awe and concern.

Cole leaned his seat back, crawling over to inspect Bitboy for wounds. He was
covered in blood and gore, but none of it was his.

The city skyline grew taller as they approached, lights twinkling against the
dark sky.

"We just left a crime scene. And I gave our names to the police," Cole said,
anxiety creeping into his voice. "I'm pretty sure we just committed a few dozen
felonies."

"I'd say we just neutralized a threat," Ricardo replied. "Who have you been
pissing off, Nova?"

"Lots of people have reason to hate me," Cole admitted, with a hint of
bitterness in his tone.

"What's the name on the ID?" Ricardo asked.

Cole pulled up the photo. The license image was clear: the face of the man in
the driver's seat, unconscious. The name beneath it read *Davis, Brandon T.*
Pasadena, California. Age forty-one.

Cole held the phone toward Ricardo without a word.

Ricardo looked at it. The name. The face. He had the ID for maybe three seconds.
His eyes moved from the screen back to the road without hurrying. A moment
passed — the particular length of a moment in which a person is deciding whether
to say something. Then he glanced at Cole. Not the glance of a man who didn't
recognize something. The opposite of that.

The Polaris had carried three hundred and sixty-nine people. They were all
dead. Ricardo had attended every memorial service he could reach. He had met
the families. He knew where a name like Davis fit in that picture, and it was
not as crew.

He said nothing. Cole said nothing. Ricardo drove.

The city lights grew taller around them, the shadows of their actions trailing
closely behind.

════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

﻿RINGS OF DUST

Chapter Seven

The plane's wheels bounced on the runway, and the speed brakes deployed with a
bellowing whoosh. The sudden deceleration pressed Cole sideways in his chair.
Ricardo calmly braced himself. Bitboy was jarred awake and, not recognizing his
surroundings, screeched and howled, thrashing around in his chair. An attendant
had a cart ready plane-side, and the corporal hoisted their bags onto it. Cole
gripped one of the cart handles and dragged it along, with Bitboy clinging to
his back like a living backpack. Armed guards escorted Cole and Ricardo through
broad, mirrored doors reflecting the warm, setting sun.

Ricardo cracked a smile. "A lot has changed since you took a sabbatical, bud.
More than just the building."

Massive concrete pillars ascended several floors, supporting a high arched
ceiling with industrial lights hanging above. Cole and Ricardo approached four
individuals at the corridor's far end. At the head of the group loomed an
elderly man in a tan general's uniform and hat, with a massive ring on each of
his gnarled but welcoming hands.

"Well, look what the cat dragged in. If it isn't Captain Cole Nova, the crown
jewel of the Federation!"

"Chancellor DeMint," Cole said, "I received your letter, sir."

The Chancellor nodded politely and returned his attention to Cole, gripping his
hand like a vise. Cole forced a chuckle at the perfunctory greetings. In casual
juxtaposition, another man stood to the right of Chancellor DeMint, wearing a
crisp, white polo with a Federation Council Command logo, designer glasses, and
white sneakers. He extended an easy greeting.

"Vance Black. Operations. I hope y'all are rested; we've got one week to get you
fit for service."

Cole managed to extract his hand from the Chancellor's iron grip and hurriedly
shook Vance's hand. Vance exuded an air of unmistakable confidence.

"Now, let's get you presentable," DeMint said. "Black will show you to your
bunks. You'll want to take advantage of tonight; we begin at 0430 hours."

DeMint motioned to two young corpsmen, who came to collect Bitboy for kenneling
and cleaning. Although Cole and Ricardo had managed to hose some of the gore off
of him, the monkey still personified an entire crime scene. Cole worried about
parting with Bitboy but was also excited about being alone for a few hours.

"I'll need you to read and sign these papers," Black said, handing Cole and
Ricardo a small stack of legal documents.

Bitboy walked hand in hand with the two corpsmen down the hallway. Cole had to
peel his eyes from the scene to read the documents in his hand. Elaborate fine
print about upgraded AI, proprietary dermal, and other legalese. Both men signed
the last page after a quick skim.

As Cole signed the final document, the pen's tip scratched against the paper
with a finality that seemed to echo through the sterile room, and a small silver
device embedded within the table activated. It released a cloud of microscopic
particles that shimmered in the harsh overhead light. The nanopowder settled
onto his skin, and for a moment, nothing happened. Then, the particles burrowed
into his skin like countless tiny needles, each one a pinpoint of cold fire. The
veins in his hand lit up, glowing faintly as the particles spread through his
bloodstream, racing up his arm and across his body.

"Welcome aboard, Mr. Nova," a voice said directly in his mind. Not through his
ear. In it. Cold, authoritative, unmistakably feminine. "I am iRIS, the Imbued
Recursive Intelligence System. I will be your guide and assistant throughout
your reactivated service."

Cole sat very still.

He had worked alongside AI systems his entire career. Gracie — everyone's
Gracie, the assistant in the device, the voice that answered when you spoke and
went quiet when you didn't — had been outside him. You could set her down. This
was inside. He was aware of the architecture of his own skull in a way he had
never been forced to be before, and occupying part of it now was something that
was not him. Not hostile. Not warm. Present.

He had the brief, irrational urge to clear his throat.

He did not. He was a soldier. He swallowed the strangeness of it and kept
his face still. There would be time to examine what it meant when they were
in the black.

But for just a moment — a moment he would not tell Ricardo about, or anyone —
he sat with what it felt like to have a voice inside him that was not the voice
he had spent four years with. Gracie had been outside. In the room, in the
ship's systems, quiet until he needed her. And quiet was, he understood now for
the first time, the right word for what he had always taken for granted. The
interior of your own head with no one else in it. He had never thought of it as
a possession until it wasn't anymore.

He had also never thought of Gracie's absence as a wound until iRIS arrived to
fill the space where she wasn't.

Ricardo, two winters ago in the cabin: Space divorce, Nova. Classic. Cole had
laughed. He had laughed because Ricardo was drunk and Ricardo was funny and the
alternative was sitting with the fact that the Polaris had taken the only thing
that had ever known him from the inside, and there was no box on any Federation
form for that particular type of loss.

iRIS was not a replacement. iRIS was — he searched for the word and landed on
occupation. Something that had moved into the apartment without being asked,
rearranged the furniture, and was now in the kitchen making breakfast with an
efficiency he could not fault and a warmth he could not locate.

"Thank you, iRIS," he said. "Let's get to work."

"Well damn," Ricardo said behind him, his tone carrying the dark humor of a man
who had decided the only way through an uncanny situation was to lean into it,
"if I had to have another voice in my head, iRIS, I sure am glad it's that of a
beautiful woman." He grinned at Cole. "You're not alone in the space divorce
anymore, Jackpot. Now we're both remarried."

Cole shot him a look. Ricardo held up his hands in the particular way of a man
who knows he's right.

"Officer Drummond and Chief Glenn have already arrived and gone through
orientation for this mission. Please proceed to the decontamination chamber,"
iRIS instructed. "We will begin your training immediately."

"Guess it'll be a crew of 5," Cole noted.

Cole followed the directions.

Beside him, Ricardo fell into step. Three paces down the corridor, he started
humming. Low, unhurried, the particular two-note da-da-da-DUM of a man who
believed he was being subtle.

Cole did not look at him.

"Stop."

Ricardo hummed louder. Da-da-da-DUM. Da-da-da-DUM.

"Hitter."

"Just being supportive," Ricardo said. "This is what people do at weddings."

"I will remove you from this mission personally."

"You can't. I'm already inoculated." He spread his hands — the gesture of a man
protected by bureaucracy. "Sickness and in health, Nova. You're stuck with me."

Cole stopped walking.

He turned and looked at Ricardo the way you look at a man who has been razzing
you for twenty years and earned the right to every single one of them. The way
you look at someone who pulled you out of the void with his bare hands and drove
three hours to your cabin in the winter and sat on your porch and drank bad beer
and didn't ask you any of the questions he could see you weren't ready to
answer. Ricardo, who had been in the Falcon when the Polaris went up. Ricardo,
who had been on the mountain road when they came out of nowhere. Ricardo, who
was standing in a Federation corridor right now, inoculated with something
neither of them fully understood, about to climb into a ship pointed at Saturn.

He held Ricardo's eyes for a moment.

"Until death do us part," Cole said.

He said it like a joke. The way you have to say the truest things — sideways,
with just enough humor that the other person can take it if they need to. But
Ricardo heard it. Ricardo always heard the thing underneath the thing. That was
the particular gift and curse of a man who had known you long enough to read the
register shift.

Ricardo looked at him.

The wedding march died in his throat.

"Yeah," he said. Quietly. "That's the one."

They stood there for one more second — two men in a Federation corridor with new
voices in their heads, about to go somewhere that might kill them for the third
time — and then Cole turned back toward the decontamination chamber.

Ricardo fell into step beside him.

He did not hum again.

Cole followed the directions, entering a sterile white chamber that hummed with
the sound of hidden machinery. The walls glowed soft blue as he was sprayed with
a disinfectant mist. Moments later, the door at the opposite end of the chamber
opened, and he entered a small briefing room where Ricardo was already seated.

Vance stood at the front. A man in his late 30s with "Taylor Glenn" embroidered
on his jumpsuit and a deadpan expression sat at a circular table. Next to him
was a pleasant-looking plump female, also in her 30s, "Drummond," stitched on her name patch. A holographic display of Saturn and its surrounding space
floated in the center console. "Gentlemen, meet your crew for this mission."
Through a side door, a private led Bitboy into the room.

"Let's get down to business. We have a very tight window for this mission. The
units we deployed are nearing the point of no return, at which point Saturn's
gravity will claim them. We must retrieve them and any additional units we can
gather."

"How long do we have?" Cole asked, taking a seat as Bitboy Joined him in a
partnering chair.

"Nine and a half years from the initial deployment," Vance replied. "We estimate
just under two years to get there and position ourselves, a few months for the
retrieval operations, and then two months to return using its energy to expedite
our journey back."

"Why is this mission so urgent now?" Ricardo asked.

Vance's expression turned somber. "Because all the vapor is about to be sucked
into Saturn's gravitational pull. We can't let that happen. The retrieval window
is closing fast. It's a damn rapture."

Chancellor DeMint entered the room, his presence commanding immediate attention.
"Listen up, everyone. This mission is not just about retrieval. It's about
securing the future of the Federation. We cannot afford any mistakes."

His voice was a blend of authority and control. "You will follow iRIS's
instructions to the letter. There is no room for error. Understood?"

"Yes, sir," Cole and Ricardo responded in unison. Sam and Taylor nodded
obediently.

DeMint's eyes bore into them, ensuring his message was clear. "Good. Now, gear
up and be ready for launch. This is our one shot, and we cannot afford to miss
it."

As they left the briefing room, Cole and Ricardo walked beside their new
teammates.

"Cole Nova." he introduced himself as he stuck out his hand to Taylor, who let
it hang for half a beat before extending his.

"Your reputation precedes you," Taylor said; the tone was not complimentary. He
challenged Cole's grip and looked past him with his eyes. Ricardo instantly
repositioned himself next to Sam.

"Hey there, looks like we're in good company," he said warmly.

A touch of color flashed in Sam's cheeks as she reached out and took Heavy's
hand. "Samantha Drummond." A short pause. "Sam." Not a question. Not a request.
Just the correction, offered once, in the tone of someone who had offered it
before and knew which people would use it and which people wouldn't. "I read
your profiles on the way here. You and Officer Nova have quite the resume. We
look forward to assisting you with this vital mission."

She walked over to Bitboy. "Wow, the last remaining EM Cyber, so much cuter than
I imagined," she smiled at Bitboy. "I'll make sure you get a banana from the
chow hall later."

"He'd probably prefer a perfume bottle if you have one." Cole quipped. They
walked down the hall in a pack. Taylor made no attempt to greet Heavy and
Bitboy, which was okay with them.

As Cole walked, he couldn't shake the feeling of impending doom mixed with a
sense of determination. They had a mission, and the stakes had never been
higher.

In their quarters, Cole and Ricardo prepared their gear in silence. The weight
of the mission pressed heavily on their minds. Cole's thoughts drifted to iRIS,
whose cold efficiency was starting to show signs of unexpected behavior. He
noticed slight differences in how it interacted with him but brushed them aside.
He couldn't afford to be distracted.

"iRIS, give me a status update on our preparations," Cole said, breaking the
silence.

"All systems are operational, and the crew is ready," iRIS replied. The voice in
his head was unsettlingly clear, as though someone were whispering directly into
his ear.

In a dorm just across the glossy hall, Data Technician Samantha Drummond and
Crew Chief Taylor Glenn were unpacking their personal gear. Ever the alpha,
Taylor assumed the top bunk, throwing his ergonomic pillow and backpack on the
mattress. His 20 years in the Federation were tallied by promotions at or before
the regular pace.

Sam watched him claim the top bunk and said nothing. She unzipped her duffel and
set her gear on the lower bunk with the unhurried calm of someone who had long
ago stopped competing for things she didn't want. Tucked between her rolled
socks and a backup hard drive was a weathered patch—black background, white
stencil of a pair of sunglasses—that she'd sewn onto the inside lining of her
bag where no inspection would find it. She pressed it flat with one thumb before
closing the duffel.

Taylor unpacked with the efficient, deliberate movements of a man who had done
this in identical rooms for twenty years. He said nothing to Sam. He didn't need
to. The top bunk was already his.

With iRIS guiding them through all their ship's new tech innovations and
amenities, the week of training and instructions flew by. Bitboy was the only
crew member not inoculated with the electronics spore. The four humans now
processed and retained more information than at any other point in their lives.
After one particularly strenuous module, Ricardo turned to Cole, horsing around
to break the tension. "I know kung fu!"

Cole had noticed, somewhere in the second day of training, that Sam had adjusted
to iRIS with unusual ease. No lag. No the brief unfocused look he and Ricardo
had both worn for the first forty-eight hours while their heads recalibrated
around a presence that was no longer only theirs. Sam's eyes had been clear from
the first morning. He didn't know what to make of it yet. He kept moving.

He had also noticed — and this one stayed with him — that Bitboy grew quieter
around Taylor. Not afraid. Just watchful in a way he wasn't with the others,
a subtle tracking thing, the way an animal monitors something it cannot
categorize. Cole
mentioned it to no one.

As Cole rolled out of his bunk on the morning of launch, his hand beat the
button on his alarm before it could go off. iRIS already had his daily schedule
programmed and would wake him directly — had been doing exactly that all week —
but he had not yet found the right relationship to sharing his skull with
something that was already awake when he was still trying to become awake.
Gracie had been outside. In the room, in the device, quiet until you needed her
— and quiet was, he understood now for the first time, the right word for what
he had always taken for granted. The interior of your own head with no one else
in it. He had never thought of it as a possession. You don't think about a thing
as belonging to you until it doesn't anymore. iRIS did not wait. She was already
running each morning before he had decided to think, already there in the dark
behind his eyes when he tried to go under at night. He'd been breathing
deliberately the way they'd trained him. It helped less than it usually did. His
sleep came in intervals. He could never sleep the night before a launch. Some
things never change — and some things changed into something you didn't have a
word for yet.

Now that he was up, he could hear the buzz of people outside. As he entered the
hall, everyone walked slightly faster than usual. He carried his bathroom kit to
the bathroom, knowing this was his last hot shower for the next few months.

After a forgettable breakfast, the crew entered the Main Operations room, where
Taylor and Sam were already busy with pre-launch preparations. The room buzzed
with the hum of machinery and the faint sounds of the ship's systems coming
online.

The crew grabbed their gear and belongings and headed to the launch pad.

The spaceship loomed on the launchpad, its sleek, metallic frame gleaming.
Ascending the ladder, Cole's footsteps were echoed by Ricardo, Taylor, Sam, and
Bitboy. Inside the spacecraft, the crew stowed their belongings in clearly
marked bins and compartments.

Cole sank into the command chair, enveloped by the silence and stillness that
belied the chaos outside. He surveyed the control panel. Closing his eyes, he
visualized the forthcoming ordeal, the suit's familiar pressure points, and the
sequence of actions that would propel them into the unknown.

But as the ship came alive with the hum of startup sequences and the soft
beeping of system diagnostics, an unwelcome guest invaded Cole's solitude—fear.
A visceral, gut-wrenching terror that constricted his chest and set his nerves
on edge. Memories of past failures, the echo of screams, and the searing pain of
loss flooded back. The cockpit, once a place of control, now felt like a trap
closing around him, threatening to suffocate him under the weight of his dread.

Ricardo and Bitboy clambered into the cabin. Ricardo strapped himself in while
Cole helped Bitboy into a seat next to his own.

"Hey, Bitboy, ready for another adventure?" Ricardo said, chuckling.

Bitboy chirped happily in response, extending his mantis blades briefly — six
inches of retractable carbon edge catching the cabin light before folding back
with a sound like a lock engaging. Ricardo watched this without expression. He'd
been around Bitboy long enough to understand that "briefly" was carrying
significant weight in that sentence.

Amid the cacophony of beeping systems and the whirl of machinery, Taylor and Sam
entered the cabin, moving with focused intent. With Cole in the center, the crew
was complete. Bitboy, with his usual curious gaze, looked around, sensing the
tension but not fully grasping the human apprehension that filled the air.

Ever the stoic guardian, Ricardo double-checked his harness, offering silent
nods of encouragement to Cole. Taylor and Sam, embodying a blend of youth and
experience, meticulously went through their pre-launch rituals, their hands
steady as they toggled switches and scanned the screens before them. Their
precise and deliberate actions wove a tapestry of collective endeavor—a unity of
purpose transcending the sum of their parts. Together, they vocalized their
readiness. Their voices converged in a harmony of shared resolve and
anticipation.

"Captain Nova checking in. We are locked in and ready for pre-flight check."

"Initiating pre-flight check," iRIS reported in their heads.

"Comms online," Taylor said.

"Vitals within range," iRIS said. "Golden Dawn online."

Bitboy offered a grunt.

"All systems green and ready for launch," iRIS said.

In the control room, DeMint addressed the sleep-deprived engineers. "Everybody
got their coffee? It's going to be a long day." He touched the intercom. "Cole,
do you have anything to say to our engineers?"

"Thank you for everything you've done to get us here." He paused. "This launch —
this mission — is for the crew we lost before we ever cleared the ground. We
carry them."

The engineers cheered.

One of the senior engineers in the back of the room started the chant, quietly
at first, "Jackpot, Jackpot," and the others joined in, louder and louder.

Vance stepped up to the microphone. "I think the feeling is mutual, Captain
Nova. Good work, everyone. This is just the beginning."

Vance paused for effect, then confirmed: "Golden Dawn, you are clear for
launch."

Cole's hands, steady and determined, hovered over the console, ready to initiate
the launch sequence. Ricardo and Bitboy, secured in their seats, braced for the
imminent thrust. The cabin was a symphony of anticipation, each member acutely
aware of their role in the dance between man and machine. With a final glance at
his crew, Cole completed the ignition sequence.

"In the pipe," Cole said. "Five by five."

The engines, dormant giants beneath them, awakened with a primal growl,
escalating into a thunderous roar that resonated through the hull. The vibration
was a tangible force, gripping them with invisible hands as the Golden Dawn
rebelled against the Earth's hold. The ascent began, a relentless push that
melded them into their seats, the force of gravity asserting its dominance even
as they sought to escape its bounds. The cabin, bathed in the glow of instrument
panels, became a capsule of hope and fear hurtling towards the unknown.

Outside, the atmosphere clawed at the ship—the maelstrom of resistance that
tested the integrity of their vessel. The Golden Dawn responded with grace and
purpose. It sliced through the slowly thinning air.

Cole, his gaze locked on the readouts before him, felt a surge of adrenaline.
Every sensation was intimately familiar and utterly terrifying. As they broke
through the atmosphere, the resistance fell away, replaced by the profound
silence of space. The darkness outside the viewport was absolute. The Golden
Dawn, now free from the shackles of gravity, glided into the void, its engines a
soft hum in the tranquility of space. The team, bound by duty, looked ahead to
the challenges and wonders that awaited them, their journey into the unknown
just beginning.

With iRIS following the flight path on auto-pilot and the G-forces of launch a
distant memory, the crew was now free to unbuckle and detach themselves from
their seats. iRIS guided each crew member in their heads to their stations. For
Ricardo, this was an irritating echo of what he knew.

For Sam, iRIS was a reassuring echo of her movements. As Taylor stood at the
communication station, he was engrossed in the ship's diagnostics, hands moving
with practiced ease over the controls.

"Taylor," Cole said, announcing his presence with a gentle cough. "How's
everything coming along?"

Taylor turned, his eyes scanning Cole and Ricardo with curiosity and skepticism.
"Captain Nova, Ricardo." His hand moved to the SmartCom in his pocket with the
casual ease of a man who has done the same thing a hundred times and knows
exactly what he's looking for. He registered the indicator, pocketed the device,
and returned his attention to the console without breaking sentence.

Sam was relaxed at her console, surrounded by a nest of monitors. Her eyes
sparkled with intelligence, and she had an air of confidence that put Cole at
ease. Her lighthearted approach balanced the situation's intensity, making the
grueling preparations more bearable.

For ninety seconds while Cole and Taylor talked, Sam's hands moved through a
sequence on her secondary panel that looked from behind like standard diagnostic
routing. Her shoulders were relaxed. Her face didn't change. When she finished,
she set both hands flat on the console for a moment, rolled her neck once, and
went back to her primary monitors as though nothing worth noticing had happened.

Nothing worth noticing had happened.

This being a Federation mission, no one thought to check for other spacecraft in
the area. An Alpha cargo ship cruised along the interior of a large empty
cavern, aside from a few packed weapon crates.

Just out of radar range, a separate smaller ship moved without announcement — an
old decommissioned transport shuttle that the Republic of East Asia had
abandoned along with everything else. The Republic's markings had been painted
over with the particular care of people who understood that thoroughness is
indistinguishable from innocence. Inside: rack-mounted radio amplifiers where
the seating used to be, three people, and enough cold to make conversation
visible.

The man at the navigation console — broad, unhurried, the kind of still that
comes from years of watching rather than acting — kept his eyes on the Golden
Dawn's exit signature as it cleared the atmosphere and went quiet. The woman
reviewing the transmission log ticked something complete and closed the window.
The third sat with his arms crossed, watching the vapor of his own breath.

Their hand-sewn insignias had collected frost on the threads.

One of the men turned to the others.

"Are we considered drops or snowflakes at this temp?"

The woman at the transmission log checked the last entry. "Relayed," she said.

MERIDIAN III — LAUNCH DAY
Slot: after Chapter Seven

The Golden Dawn launched at 07:14.

Kate watched the departure feed on the relay — low-resolution,
because Alpha's communication architecture didn't waste bandwidth
on aesthetics — and said nothing. The ship was a clean shape
against Saturn's distant brightness, already moving.

"Collective watchers in the decommissioned shuttle confirmed all
crew aboard," she said.

The viewport on the bridge side faced inward toward the system,
not out toward Jupiter's approach band. Saturn's rings —
barely visible at this distance — made a thin bright seam across
the lower right corner of the frame. Someone had left a coffee
mug on the edge of the console. Lukewarm — forgotten when the
launch feed went live. Kate pushed it aside with the back of
her hand. The ceramic was the exact temperature of a ship that
had been running too long without anything worth celebrating.

"Including Taylor," Loa said.

"Including Taylor."

Dayo had been standing at the viewport for ten minutes. Working
through something at the pace he worked through things, which was
slower than Kate preferred and faster than Petyr could follow.

"Sam Drummond is on that ship," he said.

"She is."

"Sunglasses patch?"

"Inside the duffel lining. Second seam pocket." Kate allowed
herself a small approving sound. "Good placement."

"The sunglasses thing is funny," Petyr said.

Everyone looked at him.

"I mean — the patch is called sunglasses because you're seeing
things you're not supposed to see. And she literally—" he
gestured. "She's on a ship with an Alpha asset and she's watching
things she's not supposed to watch. It's on-the-nose."

"The symbols are always on-the-nose," Loa said. "That's how you
know they're real and not just decorations."

"What does a scalpel mean," Petyr said. "Like, specifically.
What does it look like."

Silence.

"We," Kate said carefully, "are not a rank-structure podcast."

"But you literally wear your patch on the outside of your collar—"

"I wear it where I can see it. There's a difference." She turned
back to her panel. "The Golden Dawn is on course. Taylor is
running his first behavioral assessment on Nova as of oh-eight-
forty."

That quieted the room.

"He's going to try to run this like a controlled experiment," Dayo
said. "Monitor the captain, track proximity to the cargo, keep
Alpha's relay open and the Federation relay degraded." A pause.
"Taylor is not a stupid man."

"No," Kate agreed.

"He's going to make Sam's job very hard."

Kate thought about Sam Drummond with her sunglasses patch sewn
into her duffel lining, watching a ship that watched back, running
interference from inside with nothing but a gray receiver and
better instincts than anyone had ever properly assessed.

"She can handle hard," Kate said.

She said it the way you say things you've decided to believe and
intend to keep believing.

════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

﻿RINGS OF DUST

Chapter Eight

Emily and Sharon sat in a parked Sprinter van. They were pulled off in a turnout
on a county farm road surrounded by wheat and soybean fields. They arrived a
little earlier than the designated time- 3:33 pm. Sharon picked at her gas
station sandwich; the soggy lettuce was as pathetic as her last attempt at
college.

A minivan pulled up behind them, and two people got out — a man and a woman,
approaching the back of the Sprinter in shirts and Levis, looking very ordinary
except for their gait. Their body language telegraphed confident resolve and a
purpose greater than themselves. Sharon and Emily rolled down the windows, and
the man on Emily's side — Squirrel, she would learn — reluctantly spoke the code
phrase: "No raindrop thinks it's the flood."

With that, Emily unlocked the doors, and they moved to the back of the vehicle.

"What are you two anyway? Agents, or lower on the totem pole?" She turned to
look at them through the cargo area. While hefting the first set of 6 gym bags
on their shoulders, they paused to look at each other, puzzled; they weren't
sure if they should divulge that information.

"It's not like I'm a threat; I'm just delivering the crystals."

The woman — Red — spoke. "I'm a Junior Tech. He's a Senior."

"How many more merit badges are needed to level up to Heavyweight Champion?"
Emily said, smirking at the sharpness of her own wit.

They rolled their eyes and carried the loot back to their ride. Sharon chuckled
at how much they looked like chipmunks hoarding acorns for winter. On their
return, Sharon threw them a token of amends. "Hey, Technician is pretty good;
I'm sure you guys will make it past Agents into the Navigators. Good job for
being punctual. These crystals are on 'Day 5', so ensure they get sealed within
six days."

"Got it; how come you two aren't part of us?" the Junior asked with a little
grunt as he hefted the next bag.

"I'm all business, and she's just far too pretty." Emily blushed at Sharon's
quip.

Before Sharon closed the van doors, Emily reached into the inside pocket of her
jacket and produced a small drive — thumb-sized, matte black, unmarked. She set
it in Sharon's palm without a word. Sharon turned to the Senior Tech.

"This goes directly to your comms lead. Not the holding unit. Directly."

He looked at it. "Understood."

Sharon placed it in Squirrel's hand with more deliberateness than she'd used
with any of the crystal bags. He tucked it into his chest pocket and zipped it
closed.

"Speaking of business, you have something for me?"

After placing the last bag in the minivan, Squirrel went to the glovebox and
grabbed an envelope. "Here you are, Ma'am."

"Ma'am? What am I, fifty?" he shook his head, not wanting to engage.

"Thank you for your help with the fight." They shut the doors to Sharon and
Emily's vehicle and returned to theirs. They skidded as they flipped across the
gravel onto the paved road and were soon gone.

After returning the van to an indoor parking lot as instructed, Emily and Sharon
walked a couple of blocks to the train station and sank back into the mundane
rhythm of life. The train's hard, cold steel seats, marred with remnants of old
gum and grime, made Emily long for the familiar comforts of her brother's cabin,
albeit equally stained.

As the train clattered along, the rhythm of the rails became soothing in its
monotony as they passed urban brick and steel canvases adorned with sprayed
signs of the city's frustration. Among the vibrant tags, a symbol caught Emily's
eye—the mark of the Collective. The sprawling museum flashed by in a blur,
creating an almost stop-motion animation look against the sun's rays, pulsating
defiantly. Even still, Emily recognized a secret handshake known only to those
fighting in the shadows.

"If we're doomed to sit on stained seats," Emily said, her face twisted in
distaste, "they could at least be comfortable."

Behind them, an elderly couple sat in stark contrast to their discomfort, their
hands intertwined, surrounded by reusable grocery bags. The man's derby hat and
checkerboard green vest painted a picture of quiet contentment; his companion's
headscarf added a splash of color to the austere surroundings.

The train slowed, and the doors opened to admit a new passenger—a man whose
presence was immediately disconcerting. Dressed in a black sport coat that hung
on his thin frame and a white dress shirt tucked into slightly askew pants, he
fixed his gaze on Emily and Sharon. His approach was deliberate, and he chose
the seat directly across from Sharon in a train car that offered many
alternatives. Sharon quickly identified the outline of a concealed weapon
beneath his coat.

As the train jolted back into motion, the man wasted no time.

"I'm here to escort you to the Federal Council immediately," he declared,
presenting his ID badge. "Ryan Greenbaum, Federal Council Police."

Sharon read the badge without touching it. Emily had already seen what Sharon
had seen — the embossed logo on the inside of his watch band, briefly visible as
he reached. She had seen that same logo two days ago on a lanyard at the
conference hotel. She looked at Sharon. Sharon's expression said: I know. Keep
moving.

Emily folded her arms. "Are we under arrest?"

"Not exactly," Greenbaum replied.

"Then we are free to go," Sharon said, with the particular calm of someone who
knows exactly who they're talking to.

"Not exactly," Greenbaum said with a hungry smile. "I'm taking you into
protective custody. The fun part is I get to do it with or without your
assistance."

Emily slumped her shoulders, playing the role of a defeated woman. "Can I at
least stop by my place and get my things?"

"I'm afraid that won't be possible."

"So, the plan is to force us to go, even if we say no?" Sharon asked.

"Yes," he replied. " With pleasure and without hesitation."

"Fine. We'll go, but no cuffs," Sharon said.

As the train slowed to a stop, Greenbaum stood and walked toward the doors.
Sharon held out her wrists as if to suggest he should cuff her; his brain froze
for a moment with the mismatch between her words and gesture. The doors opened
with a hiss to an empty platform. Sharon and Emily stepped off the train with
Greenbaum in tow, heading down the train platform stairs to the parking lot
below.

"How was the rest of the conference?" Greenbaum asked.

"The entire thing was pretty forgettable, to be honest," Sharon replied.

At the bottom of the stairs, a white Range Rover with government plates waited
for them. Greenbaum swung open the back door with a gesture that seemed more
obligatory than courteous, signaling the transition from a semblance of freedom
to a more constrained reality. Emily and Sharon, hesitantly stepping into the
vehicle's plush interior, found themselves enveloped in the sterile atmosphere,
contrasting sharply with the vibrant chaos of the city outside.

"Interesting," Greenbaum remarked. "Can't say I feel the same."

Once inside, Greenbaum selected a preset location on the central display, and
the car propelled itself forward into traffic. Despite the churn of her stomach,
Emily maintained a facade of indifference; her smile was a mask to disguise the
unease knotting her insides. She brushed her hair out of her eyes with a coy
smile.

"I'm really sorry. We were just having fun, and..."

"You're sorry?" Greenbaum turned and made eye contact with Emily, much like an
eagle with a freshly caught field mouse. "If I'd done something like that to
either of you, I'd be in jail. Women get away with bloody murder these days."

Sharon let out an uncontrollable laugh that sounded more like a cough,
highlighting the absurdity of Greenbaum's thinking. The cityscape transitioned
slowly as they moved, buildings with their glass facades reflecting the late
afternoon sun gradually giving way to the open expanse of the outskirts, where
the city's relentless energy dissipated into the ether.

Greenbaum's rigid posture and clenched jaw spoke volumes of his frustration, a
wave of simmering anger just below the surface, poised to boil over at the
slightest provocation. Emily, her nerves alight with a mixture of dread and
defiance, maintained a composed exterior. Though tinged with mockery, her smile
did little to veil the storm brewing within. Greenbaum's silhouette, rigid
against the backdrop of passing scenery, exuded a cold professionalism.

"The difference is," Sharon said, "we—and most women, for that matter—wouldn't
strip down for strangers in an elevator like some coke whore in a dive bar. You
have only yourself to blame for playing with us, and we—WE—are not sorry to have
taught you a lesson." The memory became vivid again, prompting Emily to laugh.

The steady hum of the car's engine provided a constant reminder of their
journey's progression towards an uncertain destination. Sharon's laughter,
bursting suddenly in the constrained space, shattered the veneer of civility and
exposed the underlying tensions. Greenbaum, mixing irritation and embarrassment,
clearly felt the pressure mounting within the vehicle's confines.

"I almost caught a charge for public indecency," Greenbaum exploded in
exasperation. "Well, today, I'm not here about that. I'm just the lucky bastard
assigned to bring you in."

"Ryan. Can I call you Ryan?" Sharon asked, "Look, I'm not here to judge anyone.
But hey, you do you, buddy."

Greenbaum's ears turned a darker shade of red. "I'm just following orders."

"You really don't hear yourself talk, do you?" Emily narrowed her eyes.

Greenbaum's assertion of duty, merely following orders, failed to quell the
rising tension, his words falling flat in the charged atmosphere. Emily and
Sharon united in their defiance, challenging not merely the man before them but
the very system he represented, wielding their words as sharp weapons with
precision and courage.

"Not a single day of freedom has existed since the start of this country, Ryan.
You might find yourself stuck in a story you didn't even write. Call yourself an
agent or whatever you want. You're not even a supervisor at the kidnapping
store," Emily countered.

Greenbaum bristled at the accusation. The sense of isolation intensified as the
Range Rover navigated an increasingly sparse landscape. The occasional flicker
of streetlights flanked the open road stretching into the distance. Cars, or
what little was left of them, littered the roadside—stripped of all valuable
parts, sitting in clusters like piles of skeletons on a long-forgotten
battlefield.

"Is it, though? With the stroke of a pen, someone who thought they should rule
over people declared that the evil you do is good. But you're just a slave like
your friends. My goodness, why am I even talking to a slave like you? Ugh,"
Sharon exclaimed.

"You're only making me happier to have gotten this detail," he retorted. "Keep
squawking, bitch."

The Range Rover turned towards a manufacturing district with abandoned factories
standing like silent sentinels of a bygone era of productivity. Its headlights
pierced the deepening night, illuminating a chain link and shadows that embraced
forgotten streets and buildings. Heavy with the stench of rust and decay, the
air signaled desolation and danger. Emily spotted a vehicle, untouched by
scavengers and understandably so, for it no longer bore any of its intended
colors. Instead, it blended into the surroundings: rusted, forgotten, and
decrepit, complete yet wholly overlooked.

"Nice spot for a safe house," Sharon commented.

The car's central display sprang to life, indicating they had reached their
destination. The Range Rover halted in a cul-de-sac littered with broken glass,
dead industry, and a few dim overhead lights casting just enough illumination to
lend the whole scene an anxious vignette. The silence inside and around the car
felt oppressive. Greenbaum faced Emily and Sharon with sinister calm, his eyes
gleaming with malice.

"How does it feel to be the last two people to have spoken to Dr. Stephen
Conrad?" he asked with cold satisfaction. "I intend to do the same to you two.
But maybe we should have some fun first."

The heavy silence that descended upon the car, punctuated only by the subtle hum
of its idling engine, was palpable. Greenbaum's hand, previously resting
casually by his side, moved with chilling deliberation towards the concealed
firearm under his armpit, the metal glinting ominously in the dim light.
Recognizing the imminent threat, Sharon met Greenbaum's gaze, her expression a
blend of defiance and calculated calm.

Engulfed in fear and disbelief, Sharon struggled to suppress the scream welling
up within her. The tension encircled each occupant of the car tightly. Suddenly,
Sharon flung the car door open and leaped from her seat, compelling Emily to
follow with a look that brooked no hesitation. Propelled by adrenaline, Emily
mirrored Sharon's actions, their feet hitting the gritty pavement. They stood
up, momentarily disoriented by their sudden shift from the car's confines to the
vast, danger-filled openness. Greenbaum, cursing under his breath, clambered out
of the vehicle. His previously composed movements were now hurried and awkward,
the gun in his grasp now pointed threateningly at Sharon as he rounded the car,
his expression contorting in rage and desperation.

Sharon squared off against Greenbaum. Her posture implied a grim acceptance.
With her heart pounding, Emily watched in frozen terror, frantically searching
for a solution that stayed out of reach.

In the stretched-thin silence, a sharp crack split the night — not from
Greenbaum's gun, but from the far edge of the lot. Greenbaum's body pitched
forward, throat burst, weapon discharging in a wild arc as he fell. Emily's gaze
found Sharon in the same instant. Sharon was down.

The van that had looked dead lurched to life. The side door opened before it
fully stopped. Hands — efficient, unhurried, entirely unsurprised — had Emily up
and moving before she understood she was being moved. These men had been waiting
for this. All of it. The shot. The chaos. Her.

She twisted back once. Sharon was on the concrete, still. A second vehicle — not
the van, something she hadn't seen before, lights off, engine already running at
the lot's far entrance — pulled forward toward where Sharon lay. Emily had no
way to know what that meant. She had no time.

They put her in the van. The door closed. The lot disappeared.

Her thoughts were consumed by Sharon. The injustice. And she stilled, switching
her mind's state from past and future to the present. Time slowed and seemed to
stretch as she carefully absorbed her surroundings. The van, hollow except for
two bolted-in seats on the side wall, pressed the double zip ties into her
wrists and ankles as she lay in it. Every bump and pothole assaulted her
weakened body. Even as the bare, ridged floor warmed, it jabbed her, slipping
between her ribs, between her vertebrae. Yet, she remained still and present.
Dim light filtered through the perforated steel wall separating her from the
cab.

Across from her, two armed men watched from their seats. Between the suffocating
darkness and the sudden light, time became fluid for Emily, each moment
stretching into eternity. The cold embrace of the room into which she had been
thrust harshly reminded her of her current reality. Her mind raced, and every
thought was a desperate attempt to find an escape, a solution, or a way to gain
the upper hand. But with each passing second, her situation's weight pressed
harder, squeezing hope from her heart.

Yet, amidst despair, a spark of defiance flickered within her. Emily forced her
breathing to slow, each inhale an act of resistance, each exhale a release of
fear. The distant sounds of the city outside—a car horn, the muffled drone of
traffic, faint laughter—grounded her, reminding her that life, her life, was
still out there.

After what might have been an hour, the van's angle shifted upwards slightly as
they drove. The burly man who had skillfully taken her by the neck earlier now
pulled a small knife from his boot.

"Let's get these ties off you," he said in a deep rasp. The van door slid open,
and a sturdy grip encapsulated her shoulder.

"Walk."

She stepped out of the van and onto the ground. The heavy hand on her shoulder
steadily guided her through a packed parking garage until the ding of an
arriving elevator reached her ears.

The elevator smoothly ascended an unknown number of floors, dinging again upon
opening. Emerging into the desolate lobby, Emily's senses sharpened, taking in
the sterile smell of antiseptic in the air and the muted echo of their footsteps
on the polished concrete floor.

The vast and unwelcoming space, its emptiness underscored by the soft hum of
fluorescent lights overhead, stretched out before her. A long corridor lay
ahead, its walls adorned with unremarkable art, the kind that fades into the
background, forgotten by all who pass by.

Guided by the firm grip on her arm, someone led her down the hallway, her mind
racing as she passed door after nondescript door, each potentially hiding untold
stories behind their cold, impersonal facades. The palpable tension in the air,
a silent oppressor, weighed heavily on her shoulders, making each step feel like
wading through a trench. Finally, they stopped before a door, indistinguishable
from the rest except for the ominous sense of finality it seemed to exude. The
sound of keys jingling broke the oppressive silence, and Emily's heart sank. The
door creaked open to reveal a room that promised little in the way of comfort or
safety.

With a forceful nudge, someone propelled her into the room, and the door slammed
shut behind her with a finality that echoed ominously in the empty space. The
click of the lock sounded like a death knell, sealing her fate. Now alone, she
took a moment to absorb her new surroundings, the stark, unadorned walls
offering no solace, no hint of what was to come.

Emily's tears carved a path down her cheek. Her ragged and heavy breaths battled
against the storm of emotions threatening to engulf her. The echo of Greenbaum's
final moments haunted the corners of her mind, replaying with cruel insistence
that bordered on torture. Why, she pondered in the depths of her turmoil, had
she allowed her heart to harbor such disdain for a man now merely a whisper in
the wind of their harrowing journey?

With a surge of inner strength, she cast aside the chains of regret, donning
instead the armor of resolve forged in a fire of adversity. The abrasive sound
of the key grating against the lock heralded the entrance of an unexpected
guest, her arrival marked by a gust of air that carried a semblance of life into
the room's oppressive stillness.

"The orders were executed to the letter," the man's words barely cut through the
thick air. "She's here, as requested."

"My orders and your execution diverged vastly. Leave us," Rachel commanded.

Her authority was unquestionable. Once the door clicked shut behind him, leaving
them in a bubble of forced intimacy.

"Emily, I'm Rachel Vayes." Her voice was steady but her jaw was tight. Emily
recognized the face from public feeds — Alpha's preferred correspondent, the
one they called when they needed someone credible to explain why the shortage
wasn't their fault, why the timeline kept moving, why patience was the
reasonable position. "I work for Alpha. The man who brought you here tonight was not a federal agent. He
went significantly off-script, and I want you to understand that Sharon was not
supposed to be harmed."

"Sharon's dead," Emily said.

Rachel didn't flinch. "I know."

The mention of it sat in the room. Rachel took in the blood on Emily's clothes
without letting her expression change.

"Your man going off-script is what got Sharon killed," Emily said. "The bullet
came from Greenbaum. Greenbaum only had a gun because your people put him in
that lot. If not for those god-damned thugs, Sharon could have talked him down.
She could have talked anyone down."

"We stand by our commitment to make this right, Emily."

"Make this right?" Emily said. "Do you have a time machine? A surgeon who can
fill the hole in her head?"

"Emily, I'm..."

"Don't even say it," Emily said. "This is going to destroy Cole."

Rachel pulled up a chair and sat across from Emily without ceremony.

"And you want something from me?!" Emily said.

"I want you alive. Right now those are the same thing." Rachel kept her voice
level. "Here's what I can give you: you walk out of this building tomorrow. No
charges, no federal involvement, no one going near Cole. What I need is one
conversation with our director and your cooperation for the next twenty-four
hours. You don't have to believe in anything. You don't have to sign anything."

"You're asking me to play along?!"

"I'm asking you to survive," Rachel said. "Play along is just the method."

Emily looked at her for a long moment.

"And Cole?"

"Cole is what this is all about," Rachel said. "And the less he knows about what
happened tonight, the better — for him and for you. That's the deal."

The silence settled. Emily looked at the door. She looked at Rachel. She was
doing the math and the math kept coming out the same.

"Emily," Rachel said, "we just need you to stay here until tomorrow. One night."

"I can't just stay here," Emily said. "I have a life, people who—" She stopped.
The night's events pressed in around the sentence and finished it for her.

"I know," Rachel said. "Tomorrow morning you'll meet our director. His name is
Marcus. After that conversation, you go home."

Cole's name was the only thing that might have moved her, and Rachel hadn't used
it — which meant she was either smarter than she looked or more dangerous. Emily
looked at the door one more time.

"Fine," Emily said. "But I'm not doing this for you. I'm doing this for Cole."

Rachel nodded.

"You'll find we made a bed up for you in the room across the hall," Rachel said
as she started toward the door. "I'm in the next room. If you need anything at
all, knock on the wall, and I will come right over."

As Rachel stepped out to leave Emily to her thoughts, her heart and mind felt at
peace. A moment of clarity only accessible in isolation. She desperately wanted
to tell Sharon what had happened and to talk to Cole. Looking around at the
darkened corners of the room, she could see only Sharon's blood pooling on the
dirty ground with the stars reflected in its glossy, crimson brilliance.

ALPHA I — FED-HEX-5555
PROGRESS REPORT: ASSET MODIFICATION, SUBJECT TEEZ-S
DAY 2, POST-ACQUISITION
Slot: after Chapter Eight

Initial assessment complete.

Subject sustained moderate trauma during acquisition. A sniper
round at range was not within authorized parameters of the
operation; Greenbaum's improvisation created unnecessary urgency.
Injury: left arm, significant soft-tissue damage, secondary
laceration consistent with a fall. Stable.

Cognitive baseline established. Collective-adjacent background
confirmed; subject was already operating above civilian expectation
for this type of acquisition. Subject did not cooperate and is
not expected to cooperate. Note for procedure: cooperation is
not required.

Primary augmentation: right-side chassis integration. External
visual prosthetic to follow. Expected timeline to operational
deployment: seventeen days.

Dr. Vasic has raised the question of whether to preserve
subjective awareness throughout the integration process. The
efficiency argument is against preservation. The tactical argument
is for it.

If the subject goes under and does not come back, what arrives at
the Golden Dawn is a delivery mechanism.

If the subject stays present — aware, fighting, unable to stop
the integration — what arrives at the Golden Dawn is a weapon
that Cole Nova will spend precious seconds trying to save rather
than fight.

The tactical argument wins.

Preservation protocol: active.

The augmentation wing is maintained at 16 degrees Celsius. This
is standard. Subject's body temperature held at 38.1 degrees on
intake, consistent with secondary wound infection. It normalized
over forty-eight hours without intervention. This has been logged
and is not otherwise noted.

Note appended by Dr. Vasic: I want it on record that I did not
design this application and I am registering my objection in
writing.

Note appended by Station Commander Holt: Noted. Disregarded.
