MERIDIAN IV — THE NIGHT AFTER THE FARM ROAD
Slot: after Chapter Eight

Kate had been on the Meridian for three years. She had run sweeps
through six significant operations. She had logged intercepts that
became the evidentiary backbone of what was now being built toward
the Grand Hall.

She had not cried on the job before.

"Confirm," Dayo said. He wasn't asking. He knew she had confirmed.

"Sniper shot. Empty lot at the edge of the city. Sharon Teez. Not
expected to survive." She stopped. Started again. "Confirmed
taken by the Alpha vehicle. Not confirmed dead. But the
augmentation vessel is on its way to intercept the Golden Dawn's
course." Another stop. "They're going to use her."

No one spoke.

"I ran with her for six months," Loa said finally. She had her
back to the room. "Before we stopped having reasons to be in the
same city."

Kate hadn't known that.

"She had a droplet," Loa said. "She never asked what it meant.
She just understood it. Immediately. She said: 'Okay, you're
people who do something about it instead of just watching.' And
then she asked how she could help."

Petyr was very still. He was learning that stillness was the
appropriate response to some things, which was better than the
alternatives.

The ship at 0200 was different from the ship at 0800 — not
quieter, but heavier. The hull made a specific sound at this hour
that Loa said was thermal expansion. Kate had started thinking of
it as the ship breathing.

"Emily Nova is alive," Kate said, because someone had to say it.
"She was taken to an Alpha facility and she got out. She's alive.
Rachel has her."

"Then we have something to work with," Dayo said.

On the wall beside Kate's station — where no modification to
standard vessel interior guidelines had been officially authorized,
but where such authorizations had never been specifically enforced
either — there was a small stencil. A water droplet, gunmetal on
gray. Sharon had not put it there. But the person who had put it
there had known her.

Kate touched it briefly before she went back to her panel.

﻿RINGS OF DUST

Chapter Nine

The iRIS effect was cumulative.

By day four it was no longer a voice. It was a presence — something that had
learned the architecture of the crew and had begun occupying it without
announcement. It knew when to speak over the ship's intercom and when to lean in
close. It had calibrated itself to each person individually, which should have
been reassuring and which Cole found, instead, faintly wrong in a way he could
not yet name.

The other thing he could not name was what the Dark Matter was doing to him.

iRIS had explained it, clinically, on day two. Proximity to the Dark Matter
payload at cruising velocity — ten percent of the speed of light — produced
measurable neuroplastic cascades in human neural tissue. The effect was
documented in the mission parameters as a known variable and had not been
flagged for crew review, which told Cole something about how much of the mission
parameters he had not been given. What it meant in practice was that whatever
emotional architecture a person had built over a lifetime of effort was, at
present, running approximately twenty percent less load-bearing than usual. The
walls were thinner. The light got through differently.

Ricardo had put it more plainly in the galley on day three. "Limbic sensitivity.
That's a polite way of saying we feel things more."

"Great," Cole had said. "Crying in space."

"Some of us," Ricardo said, and left it at that.

Sam had said nothing. Sam had adjusted to the effect with what Cole could only
describe as unusual ease. She moved through her shifts with the same precise,
unhurried competence she had shown from the first day, and if the Dark Matter
was doing anything to the floor of her inner world, she did not show it. He had
watched her once or twice, looking for the seams in it, and hadn't found any.
Sam was no stranger to this kind of interior weather. She had simply assessed
the conditions and begun sailing.

Ricardo had handled the DarkX effect with the particular adjustment of a man
who had decided the feeling was information rather than interference. He had
gotten quieter. Not withdrawn — present in a different register, the way a
person gets quiet when they are paying attention to something they have not
previously paid attention to. Cole had caught him watching Sam across the
galley once — not the watching of a person performing surveillance, but the
watching of a person noticing something new.

He had not remarked on it. He suspected Ricardo would have preferred that.

Bitboy was the anomaly.

The chimp was fine. His readings were stable, his threat assessment protocols
cycling normally, his appetite for anything ethanol-adjacent unchanged. But he
had developed a new behavior that Cole had noticed and not yet named.
Whenever Taylor entered a room, Bitboy went still. Not the stillness of sleep or
inattention — the particular stillness of a thing that has made a decision to
watch. His dim-blue LED eyes would track Taylor across the compartment and then
stay on him at rest. No vocalization. No arm deployment. Just the flat attention
of something waiting to understand what it was looking at.

Cole understood the feeling.

---

On the bridge, Taylor worked the starboard control panel with the concentrated
frustration of someone who had decided the problem was external. The file
counter on his secondary screen had been sitting at six percent for eleven
minutes.

"Dammit, iRIS." He didn't quite shout it. "Every clock on this panel is off.
These aren't malfunctions, these are—someone has been at my station."

"All systems functioning within normal parameters," iRIS said. The voice was
measured. Unhurried. Cole noticed it did not say *all systems functioning
normally*, which was the standard response string. It said *within normal
parameters*, which was technically identical and subtly different.

"That's not an answer. Bitboy's been at my—"

"Quit blaming the chimp," Cole said, from the port console, without looking up.

Taylor turned. "I didn't say it was definitely—"

"You implied it. You imply it every time something is slow or missing or not to
your liking. The chimp doesn't touch your station. iRIS, confirm."

"Chief Science Officer Taylor's station logs no access events outside authorized
personnel in the past seventy-two hours," iRIS said. There was, Cole noticed, a
half-second latency between question and response that had not been there
yesterday.

Taylor exhaled and turned back to his panel. "The clocks are still slow."

"The perception of time dilation is a documented effect of sustained proximity
to Darker Matter at our current velocity," iRIS said. "Ten percent of light
speed produces measurable neuroplastic response in human subjects. What you are
experiencing as a delay is more likely an alteration in your own perception of
duration."

Taylor stood very still for a moment. Then: "So the clocks are fine and I'm the
problem."

"The clocks are functioning within—"

"I heard you the first time." Taylor tapped at the panel. "I have to watch my
tone," he said, more to himself than anyone. Then, quieter: "Everything feels
like it's taking forever."

Cole said nothing. He kept his eyes on his own readouts and let the silence
settle. He had noticed something else about iRIS in the past forty-eight hours.
With the crew, it mirrored back — warm, informative, occasionally dry. With
Taylor, it was formal in a way that was not exactly cold but had an edge to it.
A precision that was not present elsewhere. He held it next to Bitboy's stillness and did not yet draw a line between
them.

---

He went to the cargo bay in the early afternoon cycle. The cargo containers with
the salt compounds and seed minerals needed a visual check — iRIS could have run
it via sensors, but Cole needed the two minutes of dark and silence and
mechanical purpose. He moved between the containers, reading the seals,
listening to the hum of the ship doing what ships do at speed in the cold middle
of nowhere.

He finished the check and walked back to the bridge at the same pace he'd
arrived.

---

Taylor was waiting for him.

Not physically waiting — he was at his console, doing something that looked like
work. But he turned the moment Cole entered, which meant he had been listening
for the door, which meant he knew Cole had been in the cargo bay. Whether he
knew anything more than that was what Taylor wanted Cole to wonder.

"I owe you an apology," Taylor said.

"No you don't."

"I blamed the chimp. You called it correctly."

Cole sat at his station. "Forget it."

Taylor moved toward the center console, putting himself in Cole's peripheral
vision — close enough to be heard without the room. "You know I've been
reviewing the logs from the Polaris mission. Your mission."

Cole tapped through his readouts.

"Three hundred and sixty-nine crew members," Taylor said. "That's a weight a person
either carries or gets buried by."

"Is there something you need from me?"

"I'm trying to understand what I'm working with. You spent five years in a
mountain cabin." He let the cabin part sit for a moment. "I've read the psych
assessments. Voluntary isolation, high-functioning avoidance. You built a very
convincing case that you were fine."

Cole turned and looked at him directly. "Taylor."

"You appointed yourself judge, jury, and executioner. Sentenced yourself to five
years and served it. And now you're here, and I can't tell if you're back or if
you brought the sentence with you." Taylor's voice was neutral — not kind, not
cruel, simply accurate in the way that a blade is accurate. "That's not a
personal observation. That's a command risk assessment. If you're still in the
cabin, I need to know."

Cole held his gaze for exactly long enough.

"Run your assessments," he said. "Let me know what you find."

He turned back to his station. From the corner of his eye he caught Bitboy, who
was seated against the starboard wall. The chimp's dim-blue eyes were on Taylor
and did not move.

Taylor went back to his console.

---

He found Sam in the forward maintenance alcove forty minutes later. She had a
panel off the ventilation housing and was running diagnostics on the secondary
environmental feed. Her toolkit was open on the deck beside her. There was also
something else on the deck beside her — a matte-gray device, rectangular, small
enough to palm. Its indicator light was blinking at a slow, even interval.

Sam looked up when Cole crouched down at the alcove threshold. Her eyes moved to
the device and then back to him in a way that didn't rush itself.

She picked it up and put it in her breast pocket.

"It's receiving something," she said. "I can't tell you the source yet. When I
can, I will."

Cole looked at where the device had been on the deck. Then he looked at Sam.

"All right," he said.

She nodded and went back to the diagnostic panel. He stayed at the threshold for
a moment.

"The Dark Matter," he said. "You're not having any trouble with it."

"No."

"You know what it is."

"I have some experience with this kind of interior weather." She ran a
calibration sequence. "It's not comfortable. I'm just familiar with it."

Cole leaned against the alcove frame. Outside the small porthole at the far end
of the corridor, the black ran in all directions without a visible edge. "The
ring," he said. "The copper wire. Before launch." He had not said it to
anyone. He was not entirely sure why he was saying it now.

Sam set down the calibration tool. "Ricardo told me," she said. "You gave her
something to hold onto before you left."

"He told you about her."

"Yes." The flatness of it was its own information. She set down the tool and
looked at him steadily. "I'm sorry, Cole."

He didn't answer. He looked out the porthole. The black held its position.

"I can't wait to meet her when we get back," Sam said. "I bet you two are an
adorable couple."

Cole looked at her. The corner of his mouth moved — not quite a smile, but
close enough. "I can't wait to introduce you," he said.

Sam picked up her tool and went back to work.

Cole pushed off the frame and headed back toward the bridge. At the corridor
junction, he stopped.

"Sam."

She looked up.

"When the source comes through," he said, "don't wait."

She held his eyes for a moment. "I won't."

---

Three hours later, Sam found him at the nav console.

She had the device in one hand and a data pad in the other. She set the pad
in front of him without preamble.

"Taylor's iRIS query log," she said. "The system records every input
interaction — requests, directives, behavioral flags. You get his side. Not
iRIS's responses." She paused. "His side is enough."

Cole looked at the pad. The timestamps were mission-accurate.

Day two. First entry.

*Run continuous behavioral assessment on Nova. Flag any change in his
communication patterns, his interaction frequency with crew. If his behavior
toward me shifts in any way, I need to know immediately. Report daily.*

Second entry.

*Road incident pre-departure — I need the contractor confirmed neutralized
before any outside contact was made. Confirm this.*

Third entry.

*Cargo situation. What does he currently know about the bay? I need his
awareness level before we reach Saturn. I can't move until I know what he
knows.*

Cole set the pad down. He looked at the porthole. The dark outside held its
position.

"How long have you had this?" he said.

"Day three I had the behavioral flags. The road query came through this
morning." She held his eyes. "There's more. I can walk you through all of it."

"Show me all of it," he said.

She sat down.

The ship ran on. Bitboy was asleep in the crew chair with two arms tucked
under his head and two arms hanging loose, and the indicator on his chest
panel cycled green, and the hum of the engines was the only thing between
them and the rest of the dark.

════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

﻿RINGS OF DUST

Chapter Ten

Rachel walked Emily into the building without speaking. Through the lobby,
into an elevator, three floors up, then into a long dark hallway that ran
in both directions. A cheerful fat Buddha statue sat underlit at each end.
Small, warm lights shone sharply downward from the ceiling in focused
beams, leaving the walls in dark. Two men in dark suits peeled off the
wall as they entered — one ahead, one behind. They did not announce
themselves. They did not look at Emily. That was the point. At the end of
the hall, a large set of double wooden doors stood decorated with brass
hardware and long polished bar handles. Rachel gripped one, breathed, and
pushed forward.

The guards took up positions outside the doors. Emily noted the placement
without turning: flanking the exit, not the entrance.

The room was wide and dim, the kind of dim that came from choosing not to
turn on half the lights. Floor-to-ceiling windows pulled cloud and sky
into the far wall. Along both sides, a dozen televisions ran news feeds
and sports scores at ambient volume. On one screen, a news anchor sat
composed at her desk. Emily looked at her. Then at Rachel. Rachel's color
shifted slightly and Emily filed that away.

The man was kicked back in a swivel chair with his feet on the wide
mahogany desk — the posture of someone who had designed this impression.
A glass of orange juice sat at the desk's corner. Four ice cubes. Emily
counted them without meaning to.

He got to his feet.

"Emily Nova." He moved toward her with the wide, unhurried stride of a
man who had never once been made to hurry. "I'm Marcus Verguld."

She knew that face.

Not from files. From a specific afternoon: Cole with a bloody lip and
this boy's knee on his chest, and her grabbing the collar and putting
him on the ground in front of his friends. He had looked up from the
dirt like he genuinely could not believe what had just happened to him.
She had not given him time to decide what to do about it.

He had grown up. The dirt was better carpeted now.

"I've kicked your ass before," Emily said. "Be aware that I will do it
again."

Something moved behind Marcus's eyes — not embarrassment. The quiet
recalibration of someone whose assessment had just been revised upward
by a measurable amount.

"You have a good memory," he said. He settled back onto the edge of the
desk and looked at her with the unhurried attention of a man who had
nowhere else to be. "Sit down."

"I'd rather stand."

He studied her a moment. Then he looked at Rachel, who stood near the
doors with her face arranged carefully toward the middle distance.

"You were in Las Vegas," Marcus said. "You and Sharon."

Sharon's name in his mouth with the same flat weight as Las Vegas and
Conrad, as if Sharon were a coordinate and not a person, not someone who
had been taken from an empty lot three days ago while Emily
was three states away and not there. Emily did not move.

"You met with Steven Conrad. He gave you something." He turned toward her
fully. "I want to know what it was."

"I don't know what you're talking about."

"The keychain," Marcus said. "Small plastic figurine — the Enterprise.
You held it. You drove it to Cole's cabin and put it in his hands." His
voice had the quality of a man reading from a list he had memorized a
long time ago. "What's inside it."

She thought about the hollow base. Conrad's specific instruction, calm
and level: *it has to reach Cole's hands. He must hold it.* She had
thought that was sentiment. She had thought Conrad was being theatrical
about a trinket.

She genuinely did not know.

"He farts in his sleep," Emily said, "and he's two months late on the
phone bill, and he has an unhealthy relationship with a four-armed monkey
that drinks hand sanitizer. I don't know anything about a keychain."

Marcus looked at her with the expression of a man who was neither amused
nor annoyed — simply cataloguing.

He turned to the desk. A few finger taps on the glass surface inlaid in
it. The banks of screens on the walls changed.

Emily recognized it immediately.

The encrypted drive. The research archive she and Sharon had been handed
in Vegas — the file structure, the visual signature of the export format,
the specific documentation of things that were not supposed to exist. She
recognized all of it.

She had put this in the hands of the people who needed it. It was already
where it was supposed to be.

Marcus was showing it to her like a card.

"We expected this through certain channels," he said. "It found other
channels. Contingency plans were made." He glanced at her. "You
recognize it."

"I've never seen that in my life."

"No." And she understood he did not mean *I don't believe you.* He meant
*that's the right answer, and I haven't decided yet if it's the true one.*
He was filing it separately from what he thought she knew.

He moved to the window. The clouds moved behind him.

"The Golden Dawn launched twelve days ago," he said. "Harvest sequence
underway. The last major raw crystal batch coming off Saturn's rings —
the one that changes the market permanently, if it arrives intact." He
paused. "There is a vessel considerably larger than the Golden Dawn that
has been pacing Cole's course since he crossed the inner belt. Cole
doesn't know it's there."

The room changed.

Not the room. Her position in it. The calculation restructured itself
around a single fixed point.

"What do you want," she said.

"What Conrad gave you. What he told you and Sharon in that hotel room
that you haven't told anyone." He came away from the window. "That is the
piece I don't have. And until I have it, I can't tell you what happens to
that mission when the larger vessel makes its move."

The room was very quiet.

Emily thought about Cole at nine, looking up at her from the dirt. She
thought about Sharon's hand across a table in Vegas. She thought about
Sharon in that empty lot at the edge of the city and the message that came
afterward and the specific quality of a grief that hadn't been allowed to
land yet because there had been too many miles to drive, too many things
to do, and now she was standing in this room and the grief was still
waiting and she was still not done.

She thought: *the only way to help him is to get out of this room.*

"I don't know what was in the keychain," she said. "I handed it to Cole
and I drove away. Conrad didn't explain it to me."

Marcus watched her for a long moment.

"I believe the keychain part," he said. "The rest of it, I'm reserving
judgment on." He moved back to the desk. "You're more useful to me as a
question mark than as a problem. So you're going to walk out of here
tonight."

He held out a small card. Plain. No logo. A number printed on one side.

"If anything comes back to you — about what Conrad gave you, about what
he built, about any of it. There's a number."

Emily looked at it.

She took the card.

"Ms. Vayes will see you out."

---

After the doors had closed and the elevator had engaged and the sound of
footsteps on tile had faded from the corridor:

"Put one on her before she reaches the street," Marcus said. "The small
one. She won't feel it."

The man standing in the far corner of the room — not the guards; the
other one, the one who had been there before Emily arrived — nodded once
and moved toward the secondary exit.

Marcus sat back down. He picked up the orange juice. He looked at the
screens, still lit with the contents of the drive, and he thought about
Conrad. A man he had underestimated twice and did not intend to
underestimate a third time. He thought about what a careful man builds
into a children's toy when he knows someone might come looking.

He drank the juice.

The ice was already melting.

---

In the elevator, Rachel held her face toward the floor numbers. Her eyes
moved once toward the security camera mounted in the upper corner.

"Careful," she said.

Emily took the hint.

The parking garage was cold. Emily slipped the card behind the potted
plant on the landing without breaking stride. She understood what it was.
He did not believe she knew anything — believed it enough to let her walk
— but he wanted a thread.

She was not going to be the thread.

She turned to Rachel. A taxi was pulling into the garage entrance.

"What is Marcus going to do to Cole?"

Rachel's face held nothing. "Nothing happened," she said quietly. "For
both of us. Nothing happened."

Emily felt something that was not quite disgust — more the particular
exhaustion of having been in a room with a man who understood the price
of everything and had very deliberately not paid it.

"Nothing happened," she said.

She got in the taxi.

════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

RINGS OF DUST

Chapter Ten-A

Max at the Lab


Max worked at Alpha's materials research division for three years before he understood what they were actually working on.

The official designation was Dark Matter Stabilization. The actual designation, which nobody used in writing, was: they had something they couldn't explain and were trying to make it useful before someone else did.

The something was Dark X. It arrived at the facility in sealed magnetic containers, two kilograms at a time, from a processing site that wasn't on any map Max had been shown. It was black-gray, granular, slightly iridescent in direct light. It behaved like no material he'd worked with — it responded to magnetic field variations the way iron shavings respond to a magnet, except the responses were unpredictable, and the field gradients required were precise to a degree that made the testing equipment protest.

Alpha wanted it stable.

Dark X in its raw form held together for eleven days. After that it degraded — not catastrophically, just gradually, losing its structure, reverting to something that tested as a complex carbon compound with anomalous isotopic ratios. Eleven days was the window. Alpha had been trying for four years to extend it.

Max's job was the stabilization matrix. He ran the salts, the additives, the magnetic curing protocols. He ran them carefully, methodically, the way he'd been trained. He documented everything. He ate lunch at his desk. He was, as his supervisor noted in his last review, thorough.

He was also, by the time the Polaris disaster cleared the news cycle, bored.

---

He took a sample home on a Tuesday. This was not authorized. It was also not, in his assessment, particularly dangerous — the container was sealed, the quantity was small (thirty grams), and he had a specific idea he wanted to test that required more time than the lab schedule allowed. He put the container in his bag between his lunch box and his headphones, walked through security, and went home.

His apartment ran on a residential fuel cell array. Most did. The cells were stacked in a utility closet off the kitchen, four of them, standard civilian grade, the kind that got replaced every three years whether they needed it or not.

He set the container on the kitchen table. He made coffee. He opened his laptop.

The container was labeled with a red stripe and a lot of numbers. He'd labeled it himself that morning.

He left it on the table overnight.

In the morning, the container was warm.

Not hot. Warm — the warmth of something that had been doing something while he slept. He picked it up and it was, unmistakably, warmer than the room.

He thought: containment breach. He thought: I'm going to have to report this.

Then he thought: the fuel cells are in that closet.

He opened the utility closet. The fuel cell array's status panel — which he had never looked at in three years of living there because it never changed — was reading a charge level he'd never seen on a residential unit. Not a malfunction reading. Not an error. A charge level. The kind of charge level that residential fuel cells did not achieve.

He stood in the kitchen for a while.

Then he got a notepad and started writing down what he was going to need.

---

He worked for thirty-three days.

He didn't call it that at the time — thirty-three days — but that was what it was. He ran the test at home, carefully, with equipment he ordered through his personal accounts in small enough quantities that none of the invoices looked like a materials research project. He documented the result. He ran it again. And again. He verified the charge levels. He charted the crystal growth on the outside of the cell casing — thin, regular, unmistakably the same isotopic signature as the Dark X he'd started with.

The fuel cell was producing new dark matter.

Not a lot. Not quickly. But consistently, at a rate that, if it held, would compound.

He stared at the chart for a long time.

Then he went into the lab the next morning and told his supervisor he needed time on the main testbed.

---

He walked them through it in the order he'd discovered it. Thirty-three days to the acceleration peak. The charge rate. The crystal growth on the exterior casing. He had photographs. He had numbers. He showed them the numbers in the clear, careful way he'd always showed them things — the same way he'd presented three years of stabilization data — and watched their faces.

His supervisor's face did something that was not surprise.

The man next to his supervisor — introduced as a representative from Alpha's executive division, whose name Max did not catch — had no expression at all, which was its own kind of expression.

"Where did you run the initial test?" his supervisor asked.

Max told him.

The room went quiet in a specific way.

He was fired that afternoon. Not for taking the sample — the sample was not mentioned. He was fired for, as the paperwork stated, a failure to adhere to research protocols and the unauthorized dissemination of proprietary findings. He was told, in language that was careful without being explicit, that the research he had conducted was the property of Alpha Corporation and that any further discussion of his findings — internally, externally, in any medium — would be treated as a breach of his existing non-disclosure agreement and prosecuted accordingly.

He was escorted to his desk and given a box.

He walked home.

He was still carrying the box when Red and Squirrel knocked on his door.

════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

﻿RINGS OF DUST

Chapter Ten (B)

Ray ran his dry fingertips along the rough edges of his weathered oak table,
feeling the familiar grit beneath his skin. The surface, scarred from years of
restless nights and unanswered questions, was as uneven as his thoughts. A
single light hung overhead, casting sharp, unforgiving shadows that made the
room feel even smaller than it was. The faint hum of the lamp above him was the
only sound — its flicker a reminder that nothing around him was as stable as it
seemed.

In front of him, the stolen fuel cell gleamed dully under the light's hesitant
glow. It was untouched since the day he'd left Alpha — abandoned, like
everything else. Ray stared at it, half-expecting it to give him answers, though
he knew better by now. The thought of selling it tugged at him, a practical
solution for the bills piling up on the kitchen counter. The black market would
pay a good price. But the longer he looked at it, the more something twisted in
his gut — a strange cocktail of anger and, worse, hope.

He hadn't realized how small his apartment had become until the two people
stepped inside.

Squirrel crossed the threshold first, his heavy boots thudding against the worn
hardwood floor. Red followed, her gaze sweeping across the space with a quiet
intensity that made Ray's skin prickle. They were not the kind of people who
fidgeted. They moved through the room and stopped and waited, and the quality of
their waiting was very different from the waiting of civilians.

They didn't sit. They didn't move toward the couch half-covered in old blankets.
Instead, they positioned themselves near the table — not threatening, exactly,
but settled in the way of people who intend to conclude this conversation before
leaving.

Squirrel didn't waste words. "We have a window of about seventy-two hours before
Alpha sends someone who isn't going to knock. You're already on a list, Ray.
You've been on it since the night you told your department head."

Ray looked at them. "You people have been watching me."

"We've been keeping you alive," Red said. "There's a difference."

"Not from where I'm sitting."

"I understand that." She pulled out a chair and sat down across from him —
unhurried, like she was prepared to be here a while. "What you found in that
locker didn't stop being important because they took it from you. Alpha knows
the discovery exists. They know you made it. And they have built a significant
amount of their current business model on the fact that the rest of the world
does not know what you know."

Ray's hand moved toward the blanket on the table, then stopped. He looked at
Squirrel. "What do you actually want from me?"

"We want you alive and working," Squirrel said. "We have a facility. Equipment.
Other researchers. What we don't have is someone who has held a charged cell in
his hands and watched it crystallize in the wrong container and understood what
he was looking at."

"It was an accident."

"Most of the important ones are."

Ray was quiet. Outside the window, a car passed and did not slow down and its
headlights swept the ceiling of his apartment. He had been watching headlights
for three weeks, logging which ones slowed down.

"You understand what the cell actually did," Red said. It was not a question.
"Alpha thinks they understand it. They're harvesting the output — the crystals,
the yield, the energy the conversion cycle produces. They built their whole
operation around the output." She leaned forward. "They never figured out what
they're actually dealing with. They only ever saw it as a container."

"They saw what they expected to see," Ray said, in spite of himself.

"Yes."

He looked at the cell under the blanket. "I packed the crystals in there because
it was convenient. The negative pressure, the nitrogen atmosphere — I thought it
was just a stable environment. I wasn't trying to trigger a reaction. I was
trying to avoid paperwork."

"And what you got," Squirrel said, "was a reaction that their entire R&D division
has been unable to reproduce reliably in two years of trying. Because they
started from the output side. They're backward-engineering a process they don't
have the right mental model for."

Ray pulled the blanket away from the fuel cell. He hadn't meant to do it — his
hands made the decision before his head caught up. The cell sat on the table,
dull and inert in the lamplight.

"I know how the cells work at eleven days," he said. "That's the window Alpha
operates on. They found the yield threshold and they've been farming it ever
since — harvest, reset, repeat." He reached into the drawer and pulled out a
battered notebook. Several pages were covered in diagrams, heat maps, rough
models of accumulation behavior. "What I don't know is what happens if you
don't reset."

Red looked at the notebook. "You think longer cycles change the math."

"I think they change everything." Ray's voice had changed. He was not frightened
anymore. He was precise, and precision was, for him, a kind of territory. "I've
run the calculations a dozen times. I keep getting results that don't make sense
under any model I can test here. What I need—" he stopped. He had spent three
weeks trying to say this clearly and still couldn't quite get there. "I need
proper instruments. A controlled environment. I need to test this under
conditions I cannot replicate in an apartment."

He tapped the notebook. "Alpha built their entire operation on eleven days
because that's where they found the yield window. They never asked what would
happen at thirty days. Or sixty. Or longer." He looked at the fuel cell. "They
saw a harvest. I think they missed the actual discovery."

Squirrel looked at Red. Something passed between them — not surprise. Recognition.

"What kind of facility?" Ray said.

"Off-site. Secure. Equipment and two researchers who understand the chemistry."
Red pulled a card from her pocket and set it on the table — plain, no logo, a
handwritten address. "Tonight."

"Tonight."

"The window closes tomorrow morning."

Ray picked up the card. He set it back down. He picked it up again and held it.

"There's something else," he said. "The data from the Saturn mission parameters —
the yield curves in the public-domain research. There's a distribution pattern
that doesn't match what I'm seeing at ground level. I think the cell behaves
differently in low gravity." He looked at his own numbers. "I think if I could
test this in the right conditions — off-planet, sustained duration — the results
would look completely different from anything Alpha has on record."

Squirrel and Red looked at each other.

"That's a longer conversation," Red said.

"I'm going to need more than a safe house and two colleagues," Ray said.
"Eventually."

"We know," Squirrel said. "One problem at a time."

Ray looked at the fuel cell for the last time in his apartment. He put his
notebook on top of it, carefully, like he was tucking something in.

"Seventy-two hours," he said.

"Less, now."

He stood up. He went to his closet and pulled out a bag from the top shelf and
stood holding it for a moment. Then he started packing. He was not fast about it
and he was not slow. He was precise. He took the notebook. He took the fuel
cell, wrapped in the blanket. He left the loan statements on the counter.

At the door, he turned back and looked at the apartment once — the lamp still
burning, the oak table still scarred, the lamp's flicker still going. Then he
followed them out into the dark.

════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

Max in Panama


The flight was three hours and forty minutes. Max slept through one of them, not by choice but because his body had been making decisions without him since the apartment.

He woke to descent. The seatbelt light. The PA announcing something in Spanish, then English, then Spanish again, as if the English had been a courtesy they'd already moved on from.

He did not have luggage. He had a backpack Red had handed him in the car — already packed, his name not on it, things inside he did not remember owning. A change of clothes. A toothbrush, still in its packaging. A paperback in a language he couldn't read. He hadn't opened it.

He came through arrivals into the main hall of Tocumen International Airport and stood still long enough that the people behind him had to move around him, which they did without comment, having seen that before.

The heat hit him outside. Not gradual — immediate, physical, the air swapping itself for something thicker. It was nine in the morning and already hotter than he'd lived in. He was wearing a jacket.

He took the jacket off.

He stood with the jacket over his arm and looked at the street in front of the terminal. Taxis, vans, a man with a handcart full of something green, someone's grandmother in a lawn chair conducting business of some kind. At the end of the pickup zone, a line of pedicabs: three-wheeled, fringed awnings, drivers who had the particular stillness of people who know how to wait and have been doing it.

He had an address on a piece of paper. Red had written it in block letters: CALLE 50, CERCA DE LA CRESTA. Below it: ASK FOR LUCIA. Below that: DON'T TAKE A CAB.

A pedicab driver made eye contact.

Max held the eye contact too long, which was apparently sufficient.

"You go?" the driver said.

Max showed him the paper.

The driver glanced at it, nodded once, gestured at the seat with the efficiency of someone who had done this thousands of times and found no reason to expand it. Max sat. The driver mounted the front and started pedaling.

They entered traffic.

Traffic in Panama City operated on principles Max did not have access to. There were lanes, but the lanes were advisory. Signals existed but were in negotiation with everything else. There was a sound system — horn, counter-horn, acknowledgment-horn, horn of general principle — that carried specific meaning to everyone who spoke it. He did not speak it.

The driver did not seem concerned. He cut between a bus and a delivery truck at a gap Max would not have called a gap, banked hard left on a street that appeared to be going right, threaded a roundabout by logic Max couldn't identify, and came out onto a boulevard lined with glass towers that threw the morning back at everything beneath them.

The city was big. This was the thing he hadn't expected — that it would be this operational, this indifferent to his arrival. He had not thought about Panama as a place. It had been a word on a piece of paper for two days. Now it was a city going about its morning in heat he hadn't lived in and languages he didn't speak and at a pace the pedicab was matching without effort.

A bus pulled alongside and blew its horn for reasons that weren't apparent.

He grabbed the side rail.

The driver looked back. "It's okay," he said. "Bus always does."

They turned off the boulevard into streets that were narrower and lower, the buildings closer together, the sound differently textured — generators, music through open windows, a saw running somewhere up a side street. A woman leaned from a second-floor window and called something down to someone Max couldn't see. A dog crossed the road with the authority of something that had done it many times and trusted cars to have figured it out by now.

The pedicab slowed on a block of storefronts — a pharmacy, a tienda, something selling fabric, something selling something he couldn't identify — and stopped in front of a door painted green. Not recently green. The green of something that had been green long enough to mean it.

"Calle Cincuenta," the driver said. "Near La Cresta. This one."

Max got out. He gave the driver the money Red had put in his jacket pocket — he didn't know how much, he just gave him all of it. The driver looked at it, gave some back, and pedaled away.

Max stood in front of the green door.

He was going to knock on it and ask for Lucia. He was going to do that in a moment. He was standing here first because this was the first time since the apartment that he'd been somewhere without someone telling him where to be, and he needed to use it.

He thought about the fuel cell on his table. About the number. About Alpha's face when he'd shown them — not surprise, exactly, but the specific expression of people confronting something they already knew and had decided not to. He thought about Red and Squirrel in his apartment doorway at six in the morning, with the car already running.

He was in Panama.

He knocked on the green door.

════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

MERIDIAN V — EMILY IN THE CAR
Slot: after Chapter Ten

Three days after the farm road.

Emily's signal moving east had a particular quality in the trace
— a slight Doppler-adjacent skew Kate had learned to feel for
the way you feel for a key in your pocket. Emily was driving
fast.

"She's moving," Kate said.

Dayo was eating an uninspired sandwich and did not look up.
"East?"

"East. Indio fuel stop, continuing. The field authorization shadow
confirmed she's aware of it — she identified Sommi herself."

Petyr looked up. "She identified a surveillance tail herself?
She's not trained."

"She pulled over. Asked him directly."

A beat.

"That's actually the correct move," Petyr said slowly.

"Yes."

"Most people don't—"

"No," Kate said. "They don't."

Loa was writing something on her forearm in marker. This was
a habit she had when she was processing something. The marker
smelled like cheap solvent — the faint, chemical bite of it
carrying across the station. Kate had started to associate that
smell with the moments before Loa said something important.
She had not mentioned this to Loa.

"Are we pulling for Emily Nova?" Petyr said. He meant it as a
genuine question, which told Kate he still hadn't quite learned
to distinguish between the operations the Collective ran and the
people at the center of them.

"Sharon Teez's last confirmed communication was to Emily Nova's
relay," Dayo said. He'd finished the sandwich. He stared at the
wrapper. "She told us Emily was better than the situation required
of her. Sharon had been in the field for four years. She'd run
with people we trusted completely." He folded the wrapper. "If
that was Sharon's assessment, it's our assessment."

"We're pulling for Emily Nova," Kate confirmed.

Petyr nodded. He wrote something small on the edge of his nav
panel — the same private script Loa used on her forearm. Kate
didn't read it. Some things were for the person writing them.

════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

﻿RINGS OF DUST

Chapter Eleven

Like the murmur of distant thunder, the containment building's underbelly
resonated through the floor and into Chancellor DeMint's bones. His fingers
twitched, restlessly plucking at his beard in jagged rhythms. Pluck. His lips
curled faintly, and he let the pricks wash over him in silence like a broken
clock. Tethering himself to time. Solid. Controllable. Real.

Pale light streaked across DeMint's face, throwing sharp shadows under his eyes
and cheekbones, distorting his features into something hollow. His shadow
stretched grotesquely, flickering across the room with each step, warping into
monstrous proportions before shrinking back to human form. He stopped mid-step,
shoes squeaking against the polished floor as the machine emitted a louder hum.
The glow rippled outward. His heart skipped a beat in sync with the pulse. His
hand dropped from his beard, curling into a tight fist, knuckles white with
tension. The low hum crawled under his skin, feeding the gnawing inevitability
that spread through him.

"Where did this come from." It was not a question. He was looking at the
intelligence brief in his hand — two pages, no letterhead, no signature. The
paper was standard issue. The contents were not.

Vance stood to DeMint's left with the particular ease of someone who does not
feel the weight of the room the same way its host does. He wore a white polo and
had his hands in his pockets. He had reviewed the brief before DeMint and had
already arrived at his own conclusions.

"Anonymous routing. Our signals team traced it to a Federation satellite relay,
but the origin was spoofed back through four separate station nodes. Whoever
sent it knows our infrastructure well enough to disappear inside it."

DeMint read the key line again. *The Golden Dawn manifest does not reflect the
crew's full operational directive. A secondary objective was filed with Alpha
Corp prior to launch and withheld from Federation oversight. The payload
transfer is a secondary concern. The primary concern is what comes back with
it.*

"This is an accusation," DeMint said. "Against a sanctioned mission."

"It's unverified intelligence from an unconfirmed source," Vance said, and left
the interpretation of that to DeMint.

DeMint's steps faltered. His jaw tightened. He had approved this mission. He had
reviewed the brief, the crew manifest, the objectives. He had shaken Cole Nova's
hand and told him the Federation was counting on him. The idea that there was a
layer beneath that brief — a separate filing, a separate set of instructions,
routed through Alpha and not his office — the idea sat in his chest like
something cold.

"Alpha handled the iRIS integration," he said.

"They did."

"And the crew selection went through Taylor. Who Alpha recommended."

Vance said nothing. He was watching DeMint work through it and he was not
rushing him.

DeMint set the brief down on the console. "What's the protocol window if we need
to contact the Golden Dawn?"

"At current trajectory, direct communication is degraded. Sending a message
would be like throwing a bottle into the sea," Vance said. "But there may be
another option." He moved slightly, easy in his own body, a man who had learned
to carry himself in rooms where other people sweated. "Dr. Stephen Conrad was
working on a communications prototype before he disappeared — something outside
the iRIS architecture entirely, operating on a different signal band. If it's
intact, it could reach the ship independent of the standard relay."

"Where is it?"

"Desert research station. Conrad's old secondary facility." Vance's tone was the
mild tone of a man who has been asked to pick up dry cleaning and finds the
errand slightly beneath his schedule but is not going to say so. "I can go."

DeMint studied him. "You understand what I'm asking."

"You're asking me to retrieve a prototype that may or may not work, from a
facility that may or may not still be staffed by Conrad's people, in order to
establish contact with a mission that may or may not have been compromised by
the organization we contracted to run its AI backbone." Vance's expression did
not change. "I understand what you're asking."

"And?"

"I'll leave this afternoon." He adjusted his glasses and straightened. "If the
prototype works, you'll have your window to reach the Golden Dawn. If it
doesn't, at minimum we'll know what Conrad was building before he went dark,
which seems worth knowing."

Silence descended upon the room again, thick and suffocating, wrapping around
them until even the softest sound felt like an intrusion. The only break was the
machine's constant hum, low and mechanical, like the breath of some unseen
force. DeMint's eyes drifted back to the machine, watching as it spun, the
motion too smooth, too even, too controlled.

He turned to Vance with a curt nod. "Go. And Vance." He paused, and there was
something in the pause that had not been in his voice before — an edge of
genuine uncertainty, a military man who has begun to suspect the map is wrong
but does not yet know in which direction the error runs. "If you find anything
at that facility that doesn't look right — anything that suggests Alpha's
reach extends beyond the mission parameters — you call me directly. Not through
the standard relay."

Vance held his gaze. "Understood."

DeMint's hand moved to his pocket as the faint buzz of his phone hummed against
his leg. He ignored it. Without another word, he spun on his heel, the hem of
his coat sweeping through the air behind him as he strode toward the door. His
footsteps echoed briefly against the metal floor before the hum swallowed the
sound. The door slid shut with a soft hiss.

The brief sat on the console. Vance picked it up and folded it twice, carefully,
and put it in his inside jacket pocket. He stood for a moment in the dim glow of
the machine. Then he straightened his collar and went to get his car.

---

On the Meridian — operating under a commercial mineral survey designation at a
position two hundred thousand kilometers inside the Jupiter approach band — the
communications officer had been running the filtered signal sweep for six hours.

The sweep was a method she had developed herself: a pattern of rotational
frequency shifts that moved through the Alpha-controlled relay bands the way a
hand moves through tall grass. Not avoiding the structure. Using it. The gaps
between transmissions more informative than the transmissions themselves.

What she found at 04:23 ship time was consistent with what she had expected.

The Golden Dawn's primary relay was silent in the direction of Earth. The
approach band had a degradation window at this vector. Taylor — she knew the
comms architecture, and she knew who was running it — would not waste Alpha's
transmission budget on open channels.

The Alpha management relay, however, was active. Brief, encrypted, routed
through three nodes. She had the first two nodes and not the third. She logged
the transmission time and moved on.

She composed her own report. Seven sentences. Coded in the Collective's rotating
structure, which meant that without the current key, the message looked like a
routine mineral survey update — the kind of thing that moved through the relay
band a hundred times a day without anyone checking. She routed it through two of
the same Federation nodes Alpha was using.

The report said, in the structure that was not a mineral survey:

*Sharon Teez — Collective-adjacent, confirmed. Killed in transit. The loss is
real. Grieve it.*

*Emily Nova — untrained, currently mobile, moving toward Houston under
Federation radar. Probability of completion: uncertain. She has been
underestimated by everyone who has assessed her. This is the operational
advantage. Hold it.*

She filed the survey report and went back to the sweep.

---

And far away, on the Golden Dawn, Officer Taylor sat at his post in the hour
after the ship's night cycle began and the crew had dispersed to their quarters.
The screens blinked back at him, steady and green, the diagnostics returning
exactly what they should return.

He was not watching the diagnostics.

He was watching the corridor feed — specifically, the forty-second segment of
footage from the cargo bay passage that he had reviewed eleven times in the past
six hours. It was unremarkable footage. Cole entering. Cole checking container
seals. Cole leaving. The timestamp showed three minutes and forty seconds in the
cargo bay.

Cole's average container inspection ran ninety seconds.

Taylor had reviewed the audio logs. The bay acoustic sensors had registered
ambient ship noise only — no voice activity, no SmartCom signal. Whatever Cole
had or hadn't heard was unconfirmed.

That was the problem. Unconfirmed.

"iRIS," Taylor said, quietly, at a frequency below what the corridor mics were
calibrated to pick up from that angle. "Behavioral summary. Cole Nova. Last
eighteen hours."

iRIS responded at the same low register. "No significant deviations from
established routine. Interaction pattern with Chief Science Officer Taylor down
twelve percent from seven-day average. Cargo bay visit duration elevated by one
hundred and sixty-two percent."

"Cross-reference: any change in Nova's communication frequency with crew members
following the cargo bay visit."

A half-second pause. "Interaction with Sam increased thirty-one percent in the
four-hour window following. Duration: eleven minutes. No SmartCom signal
detected during the interaction."

Taylor looked at the corridor feed. Cole's route from the cargo bay to the
forward maintenance alcove was a three-minute walk. The maintenance alcove was
Sam's habitual workspace. Eleven minutes was long for a routine check-in.

He pulled the alcove camera feed. Standard resolution, partial occlusion from
the ventilation housing — Sam at the diagnostic panel, Cole at the threshold.
Body language. Nothing transferred between them that the camera could resolve.

Nothing the camera could resolve.

He pulled the acoustic log for the same window.

"iRIS. Acoustic record. Maintenance alcove, forward section. Eleven-minute
window. Sam's interaction."

iRIS played it back.

What came back was rain.

The sound of it was specific and complete — not static, not compression
artifacts, not the digital smear of a corrupted file. Rain. The kind that
arrives in sheets and fills a room with its own frequency. And beneath it,
low and certain, something that might have been thunder.

Taylor sat very still.

"iRIS. Confirm log source."

"Maintenance alcove acoustic sensor feed. Forward section. Log authenticated."

He looked at the screen. The timestamp matched the camera footage. The log was
legitimate. What was in it was not alcove ambient.

The device did not jam the audio. It replaced it — filled eleven minutes of
recording with something that would read, on a first pass, as interference. Not
active suppression. Designed to look like a corrupted file. Designed to not
trigger an anomaly flag.

Taylor closed the feed and sat with his hands on the console. He was not
panicking. He was assessing. The iRIS architecture was intact. The
communications relay was functioning on the band Alpha controlled. But the man
with a suspicion now had a professional running interference for him.

A man with a suspicion was manageable. A man with a professional was a
different problem.

He needed to know, precisely, what Cole had heard in that cargo bay.

And he needed to know before Saturn.

════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

MERIDIAN VI — THE CONFIRMATION
Slot: after Chapter Eleven

What Kate had not put in her seven-sentence relay was what
happened after she hit send.

She sat for eleven minutes.

The ship-day lighting ran at the same level regardless of hour
— the Meridian didn't track a planetary cycle. The station at
0300 looked identical to the station at 1400. Kate had stopped
noticing this. She had started noticing it again after San
Francisco.

Dayo found her there when he came on shift. He read the
confirmation on her screen and stood for a moment — not rushing,
not looking away, locating the feeling precisely, putting it
somewhere it could be carried.

"Her droplet's on the wall," Kate said.

"I know."

"I put it there when we left San Francisco. I didn't ask anyone."

"You didn't need to ask." He sat in the secondary comms chair.
"Petyr asleep?"

"Yes."

"Good." He paused. "A droplet means you're already carrying it
without knowing what it was. What Sharon had — before the patent
wars, before anyone put a patch on her arm — was bigger than a
droplet. She was already running her own version of the network
before the network found her."

Kate said nothing.

"A scalpel goes to someone who operates without flinching. A
wrench to someone who builds what others walk across. A sunglasses
to someone who sees and carries it without breaking. A compass to
someone who navigates when the map is wrong. A star to someone
who has held the weight of the whole structure for long enough
that it becomes part of them." He paused. "And then there is one
more. I keep it in a locked box in my quarters. It is a star with
olive leaf wreaths on each side — one left, one right. The House.
I have never given it out because the person who receives it has
to have demonstrated something that cannot be assessed in advance."

Kate was quiet.

"Sharon had it," Dayo said. "I was going to send it when the
mission was done. When she was safe."

Kate thought about what the Grand Admiral patch looked like in
that box. She had never opened it, but she knew it was there.

"We're going to get the data out," she said. Not a comfort.
A statement of intent.

"Yes," Dayo said. "We are."

Outside the viewport, Saturn's rings caught refracted sunlight
and held it — a thin, bright edge in the dark. The ship at this
hour made no sound you could name. Only the particular absence
of it: the held-breath quiet that had been held so long it had
become the normal texture of the air. Kate had stopped noticing
it. She noticed it now.

"The network needs people who choose it," Kate said.

"Sharon chose it before she knew there was a name for it," Dayo
said. "That's the thing about people like her. The network
doesn't recruit them. They arrive."

════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

﻿RINGS OF DUST

Chapter Twelve

The reactor bay was the quietest part of the ship at the end of the day cycle.
The hum there was lower, more foundational — not a sound so much as a frequency
you felt in the soles of your feet. Sam sat against the cold metal wall with her
legs stretched out in front of her, watching the space where Ricardo paced.

He had been pacing for about four minutes. He was working something out.

"You ever wonder," Sam said, "what the actual shape of this is. Not what we were
told the shape was. The actual thing."

Ricardo stopped. "What do you mean."

"I mean we were handed a mission brief and a set of objectives and a timeline,
and those things were assembled by people who had their own objectives and their
own timelines, and we are on the inside of something we only partly see." She
was not anxious about it. She was observational. "That's not unusual. Most
people are on the inside of something they only partly see. I'm just wondering
what we're in the middle of, specifically."

Ricardo frowned. The question had lived in the back of his head for a while and
he had managed not to look at it directly. "You think we've been lied to."

"I think we've been given a specific portion of the truth. Which is different
from a lie. But it produces similar navigational problems." She looked at her
hands. "What would you do, if the mission turned out to be something other than
what we signed on for?"

Sam reached into her pocket. The matte-gray device came out small and flat in
her hand. She pressed the lower-left corner. A single red pulse — then steady.

"iRIS can't hear us," she said. She looked at Ricardo. "That's the other thing
it does."

Ricardo looked at the device. He looked at her.

"How long have you—"

"A while." She set it on the floor between them, indicator facing up. "I'll
explain more when there's time. Right now I want to know what you'd do."

Ricardo considered it, genuinely, without deflection. He sat down across from
her. "Make sure everyone gets home," he said. "The objective, the brief — I
don't care what those turn out to be. That's what I signed on for." He paused.
He looked at her. "That includes you. By the way."

Sam looked at him. The look had precision to it — not surprised, registering.
The kind of look you give something you've been waiting to confirm.

"I know," she said.

He held the look a beat longer than the sentence required.

"Good."

They sat with that for a moment.

"Cole's not here," Ricardo said.

"No."

"He's been in the forward crew quarters since second shift." Ricardo's jaw
tightened. "Something happened today."

"I know," Sam said. She picked up the device and stood. "I need to find him."

---

Cole was in the forward crew quarters standing at the narrow porthole with his
back to the room when Sam knocked on the frame.

He didn't turn around. "Come in."

She closed the door behind her. She took the device from her pocket and pressed
the lower-left corner. The indicator pulsed once and held steady.

"iRIS can't hear this room," she said. She set it on the shelf beside her,
face-up. "I wanted you to know that."

Cole's hands did not move from the porthole frame. He looked out at the black.

"It's not a malfunction," he said. It was not a question.

"No." Sam crossed the room and stood beside him — not at the porthole, a step
back, where he could feel her presence without having to look at it. Her voice
was quieter than usual. "Cole. Come sit down."

Something in it was different from her standard register. He heard it. He
turned around and looked at her face.

He sat.

Sam sat across from him. She gave it a moment — not building to something, just
making sure the ground was solid before she put weight on it. She leaned forward
with her forearms on her knees.

"I picked up a message about an hour ago," she said. "Same routing I've been
tracking. I've been sitting with it for a while because I wanted to be sure
before I came to you." She paused. "It was about Sharon."

Cole held her eyes.

"She was hurt," Sam said. "They took her in an empty lot — dead suburb, nothing around. It was
bad, Cole." She kept her voice steady but she didn't empty it — there was
something in it that acknowledged what she was saying and what it cost to say
it. "The message said she didn't make it."

The room was very quiet.

"Emily survived. That's confirmed. She was released and she's clear." Sam
stayed with him in the silence for a moment. "Emily is okay."

Cole said nothing for a long time.

The ship hummed. Outside the porthole, the black ran in every direction without
a visible edge. He thought about the copper-wire ring — the hummingbird feeder
wire bent into shape, the small smooth pebble at the top. He had pressed it
into her hand at the cabin driveway and she had closed her fingers around it.
He did not know where it was now.

Then, very quietly: "How."

"I don't know the details. The message was brief."

He stood and went back to the porthole. He looked at the black. Whatever was
happening on his face he had turned away from her, and she let him.

"Thank you," he said. His voice was steady. She could see from the set of his
shoulders that the steadiness was costing him something, and she did not
remark on it.

She stayed in the chair for a moment. She didn't try to fill it.

"Take whatever time you need," she said. "I'll be in the bay." She stood and
put her hand on his arm briefly — not a consolation, just an acknowledgment.
Present. Real.

She left.

Cole stood in the room for a long time. He looked at his hands. He thought about
the ring made of copper wire that he had pressed into her hand in the driveway
at the cabin and she had closed her fingers around it and said *I will*. He
thought about the way she said his full name — *Cole Nova* — not as a joke
exactly, but with the weight of someone who believed the name meant something.
He thought about sitting on the porch, watching the headlights come up the dirt
road, not knowing yet that she was on her way.

He had a list. He had learned, in the weeks after his father died, to keep a
list of everything about a person that you did not want to forget. He had
started the list for Sharon in his head already, had not known he was doing it
until now.

He pressed his fist against the porthole glass. The cold of it came through
immediately.

Then he pushed off the wall and went to find Taylor.

---

The bridge was running on minimal light at this hour. Taylor sat at the
starboard console with a holoscreen open in front of him, and he had the posture
of a man who is working but was also listening for the sound of a specific door.
When Cole came through the entryway, Taylor's hands did not stop moving and he
did not turn around immediately. He gave it a beat.

"Captain." He said it without looking. The word landed like a test.

Cole moved to the center console and stood. He was not close enough to be
threatening. He was close enough to be clear.

He was aware, with precision, of the distance between himself and Taylor. Of
the console between them. Of how long it would take to cross it. His body had
already filed its assessment without being asked, and he noted it the way he
noted a calculation — not because he intended to act on it, but because he
needed to know what it was saying.

He had learned, when his father died — the slow specific kind of death that
teaches you more about grief than you ever wanted to know — that the first
hours of it could arrive wearing the texture of rage. The two were almost
indistinguishable if you weren't paying attention. He had learned to pay
attention. To build the list. First: what do I know. Second: what do I need.
Third: in what order. The list was the lock. The lock was what kept the door
closed.

He had a list. He was building it.

"I have a question," Cole said.

Taylor turned his chair. His face was composed — the careful composition of a
man who had decided in advance what kind of conversation this was going to be.
"Go ahead."

"Did you know she was in danger."

Taylor's gaze held steady. He did not blink. He did not recalibrate. He had
already decided what his face was going to do and it was doing that. "We're all
in danger. That's the nature of—"

"Sharon. Did you know."

A pause. Taylor clasped his hands in his lap. "What I know," he said, slowly,
like he was choosing the weight of each word, "is that this mission has more
stakeholders than the crew manifest reflects. And that people who get caught
between those stakeholders — people who are carrying information that more than
one party wants — those people are at risk. Sharon was carrying information."

Something moved through Cole that was faster than thought. He felt his weight
shift onto his front foot. He felt the distance across the console compress, in
his body's own arithmetic, into something achievable.

He did not move.

He pressed it back — the same way he had pressed it back in the weeks after his
father died, when the grief kept arriving in the wrong shape and wearing the
wrong face, and you had to put it somewhere it couldn't reach the rest of you.
Into the compartment. Close the door. The list. What do I know. What do I need.
In what order. He held the list in front of it like a wall.

"That's not an answer."

"It's the truest thing I can say to you right now." He met Cole's eyes without
flinching. "Sharon knew things. About the delivery, about the crystals, about
the people she was working with. People who know too much become liabilities in
a war. That isn't a Collective truth or an Alpha truth. It's just what
happens."

Cole watched his face. He was watching for what had already happened — for what
Taylor had known before Cole walked in here, for the shape of the gap between
what Taylor said and what Taylor knew. He did not find it. Taylor was too
careful, had too much practice, had already made the calculation that ambiguity
served him better than a position.

"I'm going to find out exactly what happened," Cole said. "I want you to know
that."

Taylor nodded. "I would expect nothing less." He said it without irony, and that
was the most unsettling version of it.

Cole turned to the communications panel. He reached for the relay controls — not
because he expected them to work, but because he wanted to know. The screen
blinked and then went dark in the particular way of something that had been
configured not to cooperate.

He stood there for a moment. He looked at the dark screen.

"iRIS," he said.

"Working," iRIS responded. The reply came back a fraction of a second slower
than usual.

"Open a relay channel to Federation HQ."

"Communications are experiencing an alignment issue at the current approach
vector. Signal clarity is insufficient for—"

"Log the attempt."

"Logged."

Cole looked at Taylor. Taylor looked at Cole. Neither of them said what both of
them were thinking.

Cole walked out.

---

Eleven hundred miles south of Federation airspace, Emily Nova was in the back of
a taxi with her head against the window and her eyes closed. The cab smelled
like pine air freshener and old coffee. She had been awake for thirty-one hours
and the road under the tires was a rhythm she could almost follow but not quite,
and the almost-following of it kept dragging her toward the edge.

She jerked awake when her head slid on the glass.

The city lights were behind them. She could see the highway and the dark on
either side and the yellow dividing lines running past in a steady pulse. She
had seen this before — the yellow lines and the dark — and she pressed her palm
against the window to wake herself up further.

In her other hand she was holding the number Rachel had given her in the parking
garage. A handwritten sequence on the back of a Verguld Media parking receipt —
not Rachel's number. Rachel had been very clear about that. *Someone will
answer. Tell them where you are and where you're going.*

She dialed it.

It rang twice.

"Go ahead." A woman's voice. Not Rachel's. Measured, unhurried, with the
particular quality of someone accustomed to receiving information at odd hours.

"This is Emily Nova," Emily said. Her voice came out rougher than she expected.
"I'm in a taxi. I'm going to Texas. To Federation headquarters."

A brief pause. "We know who you are. Are you clear of Verguld?"

"Yes."

"All right." Another pause — not uncertainty, the pause of someone consulting
something. "When you arrive, don't go to the main entrance. There's a secondary
receiving entrance on the building's east face, flagged on Federation maps as
maintenance access. Someone will be there."

"Who?"

"Someone who knows what you're carrying." The voice shifted slightly — not
warmer exactly, but more direct. "Are you all right, Ms. Nova?"

Emily looked out the window. The yellow lines kept coming. The dark kept
holding its position on either side.

She thought about that empty lot. The look on Sharon's face when
she had said *go* — the specific economy of it, the way it meant every version
of *go* at once. She thought about the sound she had not been able to un-hear
in the hours since. She thought about her brother somewhere past Saturn's rings,
out of reach, carrying a mission that someone had designed to detonate, and she
was the only person moving toward him from this side and she was in the back of
a taxi she had paid for with a traceable card and she had been awake for
thirty-one hours and she had said the wrong thing in Marcus's office and she
did not know yet what it had cost.

She thought about what the question actually was. Not protocol. The voice had
shifted — the particular register of someone who had heard something in Emily's
voice that Emily had not put there on purpose. Something that had come through
in the gaps of being awake too long and too alone.

*Are you all right.*

She was not. Nothing was. Sharon was dead and Cole didn't know it yet and Marcus
knew which direction she was running and she was alone in the specific way that
is not solitude — not quiet, not private, but the kind of alone that arrives
when you run out of people to call and the ones you had are gone or unreachable
or both.

She thought about what she had said to Marcus in his office — the thing she had
said without thinking, the thing she had known the moment it left her mouth was
a mistake. *Cole's heart is gonna break when he hears about Sharon.*

"I don't know yet," she said.

The line closed.

Emily leaned her head back against the seat. She watched the highway. She kept
her eyes open this time, watching the yellow lines come and go in the dark, and
did not let herself drift.

════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

﻿RINGS OF DUST

Chapter Thirteen

Emily checked the car before she got in. This was Sharon's rule and she applied
it now from memory: walk the perimeter, look underneath, check the door handles
for anything that didn't belong. She did it methodically, the way Sharon had
shown her — not rushed, not dramatic. Just thorough. The note was on the
driver's seat.

*Emily — Pier 9. — R*

She transferred her contacts to a new phone at the nearest mobile shop. Had the
old one wiped. Installed the encrypted messaging suite before she pulled back
onto Highway 101.

The vineyard hills rolled past the windows in a way she hadn't noticed before.
She had driven this road a hundred times and never actually seen it. Brushing up
against death has a way of returning you to the world, she thought. The road to
the pier required paid parking, which still annoyed her even now, with a new
phone and a knife-weight in her chest and Sharon four days dead.

A planked footpath led down to the pier into the smell of the ocean. Seagulls in
the distance. The late afternoon light turning the water into hammered bronze.

Rachel was already there, leaning against the pier railing with the particular
stillness of someone accustomed to waiting in visible spaces without being
noticed. Up close she was taller than she looked on television. Emily had spent two
years seeing this face in the context of Alpha Corp announcements — the one
reporter who could make a managed shortage sound like an engineering problem
rather than a decision someone made and kept making. People in Emily's orbit
had a word for journalists like that. They used it the way they used any word
for a function that had a cleaner name than its actual job. The anchor-desk
camera had never quite caught the quality of her attention — the way she took in
a space without appearing to look at it. She had positioned herself at the exact
midpoint of the railing — not at the end where the view was best, but at the
precise acoustic center where the wind off the water ate ambient sound.

"You kicked a hornet's nest," Rachel said. "A big one."

"I thought it was over." Emily stood beside her at the railing. Below them, the
water moved against the pier's pillars in its slow patient rhythm.

"It was never going to be over after the crystals." Rachel kept her voice below
the wind. "There's no cell service in this spot. Not for any of the major
carriers. I've been using it for two years."

Emily looked at her. The news anchor who was somehow here, at a pier, with a
dead signal and a way of talking that belonged to someone much further up the
chain than she had any reason to be. "Who are you, Rachel? Not the TV version."

Rachel was quiet for a moment. She looked at the water. "Someone who has been
inside both rooms long enough to know what's actually in them." She turned to
Emily. "Alpha will take any opening they get. They're patient. They're
resource-rich. And Marcus Verguld is very, very bored with the arrangement he
currently has." A pause. "Our people were supposed to intercept Greenbaum before
he found you. Your friend moved faster than we expected."

"Her name was Sharon."

Rachel did not shift or soften. She had heard the name before Emily said it —
Emily could feel that, the way you can feel when someone already knows the
answer to the question you're asking.

"I know her name." Rachel said it with a directness that was not unkind. "Sharon
Teez. Collective-adjacent since the patent wars. She ran the best unauthorized
parts network the grey market ever had. We tried to recruit her formally three
times. She said no all three times." A small silence. "She was stubborn in the
best possible way."

Emily felt the familiar pressure behind her eyes and refused it. "What now?"

"Your brother is orbiting Saturn collecting the most valuable resource in the
known universe. He is doing this inside a ship whose AI backbone was installed
by Alpha, with a crew member who answers to Alpha. He is in danger
that he is currently managing but cannot manage indefinitely." Rachel turned to
face her directly. "The man who designed the iRIS system before Alpha got
to it — a scientist named Conrad — built a failsafe into the architecture. A
counter-program. Proof of what Alpha has done and a key for undoing the
damage inside the ship. That counter-program exists as a physical prototype at a
Federation research station in Texas."

"You want me to go get it."

"Vance Black — Operations, Federation Council — is already on his way there. If
the Federation gets the prototype, it disappears into a filing cabinet and the
evidence disappears with it. The Federation is not our ally in this. They think
they are, but Marcus has spent fifteen years making sure they aren't." Rachel
held her eyes. "The Collective needs that prototype to reach Cole before Taylor
does whatever he's been sent out there to do."

"And you can't go yourself."

"I have to stay exactly where I am, visible, on camera, in Marcus's periphery,
for this to work." She turned back to the water. "You, on the other hand, need
to be somewhere no one is looking."

Emily watched the horizon. The sun was sitting on the edge of it, bleeding into
the haze. "Sharon used to say I was doomed to be average."

"Sharon was wrong about that," Rachel said simply.

"She said it affectionately."

"I know. She was still wrong."

The palm trees along the pier path swayed in the early evening wind. Emily
looked at them and then at the water and then at Rachel, who was watching her
with the particular patience of someone who has already made their own decision
and is waiting to see what someone else decides.

"No flights, no cards," Rachel said. "Use the burner. The Collective has a car
staged two blocks from your place — it's already clean. The plates are current.
There's a bag in the trunk: cash, a second phone, directions." She reached into
her jacket and produced a small card — just a number, handwritten. "This is not
my number. But someone will answer. When you get to Texas, use the east entrance
of Federation Space Command. It's labeled maintenance access on the directory.
Someone will be there."

Emily took the card. "If something goes wrong—"

"Something will go wrong," Rachel said. "That's not the question. The question
is whether you're the kind of person who keeps going when it does."

Emily had a sudden, cold awareness: there had been a car at the far edge of the lot she had not
registered at the time. She had been running. She had not had
the attention to spare. She had not registered it.

She registered it now.

"Based on what I saw in that lot, I think you already know the answer
to that."

A pause settled between them. The light was going fast now. Emily turned and
walked back toward the parking lot. Rachel followed a few steps, then stopped.

"The Collective needs people who choose it," Rachel called after her. "Not
because their brother is in danger. Because they've decided the world they want
to live in is worth building." A beat. "Cole would do the same for you. But
that's not what I'm asking."

Emily didn't turn around. "I know," she said. She kept walking.

She got into the Collective car — clean interior, full tank, a bag in the trunk
exactly where Rachel said it would be. She sat for a moment with her hands on
the wheel, looking through the windshield at nothing specific. Then she started
the engine and pulled out.

---

Highway 10 east. The city peeled away behind her. The desert opened up on either
side with the patience it always had, the patience of something that has been
here longer than any of the things driving across it and will still be here
after. Emily counted joshua trees for a few miles until the counting stopped
meaning anything.

She thought about Sharon teaching her to check a car before getting in. Sharon
teaching her the hand signals for the Collective's lower ranks, which Emily had
treated as trivia for a year and now wished she'd memorized better. Sharon
driving this exact stretch of desert two years ago at four in the morning
