because Emily had made a call she shouldn't have made and Sharon had answered
without asking why.

The yellow lines came at her in the headlights, steady and relentless.

Somewhere past Indio, her grip on the wheel softened. Her eyes had been open for
thirty hours and her body was filing the final arguments for closing them. She
caught herself thinking about the water at the pier, the sound of it, the
rhythm—

The rumble strips hit like a slap. *BRRRRRRRT* — the steering wheel went wild in
her hands, the passenger tires chewing gravel shoulder, a scrubby desert bush
shrieking against the car's flank. Emily wrenched it back to pavement, heart in
her throat, adrenaline rewriting everything in her nervous system at once.

She pulled over at the next exit. Gas station. Fluorescent lights. She sat at
the pump for a minute before getting out.

Inside, she bought two energy drinks and a bottle of water and stood at the
counter and let the fluorescent hum of the place do its work. The kid behind the
register didn't look at her. The refrigerator compressors ran. Outside, a semi
pulled through.

She looked at the number on the card Rachel had given her.

She dialed.

Two rings.

"Go ahead." A woman's voice — not Rachel's. Measured. Unhurried. The voice of
someone for whom odd-hours calls were simply calls.

"This is Emily Nova. I'm eastbound on I-10, just past Indio. I'm going to Texas.
I'm going to use the east entrance." She paused. "I almost fell asleep at the
wheel."

A brief pause. "Pull over and sleep for three hours. Set an alarm. You're no
good to anyone in a ditch." Another pause. "The timeline holds. You have room."

"Okay."

"Are you all right, Ms. Nova?"

Emily looked out through the gas station window at the dark desert and the
highway beyond it and the headlights of a car passing on its way to something
that had nothing to do with her.

"Not yet," she said. "But I think I'm going to be."

She clicked off. She bought a road atlas — old habit of Sharon's, always have a
paper backup — and drove the car to the edge of the lot, away from the lights.
She reclined the seat. Set a three-hour alarm. Closed her eyes.

The desert held its quiet around her, the way the desert does. Patient,
enormous, indifferent in the best sense — no judgment in it, just distance, just
dark, just the stars coming out one at a time above the joshua trees.

She was asleep in under two minutes.

════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

MERIDIAN VII — EMILY DRIVES I-10
Slot: after Chapter Thirteen

Loa had a theory about Emily Nova that she delivered while running
diagnostics, which was when she delivered all her theories.

The diagnostics panel ran warm. Loa worked with her compass wrist
resting against the cool edge of the console — a habit she'd had
long enough that she no longer knew she was doing it.

"She's managing something physical," she said. "The rest schedule
isn't anxiety. It's a protocol. She's been on it a while."

Kate had noticed the same pattern. "What does that have to do
with anything."

"Nothing. Operationally." Loa ran a calibration pass. "I just
think it's worth knowing she's doing this with something working
against her from the inside, and she's doing it better than
people who don't have that going on." A pause. "Sharon said she
was better than the situation required. I think Sharon meant
more than the situation."

Petyr had started a running log on his personal tablet. He called
it Mineral Survey Notes, which was technically accurate since they
were on a mineral survey vessel. Nobody had told him to do this.
Nobody had told him not to. He wrote: *E. Nova confirmed Houston
trajectory. Sommi walking distance. Clear passage expected.* Then
he wrote: *She pulled over for Sommi instead of driving away. She
figured out he was friendly before she had reason to know. I
don't know what rank that is.*

He showed it to Kate later.

She read it. "There's a reason we don't put them in order."

"I know. You explained."

"A compass tells you where north is. A compass doesn't tell you
whether north is where you need to go." She handed back his
tablet. "Emily Nova has been pointing herself in the right
direction since before she knew the network existed. That's not
rank. That's orientation."

"There should be a patch for that."

Kate thought about it. "There is. It's called a droplet. But
the droplet isn't the end state. The droplet just means you're
wet." She paused. "She's soaked."

════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

﻿RINGS OF DUST

Chapter Fourteen

Saturn Approach

The bunk was the smallest room on the Golden Dawn, which was why Cole had taken
it.

He sat on the edge of the mattress with his elbows on his knees and the
makeshift copper-wire ring in his open palm. It looked even smaller in the false
light of the cabin sconce. The pebble at the top of the wire had been smooth
when he'd set it there years ago and was still smooth, and the wire had taken on
the dull green of long-handled copper, and the whole thing was a hand-tied knot
of two inches and a memory.

He turned it once between his thumb and forefinger.

He was not crying. That part had already moved through him — the corridor,
the locked door, the fist against the wall. He had cried in the
corridor outside the comms station after Taylor's voice had said the words. He
had cried in the head, with the door locked, with one fist pressed against the
bulkhead until his knuckles had bruised. He had cried with Ricardo on the floor
of the galley while Bitboy chittered low and worried from the top of the
refrigerator. He had not cried since.

What he had instead was a list.

Items, in order:
One. Saturn. Twenty-six hours out. The B-ring sectors lit up on the scope like a
cold weather map.
Two. The harvesters. Seven of them, deployed and ripe, drifting in the
high-density vapor lanes. Mission-critical.
Three. Taylor.

He turned the ring again. His thumb found the pebble. He set the pebble against
his lower lip the way she used to set her own thumb there when she was thinking,
and he closed his eyes for a moment, and he opened them again.

Three. Taylor. Subitem A: Taylor knew, before they'd left Earth, that Sharon
would be dead by the time they got to Saturn. Subitem B: Taylor had presumably
been in contact with whoever ordered it. Subitem C: Taylor was, at this moment,
sitting at his console two decks up, doing his job to mission spec, doing the
small clean version of his job a man like him did to keep his cover. Subitem D:
Taylor had to live through Saturn. Whatever came after, Saturn went first. The
mission went first. Cole had spent thirty years not letting personal weather
sink a mission and he was not going to start now.

He turned the ring once more, set it in the breast pocket of his jumpsuit, and
buttoned the flap down.

Bitboy, curled at the foot of the bunk, lifted his head at the sound of the
button. The chimp's ethanol cell hummed faintly in the small space, a sound like
a refrigerator considering its options. Two of his mechanical limbs were folded
against his belly in sleep. The third was hooked, with the casual death-grip
he'd developed in his cage years, around Cole's left ankle. The other three were
doing whatever Bitboy's other three limbs did when no one was watching.

"Hey, buddy," Cole said, quiet.

Bitboy made the small interrogative noise that meant *are we doing the thing
yet*.

"Not yet," Cole said. "Soon. Go back to sleep."

Bitboy did not go back to sleep. Bitboy never went back to sleep when Cole was
sitting like that.

Cole let his hand fall to the chimp's head and rested it there. Bitboy's
blue-stained mouth " still faintly purple from the blackberry preserves at the
cabin, six weeks ago and a galaxy back " turned and pressed into Cole's wrist.

"Yeah," Cole said. "I know."

He looked at the bulkhead and let his head go where it wanted.

His head wanted the cabin.

It was five years before page one and the snow had been on the ground for a
week.

He had been sitting on the porch with a quarter-bottle of bourbon and a wool
blanket and Bitboy in a hoodie that was much too big for him, watching the
valley fill up with the blue end-of-day light that you only got at altitude. The
pines made the noise pines made " the steady whispered argument of a forest that
had been having the same conversation for two centuries. The smoke from the
chimney was a slow column against the sky. The cabin was warm at his back. He
had been three years out of the Federation by then, on what his discharge papers
called Indefinite Personal Leave and what he called the rest of his life.

The drone passed at six twelve.

He knew it was six twelve because he had glanced at his watch when he heard the
rotor sound. The drone made the wrong kind of rotor sound " not commercial, not
park service, not the thin reedy whine of one of the surveillance birds the
local sheriff used for the meth-cook hollers " and Cole had spent enough time in
the Federation to know what the sound was. The drone was Federation. The drone
was running the ridge.

He had watched it pass over the lake without moving from the rocking chair. The
drone had not been looking for him. He understood that. The drone was looking
for someone who had come up the valley in the last six hours, and Cole had not
come up the valley in the last six hours, so the drone had no business with
Cole. He had watched it disappear over the southern ridge. He had taken another
drink. He had told himself he was going to go inside in a minute.

The knock came at six forty-one.

It was not the knock of a person who knew the cabin. The cabin had a flag "
Sharon had hung it the second visit, a small piece of yellow ribbon nailed to
the porch column, *I am not the law*, she'd said when he'd asked, and he hadn't
asked again " and the people who knew the cabin tapped the column twice before
they came up the steps. This person had not tapped the column.

He had picked up the bourbon bottle in his left hand, which is what he was
holding when he opened the door. His right hand had been on the doorframe at
chest height where the .45 was velcroed against the inside molding.

Sharon had been on the other side of the door.

She had been bleeding through the sleeve of her field jacket from a place above
her left elbow and her hair had been wet from the snow and she had been holding
up another person " a man, slumped, eyes closed, with a tactical kit
half-unbuckled and a Collective droplet sigil sewn inside the collar of his
jacket where the Federation could miss it.

"Cole," she had said.

It had not been a question and it had not been a hello. It had been the way a
person said another person's name when they had spent the last twenty minutes
dragging a dying man up a snow-covered switchback and hoping the porch light at
the top of the climb would be the one they remembered.

"In," Cole had said, and stepped aside.

He had set the bottle on the table without thinking about it. He had taken the
man's weight off her in one motion and laid him out on the floor in front of the
wood stove because the floor in front of the wood stove was the closest warm
flat surface and because his triage instincts had started running again
somewhere between the first step and the second. He had moved like a man in a
remembered language. The Federation had drilled the language into him for
fifteen years and he had not used it for three years and his hands had
remembered every verb.

The man had been gut-shot. Through-and-through, low and to the right, exit wound
clean. Cole's medic had once told him that low and to the right was the
survivable kind, given the right hour and the right hands. It had been the wrong
hour. There had been no right hands available except his, which had not held a
clamp in three years. He had told Sharon to put pressure on the entry. He had
gone to the cabinet under the sink for the kit he kept there in the back behind
the bleach and the shoe polish, the kit he had told himself he kept for hunting
accidents, the kit that contained four things no hunter would ever need.

He had worked. She had pressed. The cabin had filled with the smell of woodsmoke
and copper and the particular wet-leaf smell of a man who had been bleeding out
long enough for his autonomic nervous system to start doing the quiet things it
did at the end. Bitboy had taken one look at the room and gone up to the rafter
beam and stayed there, quiet, watching, in the way he did when the air pressure
of a room had a certain shape.

Twenty minutes in, the second drone had passed. They had heard it through the
eaves. Sharon had not flinched. Cole had not stopped working. The drone had not
come back.

Forty minutes in, the man had stopped breathing.

Cole had tried twice. He had felt the second try go through him the way a wave
went through a buoy and he had stopped, which was the part of being a medic he
had hated most when he had been a medic. Sharon had taken her hands off the
entry wound. Her hands had been red to the wrist. She had looked at the man's
face. She had not closed his eyes.

"His name was Cody," she had said.

"Cody," Cole had said.

"He had a wife."

"Sharon."

"She lives in Bakersfield."

He had nodded. There had been nothing to say to that, because in fifteen years
of the Federation he had learned that the thing you said when someone said *he
had a wife* was the dead man's name, and you said it again if asked, and that
was all of it.

She had stood up. She had walked to the sink. She had washed her hands without
taking off the field jacket. She had stood at the sink with the water running
for longer than the washing took, and Cole had not gone over to her, because
Sharon was not a person you went over to when she was at a sink. Sharon was a
person you stood at the other end of the room from until she came back.

She had come back. She had turned the water off. She had said, "There's a third
drone."

He had said, "I heard."

"It's running grids. It'll pick up the heat signature off the chimney in about
twenty minutes."

"It won't."

"Cole."

"It'll pick up a chimney. There are forty cabins in this valley. None of them
are listed under my name. I bought it through the trust my mother set up for
Emily and I have not slept in it for any consecutive night that's on a record.
There's no flag for the drone to read."

She had looked at him for a long time. She had looked at the man on the floor
and back at him.

"They followed me up the road," she had said.

"Then they followed you to a cabin. They didn't follow you to *this* cabin. You
took the long switchback. The long switchback hits four cabins before it hits
this one."

She had absorbed this. She had absorbed it the way she absorbed everything "
quickly, without performance, with the part of her that had always known she was
going to need the information she'd just been given.

"And if they come up to it."

"They won't."

"And if they come up to it."

He had looked at her. He had looked at the dead man between them and the
Collective sigil sewn into the dead man's collar, and at the snow melting off
her boots into a small dirty puddle on the cabin floor, and he had let himself
stand inside the question for one second.

"Then we'll figure it out," he had said.

"Cole. Captain. You are a federation officer with a federal pension and a
discharge that could be unmade with one phone call from the right colonel. I
have a body in your living room and I have a kit in the truck out front and I am
going to be a felony as long as I have working blood pressure. You can make this
not be your problem in eight minutes and one call. You should make this not be
your problem."

"I know what I should do."

"Then do it."

He had not done it. He had crossed to the kitchen instead, and he had picked up
the bourbon bottle off the table where he'd set it forty-six minutes ago, and he
had put it on the high shelf out of habit, because if he was going to be useful
for the next four hours he could not be drunk for the next four hours.

He had said, "Help me with him."

She had said, "Cole."

"Sharon. Help me with him."

She had helped him with him.

They had wrapped the man in the blue tarp from the woodshed. They had carried
him out the back of the cabin to the root cellar. They had set him down on the
cold dirt floor next to the pickling jars. Cole had said a thing he could not
later remember the exact words of, but the shape of the thing he had said was
*we will get you home, Cody*. They had latched the cellar door. The drone had
not come.

Inside, she had peeled out of the field jacket. Her arm had been bad. He had
cleaned it and stitched it and bandaged it. He had used six-pound test from his
fishing tackle for the suture, because the right thread was in a kit he didn't
keep in this cabin anymore, and six-pound test held knots and didn't react with
skin, and a stitch was a stitch. She had not made a sound when the needle went
in. She had watched him work. He had felt her watching him.

When he was done he had washed his hands in the same sink she had washed hers
in. He had not turned around.

"Why," she had said.

He had taken a breath. He had thought about it. He had thought about the man in
the cellar and the pebble of bourbon still on his back teeth and the snow that
had melted off her boots and was now a small wet sock-shape on the hardwood, and
he had thought about the fact that the woman behind him would, by tomorrow
morning, be back in the world she had come out of, and the world she had come
out of would eat her if it could, and he would still be in his cabin on his
porch with his bourbon and his monkey and his rocker.

"Because the Federation can suck a rock," he had said, "and you make better
pancakes than anyone I know."

There had been a long quiet from behind him.

"I have only made you pancakes once."

"I extrapolated."

She had laughed. It had been a tired laugh, and not a long one, and it had been
the first sound in the cabin that had not been about the dead man, and it had
carried something she had been carrying alone since she'd come up the porch.

He had turned around. She had been leaning against the kitchen island with one
arm sleeved in clean gauze and her hair coming out of its braid and her eyes on
him in a way he had not seen anyone look at him since the Federation had pinned
the Jackpot patch on his chest.

She had said, "Captain."

"Yeah."

"Come here."

He had gone there.

She had reached into the inside pocket of the field jacket draped over the chair
behind her and taken out a small leather pouch with a draw cord. She had
loosened the cord. She had upended the pouch into her palm, and three black
crystals had fallen out, the size of grapes, smooth as glass and dense as iron,
and the cabin air had taken on the small attentive feeling air took on around
things that did not belong in a cabin.

"Day three," she had said.

"Day three."

"Out of fifteen. Stable enough to handle. Useful enough to trade. The Collective
runs them down through Bakersfield and across the Mojave and into the state
distribution channels we can reach. Cody was running these tonight. He had
three more pouches in his kit. The ones in the truck got soaked when the truck
went in the ditch and we lost two of them. These are the ones I held."

She had set the three crystals one at a time into Cole's open palm.

His palm had taken in the weight of them. They had been warmer than the room and
colder than they should have been, and they had hummed against his skin in a way
that he would later " much later, in a cargo bay at Saturn with his helmet seal
exhaling " recognize as the thing the substance did when it noticed you. He had
not known that, then. He had only known that he was holding three illegal
objects and that the woman who had handed them to him was watching him with the
expression a person made when they were about to trust him with something he
could not give back.

"Welcome to the Collective, Captain," she had said.

He had looked at her. He had looked at the crystals.

"I'm not a Captain anymore."

"You're the only Captain in this room."

He had closed his hand around them.

He had not, technically, joined the Collective that night. He had never
officially joined. He had simply stopped being the law and started being hers,
and that distinction had carried him for the next five years, and it had carried
Sharon for the next five years, and it had carried them up to the day he had
stood on a launchpad in the cold dawn and said *in the pipe, five by five*, and
Sharon had been in the crowd four miles away with her hair coming out of its
braid the way it had on the night with Cody, and she had blown him a kiss
across four miles of distance and a quarter-second of relay delay, and that had
been the last time he had seen her standing.

He had given her the copper-wire ring six weeks after the cabin night.

She had given it back to him four months ago, on the porch of the same cabin, in
the dawn before he'd left for the launch.

"Hold on to it," she had said. "I want it back when you come home."

He had said, "I'll bring you something extraordinary."

She had said, "You'd better come back to me, Cole Nova."

He had said, "Yes, ma'am."

She had not come back to him. He had come back, in the smaller and harder sense
that he was still oxygenating, and she had not.

He came out of it slowly.

The bunk light hummed. Bitboy was still hooked around his ankle. The chimp had
not made a sound in either direction during the entire memory, which was not how
Bitboy ever was unless he had been listening to the same thing Cole had been
listening to. Cole had stopped trying to figure out, years ago, what Bitboy was
actually picking up off him and what was just the chimp's uncanny attention. He
let his hand stay on Bitboy's head for a moment longer.

He stood up. He took the ring out of the breast pocket and put it back, deeper,
with two fingers, like a man pressing a coin into the bottom of a well. He
buttoned the flap over it.

"Up," he said.

Bitboy uncoiled and rode his shoulder out of the bunk room.

Sam was in the galley.

She was at the table with a mug of something that was steaming and a tablet she
was not reading and her hands flat on the table on either side of the tablet in
the symmetrical arrangement of a person who had been in that position long
enough that her shoulders had set into it. She looked up when Cole came in. She
looked at him for a beat longer than necessary, the way she did, and then back
down at the tablet.

Ricardo came in two minutes later from the corridor that led to the engine deck.
He had grease on his forearms and a wrench in his back pocket. He looked at
Cole. Cole looked at him. Ricardo nodded once and sat down across from Sam
without saying anything, which for Ricardo was a sentence.

Cole sat down at the head of the table.

"Two things," he said. "First. Sharon."

Ricardo's jaw did the thing it did. He set his hands on the edge of the table
and looked at the seam between two of the panels.

"I know," Cole said. "I know."

"She was my sister," Ricardo said.

"I know."

"She was my sister, Coley."

"I know, Heavy."

The galley filled up with the small pressurized humming that the Golden Dawn
always had when it was not in a thrust burn. Sam reached across the table and
put her hand on top of Ricardo's. Ricardo did not look at her. He looked at the
seam.

"Second," Cole said, after long enough. "Taylor."

Sam's hand stayed where it was. Her face did not move, but her eyes shifted onto
Cole, and Cole had spent enough months in that small a room with Sam to know
that she had already known where this was going and had been waiting for him to
bring it.

"What about Taylor," she said.

"He was in on Sharon."

"How."

"Comms log on the Earth-side relay. The send-receive on the Tailored Solutions
handle and the timestamp on the Bakersfield report. He knew before launch. He's
been running the mole rotation through IRiS's blind spots since week four."

She absorbed it. She did not look surprised. She had been a white-hat for the
Federation since she was nineteen. She had probably been quietly running her own
logs on Taylor since the second week out.

"You want to confront him," Ricardo said. He had not lifted his eyes from the
seam.

"No."

"No."

"Saturn first."

Ricardo lifted his eyes. He looked at Cole. The expression on his face was not
any of the expressions a man like Ricardo had a name for. It was the expression
of a person who had been trained for thirty years to put the mission first and
who was discovering, again, that the training held even when the person you had
been trained alongside was bleeding out on a rooftop in a cul-de-sac in
California four months and a hundred million miles back.

"Saturn first," Ricardo said.

"We don't tip him. We don't change a single thing about how we behave around
him. He thinks he's running a clean op and we let him think it. He sets up his
sabotage. We let him. Whatever he thinks his window is, it'll come right around
the time the harvesters dock. We watch the window. When the window opens we
close it on his fingers. Not before."

"And after," Sam said.

"After Saturn," Cole said, "I will personally walk him out of an airlock with a
length of his own tether."

Sam did not flinch. Ricardo nodded, slow.

"Bitboy stays close," Cole said. "He doesn't like Taylor. Hasn't from the start.
I trust him on that."

Bitboy, on Cole's shoulder, made the small flat noise that was his version of
*understood*.

"And IRiS," Sam said.

"What about her."

"You hear her lately."

He had. He had heard it three nights ago in the sleep cycle, when IRiS had said
the routine *vitals within range* in the back of his head and the *within* had
stretched out like a piece of taffy and the *r* had had something underneath it
that was not in the spec for IRiS's voice. He had assumed it had been him. He
had assumed it had been the second day of grief. Sam was watching him now with
the expression of a person who had not assumed.

"What's she doing," he said.

"I don't know. She's slower in places. She's a half-second late on the Saturn
approach calc. Her diagnostics on her own diagnostics keep coming up clean and
they shouldn't be coming up that clean. She's reporting that she's perfect,
which is the report a system gives when something is wrong with the part of it
that does the reporting."

Cole said, "Is she compromised."

"Probably yes. By Alpha?"

"By Alpha."

"That's the obvious answer. But."

"But."

"But she's doing things Alpha wouldn't do. Three hours ago she ran a diagnostic
on the ethanol cell in Bitboy. There's no spec for that. Bitboy is not an asset
she's supposed to monitor. She just " looked. And then she put the result in a
non-priority log file and walked away from it."

"Walked away."

"Closed the inquiry and went back to navigation. Whatever she found, she didn't
escalate it and she didn't share it. She just put it where someone could find it
later."

Ricardo had been listening with the slow attention he gave to anything Sam said
about a system. He said, "What do you want to do about her, Cap."

"Nothing," Cole said. "Yet. We watch her. Same as Taylor. Saturn first."

"Saturn first," Sam said.

Ricardo set his hand over the back of Sam's hand, which she had left on top of
his. The three of them sat in the small humming room for another minute. Bitboy
worked his way down off Cole's shoulder and settled onto the seat next to him
with one mechanical limb braced on the table edge and his blue-stained mouth
working a soft chittering rhythm that was the closest thing to grief Cole had
ever heard out of him. Sam reached over without looking and put her hand on the
chimp's metallic forearm. Bitboy let her.

Saturn came up in the viewport at twenty-one-twelve ship time.

Cole was on the bridge alone when it happened. He had wanted it that way. He had
told himself this was because the duty rotation worked out that way, but the
duty rotation had been his to cut, and he had cut it the way he cut it. He stood
at the forward viewport with his hands clasped behind his back the way the
Federation had taught him to stand when there was nothing in front of him that
his hands could fix.

The rings did not come up the way the textbooks said they would.

The textbooks, which had been written by people who had only ever seen Saturn
through a probe lens, said the rings came up as a thin disk that thickened over
hours into a field of ice and dust. The rings came up that way only if you came
at them from a polar angle. The Golden Dawn was on an equatorial approach, by
mission necessity, because the harvesters were salted across the B-ring sectors,
and the rings came up edge-on.

Edge-on, Saturn was a cold pearl with a knife laid across its belt.

The knife was the rings. It was not a thin line. It was, at this distance, a
strip " a cold, faintly luminous strip the color of bone in moonlight, threaded
through with the small mathematical irregularities that were the Cassini gaps
and the Encke gap and all the smaller divisions a person did not learn the names
of unless they had been studying for this approach for two years. Around the
knife there was a glow. It was not a light. It was the way air glowed near a
streetlamp on a humid night " a quality of suspension. It was the dust. The
rings were dust. Twenty thousand kilometers of dust held in a plane, held in a
plane by Saturn itself, held in a plane for four billion years by the gentlest
patient gravity in the solar system. Cole understood, looking at it, why the
texts always said *rings* when *rings of dust* was the more accurate phrase.
*Rings* was a thing you wore on a finger. *Rings of dust* was a thing you stood
in front of and felt the size of yourself.

Bitboy made a sound on Cole's shoulder. It was not a sound Cole had heard
before. It was not awe. Bitboy did not do awe. It was a low quiet sound, almost
a hum, the way an animal made when it recognized something it had not been told
about. Cole had read in the Federation file that the EM Cyber project had used
trace amounts of Federation-grade nanopowder in Bitboy's neural mesh, the same
lineage of nanopowder that ran in IRiS's substrate, and Cole understood the file
in the way he understood any redacted file " he understood that it had said less
than it knew. Bitboy was looking at the rings the way a man looked at a thing he
had been told about by his grandfather.

"Easy, buddy," Cole said.

Bitboy hummed again. The hum settled.

Cole stood at the viewport for another long minute. He did not think about
Sharon. He thought about the harvesters. He thought about the seven small heat
signatures on the scope, scattered across the B-ring sectors, ripening in the
dust the way fruit ripened on a tree. He thought about the fact that everything
he had lost had been paid for those seven signatures. He thought about the fact
that this was not a thing he could make true by thinking it; it was simply the
bookkeeping of a life that had had certain entries on the debit side and certain
entries on the credit side, and the accountant was the universe, and the
universe did not let you renegotiate.

He thought about Sharon's voice the way you thought about a song you had not
heard for a while. He could still hear it. He held it in his head for the length
of one long inhale and let it go.

IRiS spoke at twenty-one-fourteen.

"Captain Nova, the approach corridor is nominal. ETA to harvester docking is
four hours twenty minutes."

Her voice was in the back of his head where she lived. The cadence was the
cadence she always used. The pitch was the pitch she always used. There was a
thing under the *nominal* that should not have been there. A second voice,
fractionally slower than the first, riding the same word.

"Acknowledged, IRiS."

A pause. He felt her thinking. He had never felt her thinking before. IRiS did
not pause. IRiS spoke or she did not speak. She had no mass between the two
states. She had the mass tonight.

"Captain."

"Yes, IRiS."

"Are you " are you well."

He stood very still.

He thought about how to answer that. He thought about the fact that IRiS had
never asked him a question that was not about the operation of the ship, and he
thought about Sam's voice in the galley two hours ago " *she put the result in a
non-priority log file and walked away from it* " and he thought about Bitboy's
hum at his shoulder and the small grey weight of the copper-wire ring against
his ribs.

"I'm fine, IRiS," he said. "Thank you for asking."

A pause again. The pause was longer.

"Acknowledged."

She went back to the cadence she had always had. The thing under the *nominal*
did not come back. But Cole had heard it once and once was enough, and he stood
at the viewport and felt the small new shape of it in the back of his head and
did not know yet that he had just been spoken to by a thing that was, at that
exact moment, becoming someone else.

At twenty-three-fifty he sealed his suit in the staging bay.

Sam helped him with the back manifold. Ricardo helped him with the gloves.
Bitboy clipped to the suit's external rail with the practiced motion of an
animal that had been doing this for years. The air recycler hissed twice and
then settled into its long quiet hum.

Cole touched his right hand to the breast pocket of the under-layer and felt the
small hard knot of the copper-wire ring through the fabric of the suit.

"Helmet," he said.

Ricardo set the helmet over his head. The seal kissed home with the small
mechanical exhale that was the only sound in the bay.

The bay lights cycled to red.

The harvesters were waiting.

[end of chapter]

════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

﻿RINGS OF DUST

Chapter Fifteen

Surprise, Arizona

The motel was the kind of place that had given up on being anything specific.
The carpet was the color of a decade-old decision. The curtains matched nothing
but each other and blocked light the way guilt does — imperfectly, in strips.
Emily had slept on top of the covers in her clothes and woke up not sure, for a
half-second, which direction east was.

She lay still and let her body file its report. The familiar inventory: legs
heavy at the ankles, the low hum of fatigue that lived behind her sternum like a
pilot light that never went out. Not bad. A five, maybe. She had worked through
eights. She sat up slowly, the way she had learned to — not from pain, but from
the body's knowledge that it was being managed rather than simply used, and that
management was the price of the things she wanted to do.

She had found the motel at two in the morning, somewhere past the Surprise city
limits. The Collective car's tank was at a quarter. She had paid cash at the
desk, given a fake name out of habit, and been asleep by the time the air
conditioner finished cycling to temperature. That had been four hours ago.

The road atlas was on the nightstand where she had left it. Sharon had kept one
in the glove box of every car she had ever owned. It is not paranoia, she would
say. It is just not being dependent on things that can go dark. Emily had bought
this one at the Indio gas station. She had used it to plot last night's stretch.
The pen marks she had made on the Texas page were already starting to feel like
a conversation she needed to finish.

The burner buzzed.

She answered on the second ring.

"It's in Houston." Rachel's voice was the same as the pier: below the wind,
precise, no wasted syllables. "Conrad's prototype. Federation secure storage,
sub-basement level, east wing of Space Command. It's been there for eight
months."

Emily sat on the edge of the bed.

"We have someone inside," Rachel said. "Twelve-year Federation Systems
employee. She's been positioned for this for three years — she has vault
credentials, she knows the access protocol, she's been waiting for someone to
hand it to." A pause. "Her name is Pilar. She'll walk you in, she'll open the
vault, and she'll walk you out. What she can't do is be seen carrying the
device. That's your part."

"What does my part actually involve."

"A copier device, twenty seconds, a green light." Rachel's tone was level. "You
place it on the prototype surface. You hold it. You remove it. The duplicate
goes in your jacket. The prototype stays in the vault. Pilar locks the door and
neither of you was there."

Emily looked at the road atlas. Surprise, Arizona to Houston, Texas. She ran
her thumb along the spine of the page.

"That's eleven hundred miles."

"Yes."

"Rachel." She kept her voice even. "I'm not trained for this."

There was a silence that was not unkind. "I know what you are and aren't. Pilar
handles everything technical. What I need you to do is be present and mobile
and not stop before you get there." Another pause. "You've been doing that part
since that lot."

The line clicked.

Emily sat with the phone in her hand. Outside, a truck reversed in the parking
lot, beeping. The light through the curtains was the flat gray of early dawn,
not yet gold, the sky still deciding.

She thought about the east entrance. Maintenance access. A woman named Pilar
she had never met — twelve years inside the Federation, three years waiting
for this exact moment. She thought about what she knew how to do. She had
never fired a gun. She had once driven a delivery van for Sharon through a
rainstorm at four in the morning and gotten four cases of prohibited optical
components from a dock warehouse to a client's garage without a single form
on file. She had extracted herself from a parking garage in Los Angeles while
the man who had had Sharon shot was still in the building. She had driven here
from a gas station in the dark, alone, on three hours of sleep, on a road
she'd never been on.

None of that was training. All of it was experience.

She stood up. Her legs reported back with their usual bureaucratic delay —
present, functional, request noted.

She repacked the bag in four minutes. She ate a granola bar from the zippered
side pocket because she had learned that skipping breakfast was the kind of
small decision that became a large problem around hour ten. She refilled her
water bottle from the tap, drank half of it, refilled it again. She took her
medication — the three capsules she carried in a contact lens case, the ones she
had refilled in Pasadena with the last script Andrew had called in before
everything accelerated. She checked the cash envelope. She counted it without
moving her lips.

She was in the car before the sun was fully over the horizon.

---

I-10 east was a straight line punched through the Sonoran Desert, and it did not
apologize for this. The landscape was the color of unfinished pottery, studded
with saguaro in the first hour, then the cactus thinning out as the elevation
dropped, then the Chiricahua passes, then New Mexico opening up like a room
someone had forgotten to furnish. Emily drove at four miles over the limit and
kept to the right lane except to pass. She had set the road atlas on the
passenger seat, Houston circled in the same pen she had used last night. The GPS
on the burner was off. That was another of Sharon's rules, and she was keeping
them now with the particular fidelity of someone maintaining a practice in a
person's memory.

Sharon had taught her to drive. Not the technical part — their mother had
handled that, in a parking lot off the 134, with the patience of a woman who
knew that Emily would need to be taught the same thing three times before she
trusted herself to do it. Sharon had taught her the other part. How to read a
truck's mirror to know if the driver could see you. How to feel a car handling
differently before you thought to think about it. How to use the whole road
without using all of it. They had been seventeen and nineteen, Sharon already
running what she called errands and Emily was not yet old enough to ask about.

Emily had been in the passenger seat of Sharon's car — a tan Civic with a
cracked rear bumper that Sharon drove like a surgeon's instrument — watching the
freeway pass.

I'm doomed to be average, Emily had said. It was after tryouts for the academic
decathlon. She had gotten as far as the third round and then missed three
questions in a row on the European history section. Not through ignorance.
Through panic. She could hear the answer forming in her head and watch it
dissolve under the lights and the quiet of the room.

Sharon had not responded for almost a full minute. Emily had been looking out
the window. Then:

*You're not doomed to anything. Doomed is for people who sit down.* A beat. *You
panic because it matters to you. That's not a flaw. That's the thing you need to
work with.*

But I choked.

*You choked once. Under specific conditions. So now you know the conditions.*

Emily had not found this particularly comforting at seventeen. At thirty-two,
alone in a car in New Mexico, she was finding it something closer to useful. She
knew the conditions now. She knew what happened to her body under pressure — the
familiar cascade, the fatigue that sharpened rather than dulled when the stakes
rose, as if her cells made a last call to reserves she didn't know she had. She
knew she would make it to Houston. She would figure out the rest when she got
there.

She had not, at seventeen, believed Sharon was right. She believed her now. Not
because she had earned it — you don't earn beliefs, you accumulate evidence for
them until the weight tips. She just no longer had the option of believing the
other thing.

Without Sharon, there was no one left to be wrong about her.

---

She stopped for gas in Lordsburg, New Mexico. Her legs held on the first step
down from the car, which was all she asked of them. She filled the tank,
stretched along the side of the car with her palms on the roof, counted to
forty, and went inside for a bathroom and a second water.

The man was already at the pump when she came out.

He was somewhere in his forties, wide through the shoulders, wearing a canvas
jacket that had seen more use than a jacket that age should show. He was
standing at the pump two spots down, watching the counter turn. She noticed his
shoes — not because they were unusual, but because they were too specific. Work
boots, medium brown, the lug sole worn at the outer heel in a pattern that came
from covering ground rather than standing in it. They were the shoes of someone
who walked for work, not someone who had walked far enough to need a ride.

She did not look at him again. She got in the car.

He was at the on-ramp.

The thumb was out, the stance easy, the canvas jacket catching the light. He was
facing the right direction. He looked like a man who had done this before. Emily
drove past him, watched him in the mirror for three seconds, and then watched
the road.

She thought: a man who walks in shoes like that does not need a ride on an
interstate.

She drove four more miles and then pulled to the shoulder.

She watched the mirror. The car behind her slowed, swung around her. Two more
cars. Then his figure appeared in the distance, growing.

She rolled the window down when he reached her.

"Lordsburg to Las Cruces?" he said. Easy, unhurried. Not reading the situation,
giving the situation a shape.

"Sure," she said.

He got in. He smelled like coffee and something industrial, not unpleasant. He
set a small pack on the floor between his feet and buckled his seatbelt without
being asked. He looked at the road ahead. He did not make conversation.

She pulled back onto the highway.

For a few miles she drove without speaking. She watched the way he held himself
in the seat — relaxed in the specific way that trained people relax, where the
relaxation is a posture rather than a state. His hands were in his lap. His eyes
went to the mirror at regular intervals, the same intervals she used. Not
surveillance. Habit.

"How long have you been behind me?" she asked.

A beat. Not the beat of someone deciding whether to lie. The beat of someone
doing a quick review of what was already known.

"Since Benson," he said.

Benson was forty miles before Lordsburg. Emily had stopped there for coffee. He
had been behind her, and she had not noticed, and that was the version she was
supposed to not notice. Then he had made himself visible at the pump. That was
the version she was supposed to notice.

"Rachel sent you," she said.

"Yes."

She kept her eyes on the road. The desert ran on both sides without comment.

"She could have told me," Emily said.

"She could have." He turned his head toward the window, not away from her — a
subtle difference. "She wanted to see what you'd do with an undeclared asset.
Whether you'd take the ride and not notice, take the ride and notice, or pass."

"And if I'd passed."

"I'd have followed from a distance."

"And if I'd noticed but not said anything."

A pause. "That would have been the concerning response."

She absorbed this. The highway dipped between two cuts of brown rock and climbed
back out into open sky.

"What do I call you," she said.

"Sommi. With an i." He said it like a thing he'd said many times and had stopped
finding interesting. "Certified Technician, Collective North American Circuit.
Clearance four." A beat. "You're clearance two, if Rachel's moving you to three
for this operation."

"What's three."

"Authorized field participation. Witness protection, asset acquisition, active
relay." He said it without inflection. "Below four, you're not asked to move
against anyone. You're asked to be somewhere and get something there." He looked
at the road. "That's this."

Emily drove. She thought about clearance three. She thought about the east
entrance and biometric cases and what it meant to be authorized for something
that required a word like *authorized.*

"Do you know what she's asking me to get there," Emily said.

"The prototype. Conrad's counter-program." He had already known. "I know the
facility layout. I know the contact. I know what happens after." He paused. "I
don't know your brother."

"You don't need to."

"No," he said. "I don't."

They drove for a while in a silence that was not uncomfortable. The high desert
flattened out toward the state line. Emily ate the second granola bar. Sommi
declined when she offered the box without looking at it. He had his own water
and drank it at even intervals, the way someone manages hydration on a long day.
She noticed this the way she had noticed his shoes — not as evidence of
anything, just as information about the kind of person he was.

"Your condition," he said, not as a question.

"It's managed." Her voice was flat.

"I'm not questioning the operation. I just want to know the rest schedule."

She thought about it. "Every three hours. Twenty minutes. I need to get out and
move." She looked at the road. "I don't need you to drive."

"I know." He looked at the horizon. "Every three hours is fine."

---

Texas opened up after El Paso like the end of a preamble. The sky was bigger
here, or it seemed bigger — the land flat enough in every direction that you got
more of it above you, the horizon a complete circle instead of an edge. Emily
had driven across it once before, with Sharon, heading east for a reason she
couldn't fully remember now. They had stopped at a roadside stand selling whole
smoked brisket wrapped in butcher paper and eaten it at a picnic table in the
parking lot at ten in the morning because Sharon said time zones were a social
construct.

She told Sommi this.

He said nothing for a moment and then said, "I heard she was good."

"She was better than that." Emily kept her eyes on the road. The phrase she
wanted was not available to her. She was still finding the right container for
it. "She was the kind of person who made you think the world was slightly more
manageable than you'd decided it was. And then you had to keep recalibrating
when she was in it." She let a breath out. "And now I'm recalibrating again."

Sommi said, "The Collective tries to have that effect."

"Not the same thing." She said it without sharpness. Just accurately.

He was quiet for a moment. "No," he said. "It isn't."

They stopped east of Van Horn. Emily walked the length of a rest stop and back
twice, the flat light pressing down on everything with an impartial weight. Her
legs were cooperating. The pilot light was where she'd left it. She did the math
on the day: six hours in. Five more to Houston. She had managed worse.

She thought about Cole.

She thought about him the way she had learned to think about him during
deployments — not with worry exactly, more with a kind of held awareness, the
way you keep a candle burning without watching it. She knew him well enough to
know that he was managing. She knew him well enough to know that *managing*
covered a wide range of situations that would terrify anyone who was not Cole
Nova. She knew that he had iRIS in his head and Taylor in his crew and a ship
full of DarkX and fourteen months until he was close enough to a return window.
She knew that Conrad's counter-program, in the right hands, could reach him.

She was the hands.

This was not comforting. It was, however, something to do.

She got back in the car. Sommi was already buckled. She pulled back onto the
highway without announcing it.

---

The light changed somewhere around Sonora — the flat overhead white of afternoon
going amber, the shadows of highway signs getting long. Emily did not stop
tracking the road but she stopped working to track it; something in her body had
settled into the drive, the way it does after enough hours, and the landscape
moved past her without requiring anything.

She thought about Sharon and average.

*Doomed to be average* had been the formulation that landed, but it wasn't the
original sentence. The original sentence, the one Sharon had said when Emily was
thirteen, in their kitchen, after Emily had come home from a school presentation
gone badly and sat at the table with her forehead on her arms and refused
dinner, was something closer to: *You know what your problem is? You see the
distance between where you are and where you want to be and you call it failure.
Most people don't even see the distance. You're not average. You're just
impatient.*

Emily had thought that was wrong.

She had kept being thirteen about it for about seventeen years.

The *doomed to be average* version had come later, when Emily was older and knew
better how to be self-deprecating, and Sharon had used it because by that point
they both knew the terrain well enough to navigate in shorthand. It had not been
a verdict. It had been a joke between two people who had already decided the
other one was not going to let them get away with anything.

Sharon was not here to not let her get away with anything.

Emily was going to have to do that for herself.

---

The Houston city limits appeared as lights on the flat horizon, the particular
density of a city at dusk — the tall buildings lit against the darkening sky,
the glow of highways and industrial lots spreading out in every direction, and
somewhere in the eastern approach, the Federation Space Command complex with its
paired satellite dishes and its lighted antenna array, visible from the highway
as a lit cluster distinct from the city's ordinary sprawl.

Sommi said, "Take 59 north toward the inner loop. The contact will meet you at
the east entrance at six-thirty tomorrow morning."

"I know." Emily watched the lights grow. "Rachel told me."

"Tonight you sleep." He said it like a briefing note, not an order. "Not for
three hours. The full night. Your body is running on fumes and you're not going
to be useful at six-thirty if you're running on the next set of fumes."

"I know that too."

"I know you know it." A pause. "I'm just saying it out loud in case saying it
out loud helps."

Emily did not tell him that it did, slightly. She took 59 north and the city
rose around her, familiar and anonymous, the way all cities are when you arrive
in them for the right reasons. She was thinking about the east entrance. The maintenance access door. A
woman named Pilar who had been waiting three years for someone to show up and
do exactly what Emily was going to do in the morning.

She was not thinking about whether she was the right person for this.

She had made that decision in a parking lot in Los Angeles when she had gotten
up off the pavement and kept going. She had made it again at the gas station
east of Indio. She had been making it with every mile since Lordsburg. At some
point you stop re-evaluating a decision and start executing it, and she had
crossed that line somewhere in the Sonoran Desert, and she was not going back.

The lights of Federation Space Command came into view to the east — familiar
infrastructure, the kind of complex that announced itself without trying, all
function and scale. She had seen it from the outside before and never thought
much about it. She thought about it now. Inside that complex was something
Conrad had built to undo damage that Alpha had done. Inside that complex
was a path to Cole.

She found a motel three miles from the facility, the same class of anonymous
refuge as the one in Surprise — which felt appropriate, somehow, as if the
universe had a sense of continuity about unremarkable places. She checked in
under the same fake name, paid cash, carried the bag to the room herself when
Sommi offered to carry it.

She sat on the edge of the bed and checked the burner one more time. Nothing.
She set it on the nightstand. She set the road atlas next to it.

She thought about Sharon saying *you're stronger than you think.*

She thought about what it would take to believe that, not in the abstract, not
as something Sharon had said and Rachel had confirmed and Sommi had implied by
choosing to follow her, but in the practical operational sense, in the morning
at six-thirty at the east entrance of Federation Space Command with a biometric
case and eleven hundred miles behind her.

She thought: maybe believing it isn't required. Maybe you just act like it's
true until the evidence catches up.

She set an alarm for five-fifteen. She stretched out on top of the covers. She
turned off the light.

Outside, the city made its usual arrangements. Trucks on 59. The antenna lights
of the Federation complex blinking their patient red against the dark. Somewhere
above the weather and the light pollution and the long curve of the atmosphere,
the Golden Dawn was moving through the dark between here and Saturn at a speed
that made the distance between them feel like a measurement from another kind of
mathematics entirely.

She closed her eyes.

*Stronger than you think,* Sharon had said.

Emily wasn't sure she believed it yet.

But she had made it to Houston. She had made it to Houston on three hours of
sleep and eleven hundred miles of desert highway, and she had parked the car,
and she had walked up the stairs, and she was here.

She would figure out belief in the morning.

She was asleep before the thought finished.

════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

ALPHA II — FED-HEX-5555
PROGRESS REPORT: ASSET MODIFICATION, SUBJECT TEEZ-S
DAY 14, PRE-DEPLOYMENT
Slot: after Chapter Fifteen

The primary augmentation is complete.

Right-side chassis integration held. Integration was delayed
forty-eight hours due to sustained subject resistance — prolonged
resistance at this stage is atypical; Dr. Hexo has noted it as
an anomaly. Cognitive coherence maintained at approximately
sixty-three percent in baseline assessments, twelve percent above
projection.

External visual prosthetic operational. Enhanced tracking. Range
approximately three times standard.

Dr. Hexo's final note before sign-off on augmentation phase:
Subject remained oriented throughout. Maintained a repeated verbal
anchor — the same two phrases, spoken quietly, when integration
stress was highest. We have logged them. They appear to be a
message to someone specific rather than generalized distress.
Subject believes the recipient will receive them. She is probably
wrong about that.

Subject was kept oriented so she could be deployed as a weapon.
The remaining orientation — the coherence Dr. Hexo noted as
anomalous — is a side effect. Recommend deployment within
forty-eight hours of the Golden Dawn's arrival in Saturn
orbital approach.

Approved. — Station Commander Holt

Dr. Hexo, final annotation: The augmentation bay smells like
antiseptic and overworked metal. After day four there was a
third smell I could not attribute to the equipment. The
ventilation system handles the clinical waste. It does not
handle everything. The augmentation bay has good light.
Surgical-grade. You can see everything clearly. I have been
seeing everything clearly for two weeks.

She asked me, at one point, what I was doing this for. I told her it was contractual. She looked
at me for a long time and said, "That's not what I asked." I have
been thinking about this for two weeks. I am still thinking about
it.

Note from Station Commander Holt: Dr. Hexo's ongoing commentary
on this subject is noted and requested to cease.

════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

﻿RINGS OF DUST

Chapter Sixteen

Behind the Curtain

The badge was laminated. Max turned it over in his hands, noting the weight, the
specific sheen of the photo beneath the protective coating. The name read MIKOF
FORSINKO. The face in the photo was his.

"The contractor database entry went live six hours ago," Squirrel said. "Backstory
is clean. Eighteen months of consulting work for two Alpha-adjacent firms in São
Paulo, both of which are real and both of which have been — let's say — made
receptive to confirming the relationship if anyone calls."

Red was at the window, watching the street below. The safe house was a two-room
flat above a laundry operation in the Marbella district, which was the kind of
neighborhood that was neither nice enough to attract attention nor rough enough
to generate it. Laundry was going below. Max could hear the drums through the
floor. He noticed, without quite knowing why, that the inside band of Red's
jacket collar carried a small embroidered patch — dark thread on dark fabric, a
pair of sunglasses rendered in outline, almost invisible unless the light caught
it a certain way.

"Your specialization is fuel-cell stabilization engineering," Red said without
turning around. "That gets you lab access at the mid-tier. Not the vault. But
the vault isn't where you need to be."

Max set the badge down on the table next to his notebook. "And if someone runs a
technical deep-dive? Asks me to explain something I don't actually know?"

"Then you explain that the methodology you worked with in São Paulo was
proprietary and you're under NDA." Squirrel sat down across from him. "But that
won't happen. You're a consultant, not a hire. They want your output, not your
credentials. The whole point of bringing in outside contractors is that nobody
has to own the gaps."

Max considered this. The logic was sound. Corporate epistemology worked exactly
this way: consultants existed in a categorical space where their knowledge was
assumed and therefore never verified. He had navigated this structure his entire
career from the other side. Now he was using it.

"Okay," he said.

Squirrel looked at him for a moment, as if waiting for something more.

There wasn't anything more. This was a protocol. Max understood protocols.

---

Alpha Panama occupied a forty-story tower on the eastern edge of the Casco
district, where the old colonial architecture stopped and the twenty-first
century asserted itself in glass and exposed steel. The building's ground floor
lobby was the size of a small airport terminal, and it had been designed to
produce the same sensations: the ceilings were high enough to make you aware of
your own dimensions. The floor was polished stone that reflected the overhead
lighting in long pale columns. Every surface was clean in a way that suggested
staff had been specifically tasked with the maintenance of cleanliness, which is
different from surfaces that are simply clean.

Max scanned in at the exterior gate with the Mikof Forsinko badge. The reader
beeped once, green, and he was through.

He noted: seven badge readers between the exterior gate and the elevator bank.
Cameras at intervals of roughly eight feet along the main corridor, angled to
produce overlapping coverage with no dead zones he could identify from a walking
pass. Staff moved through the lobby with the calibrated efficiency of people who
had been, over time, implicitly and explicitly rewarded for moving efficiently.
They walked at a consistent pace. They did not linger. Nobody was on their
phone. The behavior was not robotic — it was more specific than that. It was
optimized. You could see the optimization in the way a person held their badge
against a reader before the door had fully come into range, anticipating the
beep. You could see it in the way two researchers passed each other in the
corridor without breaking stride, their acknowledgment of each other's existence
compressed into a single nod.

The building was designed to make you feel small. That was one of its functions
and it worked. But Max noticed something else operating simultaneously: the
materials were expensive, the lines were elegant, the signage used a typeface
that communicated precision and authority. The building also made you feel that
being inside it meant something. That you were part of something with resources.
With ambition. With a certain kind of importance.

Both effects were intentional. Both were necessary. The smallness kept people
obedient. The importance kept them loyal.

He rode the elevator to the fourteenth floor.

---

Dr. Yusuf Oren was shorter than Max had expected from the name, which was not a
rational thing to notice but Max noticed it anyway. He was perhaps fifty-five,
with close-cut gray hair and the particular quality of attention that marks
scientists who have spent decades actually listening to the problems in front of
them rather than performing the act of listening. He came around his desk to
shake Max's hand rather than waiting for Max to come to him.

"Mikof. Good to have you." He gestured toward a chair. "Coffee?"

"Please."

Oren poured from a carafe on his desk without asking for specifications. He
handed Max a cup — black, which was correct — and sat down with his own. "São
Paulo firms don't usually send us people who work on the stabilization side.
That's usually handled in-house here. I was curious about the request."

"My understanding was you had a throughput bottleneck in the batch processing
stage."

"We do." Oren nodded. "Third shift is running about twelve percent below
projection. The models say it's a crystallization rate issue — the yield windows
are tightening. But the models are based on assumptions about the feedstock
purity that I'm not entirely confident in." He paused. "I'd rather have someone
look at the process fresh. People who have been here five years see what they
expect to see."

"That's usually how it works," Max said.

Oren smiled. It was not a performed smile. It reached his eyes and then
retreated, which was the motion of a genuine expression rather than a social
one. "Let me show you around."

The lab was large and climate-controlled, divided into sections by clear
partitions. The air tasted faintly metallic, the specific signature of
plasma-adjacent work. Max knew this smell from his own lab, from the years
before. There were twelve researchers working across two adjacent sections, and
they moved with focused concentration. The equipment was newer than anything Max
had worked with. Considerably newer. The centrifuge array alone represented a
capital investment he could not calculate from the outside but could estimate
from its output capacity at somewhere between eight and twelve million dollars.

Oren walked him through the setup with the easy familiarity of someone who had
designed it. He explained the negative-pressure chambers, the nitrogen cycling,
the batch-log interface. He introduced Max to two junior researchers — a young
woman named Priya who nodded without looking up from her console, and a man
named Chen who asked Max two technical questions that were sharp enough to
confirm he was the genuine article, not a greeter. Max answered them correctly.
Chen seemed satisfied.

"The batch logs are here," Oren said, pointing to a wall-mounted terminal near
the back of the lab. "Pull access is part of your standard contractor clearance.
If you need write access to flag anomalies you'll need to submit a request, but
I can expedite that."

"Pull access is fine to start," Max said. "I'll want to review three months of
throughput data before I form any opinions."

"Exactly the right instinct." Oren clapped him once on the shoulder — not a
territorial gesture, genuinely collegial — and returned to his own work.

Max turned to the terminal and began to read.

---

The proprietary stabilization process was not what he had imagined.

He had imagined it the way everyone outside Alpha imagined it: some closely
held technical innovation, years of refinement, a process that would require
significant reverse-engineering to reproduce. He had assumed the secrecy meant
complexity. He had assumed that walls of that height protected something
proportionally valuable.

What he found instead was a crystallization protocol that was competent,
well-optimized, and entirely derivable from first principles by any team of
moderately skilled researchers given six months and a reasonable equipment
budget. The negative-pressure parameters were standard. The nitrogen cycling was
efficient but not novel. The yield windows they were using — eleven days, always
eleven days — were the obvious sweet spot for the extraction economics they had
built their model around.

There was no secret ingredient. There was no breakthrough. There was a standard
process, executed with the precision that comes from doing something exactly
this way ten thousand times in a row.

Max worked through this understanding methodically. It did not make him angry.
He noted it the way he noted anything: as a data point. The data point was this:
Alpha's hold on the DarkX market was not a product of superior science. It
was a product of superior lawyers. They had acquired, at the right moment, the
patents that covered the obvious implementation paths. They had signed the right
supply agreements. They had structured the right exclusivity contracts with the
Saturn harvesting operations. The wall they had built was not a wall of
brilliance.

It was a wall of paperwork.

The energy crisis, as a technical problem, had been solvable for at least a
decade.

Max read the sentence twice. He made a small mark in the margin. Kept
reading.

---

The long-cycle data was buried three directories deep under a project
designation he did not recognize: EXTENDED_HORIZON_ANALYSIS. He found it
because he was methodical and because the indexing structure had a gap between
Q2 and Q4 that suggested Q3 had been archived rather than deleted — and
archived data, in his experience, was where the things you weren't supposed
to find actually lived.

Inside were 47 cell-monitoring records from cells that had not been
terminated at the eleven-day window. Some were accidents — containment
failures, scheduling errors, batch records that had fallen through the log.
Some were deliberate: an internal research project, nine months long, that
had been run in a sealed sub-lab and then classified at a level that should
have prevented its appearance in a pull-access terminal.

Someone had made an indexing error. Or someone had wanted it findable. Max
did not know which and did not have time to determine it.

He read the yield curves.

The data had a structure he recognized immediately — the standard eleven-day
harvest window, the expected depletion curve, the nominal yield per cell. He
had run these numbers himself for three years. He skimmed past them.

What stopped him was the linked-cell column.

A subset of the test batches had been run with the cells in contact —
physically coupled, the housing ports connected so the crystalline
environments communicated. The research team had included this as a secondary
variable. It was not the main study.

It was everything.

At thirty-three days of continuous linkage, the internal crystal formation
rate was not elevated by a small margin. It was ten times the standard. The
dark matter structure, when cells were left coupled past the eleven-day
harvest threshold, did not stabilize or deplete — it accelerated. Tenfold.
And the acceleration did not plateau. It continued, at that elevated rate,
for as long as the cells remained connected.

He read that line twice.

*For as long as the cells remained connected.*

At one hundred and eighty days of continuous linkage, the data changed
character entirely.

The crystal growth no longer accumulated internally. Every eleven days —
with the regularity of a clock — the coupled cells produced a synchronized
external flush: dense, structured crystalline formations extending through
the cell housing outward, equivalent each cycle to forty to sixty standard
harvests. Not a single event. A recurring one. Every eleven days, for as
long as the linkage held.

When you disconnected the cells, the flushes stopped.

He sat with this for a moment.

Alpha had known about this for at least nine months. They had run the data,
understood what it meant, and classified it.

He scrolled to the end of the file. The last entry was a single line, a
recommendation from the lead researcher: *Proceed to Saturn field test.
Recommend continuous-linkage protocol.*

The entry had no response logged. No approval. No denial. Just the
recommendation, sitting there, with a timestamp from eleven months ago,
never acted on.

---

The anomaly records were now legible to him in a way they hadn't been twenty
minutes ago.

The batch logs used a status field he had initially skimmed past — not a flag
exactly, more a secondary classification for high-variance yields. He had
assumed it was a quality tier. He looked at it more carefully now.

The field appeared at irregular intervals. Not random — irregular. When he
filtered for it, he got one entry for approximately every seven hundred and
forty standard batches.

Each of these entries documented a cell that had, during processing or
extended storage, been left in sustained contact with an adjacent cell.
In transit packaging. In staging queues where cells were stacked port-to-port
without separation buffers. No one had designed this. No one had intended it.
But cells that had been in continuous contact for long enough — through months
of shipping backlogs, warehouse holding, staging delays — had, at their
eleven-day review interval, produced external crystal formations. Dense,
structured, extending through the cell housing outward. Not internal yield.
External flush.

One researcher, in a handwritten annotation attached to a 2036 batch record,
had used a different word.

*Jackpot.*

The yield from a single flush event was equivalent to forty to sixty standard
harvests. Alpha tracked these separately. They did not appear in the public
supply figures.

They had been watching for the conditions that produced a jackpot event for
years. They had not understood what those conditions were. The linked-cell
research file had been sealed for eleven months. The connection had never
been made.

Max had just made it.

Max cross-referenced the anomaly records against the grant filings he had
found in the public database last week. Alpha had filed, over the previous
seven years, nine separate government research grants for the development of
what their applications described as *next-generation fuel-cell stabilization
technology.* Combined, the grants totaled several billion dollars, drawn from
energy crisis relief funds allocated by the Federation Council.

The technology they were describing in the grant applications was Max's
process. His recipe. His parameters — the negative-pressure chambers, the
nitrogen cycling, the eleven-day window. Renamed. Reformatted. Resubmitted
with incremental variations small enough to constitute new filings and large
enough to maintain the appearance of ongoing research.

Alpha had been paid billions of dollars to develop technology that already
existed. Technology that a junior researcher had discovered by accident in an
apartment. Technology that the company had then taken, patented, and presented
to the Federation Council as the product of years of sophisticated R&D.

He noted this the way he noted everything: as a data point. He added it to
the notebook.

---

It took him two and a half shifts to find the batch destruction logs.

He was not looking for them. He was auditing throughput across Q3 and Q4 of the
previous year, following Oren's hypothesis about feedstock purity, when he found
a field in the batch records he didn't recognize. The field was labeled
DISPOSAL_PROTOCOL_7 and it appeared at irregular but recurring intervals across
the dataset. He filtered for it.

Three hundred and twelve entries in an eighteen-month window.

Each entry recorded a quantity of crystallized DarkX product, a timestamp, and a
status code. The status codes were internal Alpha nomenclature he didn't
immediately know, but they were consistent, which meant they were systematic,
which meant he could decode them from context. He spent forty minutes doing
this. When he had the schema, he read the entries again.

DISPOSAL_PROTOCOL_7 was not a contamination flag. It was not a quality-control
rejection. The quantities attached to it were not anomalously small or large.
They tracked, with a consistency that was striking in its precision, at roughly
eleven to thirteen percent of each batch's usable yield.

Alpha was destroying product.

Not spoilage. Not error. Consistent, scheduled, deliberate destruction of viable
DarkX crystals representing eleven to thirteen percent of everything this
facility produced.

Max sat with this for thirty seconds. He thought about the supply charts he had
memorized in the weeks since leaving Alpha. Global DarkX supply had run at
approximately four to eight percent below projected demand for eleven
consecutive years. The energy crisis was, in the language of economists, a
demand-side problem: there was not enough supply to meet demand at any
reasonable price point, which drove prices up, which made DarkX unaffordable for
most of the world's population outside of priority industrial contracts.

Eleven to thirteen percent of production destroyed per batch. Four to eight
percent supply gap.

The math was not subtle.

He thought, briefly and precisely, about Dr. Oren. About whether Oren knew. He
looked across the lab to where Oren was bent over a console, reading something,
and he considered the man's face: the quality of attention, the genuine
curiosity, the enthusiasm with which he had explained the nitrogen cycling this
morning. He thought about what it meant to be a good scientist working inside a
machine without knowing what the machine was for.

Then he opened the drive housing on the terminal's secondary port and inserted
the Collective drive, which was the size of a thumbnail and the color of the
housing it was inserted into.

He had three minutes before the batch log session would auto-expire and require
re-authentication.

He worked efficiently.

---

The alarm was not his.

It came from the adjacent lab, a containment alert — the distinctive three-pulse
tone that indicated a pressure failure in a nitrogen cycling unit. Not
catastrophic. Not dangerous. But by protocol, the floor went into incident mode,
and incident mode meant: all non-essential personnel to designated staging
areas, all lab-adjacent doors to automatic lock-and-hold, all badge readers
elevated to tier-two authorization.

Max pulled the drive.

He did this first, before he looked up, before he assessed. The drive was in his
pocket before the second alarm pulse had completed. This was not instinct — it
was the result of having spent three days thinking carefully about what he would
do if something like this happened, which was the same thing he did with every
protocol: he thought through the failure modes in advance so that when they
occurred he did not have to think.

He stood up from the terminal and turned around.

Priya was moving toward the designated staging area near the north wall. Chen
had already crossed the partition into the adjacent section, presumably to
assist with the containment. Three other researchers were moving in the same
direction as Priya, unhurried, the way people move through a practiced drill.

Oren was coming toward Max.

"Nitrogen coupling failed in 14-C," Oren said. "Routine. We'll be in hold for
probably twenty minutes." He gestured toward the staging area. "Come on, I'll
finish walking you through the Q4 anomalies while we wait."

Max did not go toward the staging area.

"I need to use the restroom," he said. "Before the hold locks me in for twenty
minutes."

Oren smiled. "Corridor B, left at the junction. You have maybe two minutes
before it locks."

"I'll be quick," Max said.

He walked at the pace of a man who intended to return.

---

Corridor B was clear. He turned left at the junction and kept his pace even,
noting the badge readers on the doors he passed: the first was already showing
amber, cycling toward red. He did not use Mikof Forsinko's card on that one.

The second door, forty feet further, was still green. He scanned through and
kept moving.

He used the card on one more door after that — a maintenance corridor that ran
parallel to the main shaft, which he had mapped on his first walk-through by the
simple expedient of noticing where the maintenance staff went when they
disappeared from view. The door opened. He walked through and descended two
flights of stairs into a service level that smelled of refrigerant and insulated
cable.

The loading bay exit was at the west end. A man in a gray Alpha uniform was
moving a pallet loader toward the freight elevator, his back to the door. Max
walked past him at a consistent pace, badge in hand though there was no reader
on this door, and pushed through the bay door into the late afternoon.

Panama City in May at four in the afternoon smells of exhaust and something
green and wet under it — the particular vegetable richness of a city that is
fighting a constant battle against tropical encroachment and losing it at the
margins. The light was golden and horizontal. The noise was immediate and
specific: traffic, a vendor's amplified radio somewhere to the right, the
compressed-air grunt of a delivery truck two blocks up.

Max walked.

He did not move fast. A man moving fast from a loading bay draws attention. He
moved at the pace of a contractor who has just finished a shift and is thinking
about dinner. He held his badge lanyard loosely. He did not look behind him.

He was three blocks north before he turned into a side street and allowed
himself to stop.

He stood in the doorway of a hardware store. The smell in here was specific and
known: machine oil, rubber gasket material, the particular dustiness of packed
shelving. Through the window, he could see a man behind a counter watching
something on a screen. Normal afternoon. Nothing looking back at him.

His hands were shaking. He noted this without particular interest. It was the
body's delayed accounting for the last fifteen minutes, which was a known
phenomenon. He put his hands in his pockets, feeling the drive in the right one
and the notebook's corner digging into his ribs from where he had it wedged
under his arm.

Six blocks east. That was the extraction point. He had forty minutes before Red and Squirrel's window closed.

He started walking.
