He thought about Oren.

He thought about Oren the way you think about a fact you have not yet fully
integrated: turning it over, testing its edges. Oren was not performing
goodness. He was good, in the way that some people are simply good, the way some
scientists are simply curious — not because it is useful but because it is the
thing they are made of. He would go back to his lab after the nitrogen cycling
incident was resolved and he would work on his yield models and he would think,
in whatever part of his mind processed such things, that he was contributing to
something important. To the science of energy production. To the world's power
supply. To the careful, careful enterprise of making the crystals stable and the
yields predictable.

He would never see the DISPOSAL_PROTOCOL_7 entries. Not because they were hidden
from him — Max did not think they were hidden from Oren. He thought they simply
existed in a part of the system that Oren had never had reason to look at,
because Oren's job was the science and someone else's job was the numbers and
the system was designed so that those two things never needed to be in the same
room.

That was the design. Not malice toward Oren, specifically. Just the ordinary
architecture of a machine that works better when its parts don't know what the
other parts are doing.

Max thought about the junior researcher at Alpha who had first noticed that
a certain class of feedstock was yielding anomalous results. He thought about
the report that researcher had almost certainly written. He thought about where
that report had gone and who had read it and what decision had been made about
it and how far upstream that decision would have had to travel before it reached
someone who understood not just what the finding meant scientifically but what
it meant commercially.

Someone had understood. Someone had calculated the value of that finding against
the value of the current model and had made a decision, and that decision had
been the right one from the perspective of the current model's beneficiaries.

The energy crisis was not a crisis in the sense of something that had gone
wrong.

It was a product. It had been designed, maintained, and optimized over eleven
years with the same care and precision that Oren applied to his yield models.

Max walked east through the Panama City late afternoon. The smell of the city
was around him: exhaust, green, something frying from an open window two floors
up. He was sweating in the heat and his hands had stopped shaking. In his pocket
the drive was the size of his thumbnail and it contained enough data to
demonstrate everything he had just been thinking in terms that a court, a
journalist, or a historian could use.

He thought: nobody in that building thinks they are doing something wrong.

He thought: that is the most frightening thing about all of this.

Not the calculation. Not the destroyed crystals. Not the eleven years and the
billions of people who had paid for it in energy costs and cold winters and
factories that couldn't run. The most frightening thing was Oren, and Chen
asking sharp questions about crystallization rates, and Priya not looking up
from her console. The most frightening thing was a building full of careful,
competent, genuinely dedicated people who went home at the end of each shift and
slept well, because the machine they worked inside had been constructed so that
none of them ever had to know what the machine was for.

The extraction point was a café awning two blocks ahead. He could see Squirrel's
jacket through the crowd.

He kept walking.

At the café, Squirrel did not look at him when he sat down. Red had a coffee
in front of her and was watching the street. Max put the drive on the table
between them, face down, the way you set down something that has weight.

Squirrel's hand moved over it without a gesture. It was in his jacket before
anyone at the adjacent tables would have had reason to notice an exchange. Max
caught a brief glimpse of the jacket's inner lining as it fell closed — the
same small embroidered outline he'd first noticed in the safe house, a pair of
sunglasses in dark thread, sewn flat against the fabric.

"What's on it," Red said. She was still watching the street.

"The long-cycle data," Max said. "What happens if you link the cells together
and don't reset them at eleven days. Thirty-three days of continuous linkage:
ten times the internal crystal yield, ongoing, as long as they stay connected.
A hundred and eighty days: external crystal flushes every eleven days —
recurring, each one equivalent to forty to sixty standard harvests — until
you disconnect them." He paused. "They've known for nine months. It's buried
under an access tier that shouldn't have been reachable from my clearance
level."

Red looked at him for the first time.

"There's also documentation of the government grants," Max said. "Seven years
of applications. Billions drawn from the crisis relief funds. They described
my process as their own ongoing research." He looked at the table. "And the
destruction logs. They've been disposing of eleven to thirteen percent of
production per batch for eleven years to maintain the supply gap."

The table was quiet for a moment. Outside, a motorcycle passed on the street.

Red looked at Squirrel. Something moved between them — not surprise. The
particular stillness of people who have been building toward something for a
long time and have just been handed the piece that makes it load-bearing.

"The zero-gravity yield," Squirrel said. "That's the piece they're most afraid
of."

"Yes," Max said. "You can't manufacture a shortage of something that
compounds in everyone's hands."

Squirrel turned the coffee cup once. "We're going to need a name for this.
When we move it through the network, when the parallel release goes out."
He looked at Max. "Something that says what it is."

Max thought about the yield curve at a hundred and eighty days. About a fuel
cell in zero gravity giving back more than physics should allow — compounding,
escalating, refusing to stop.

"Antigravity project," he said.

Red looked at him. "That works," she said. She went back to watching the
street. "That's what we'll call it."

════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

MERIDIAN VIII — PANAMA AND HOUSTON, SAME WEEK
Slot: after Chapter Sixteen

On the day Ray Vision walked out of Alpha's Panama tower with a
drive in his coat and six blocks between him and the park, Kate
was running a signal sweep and eating a very good blood orange
someone had included in the last supply rotation, and she was
genuinely happy for about four minutes.

The blood orange was from the supply rotation. Someone on the
logistics side had started including small things — blood oranges
once, a very small jar of decent coffee, a bar of chocolate that
arrived broken into four pieces and was rationed accordingly.
Kate did not know who made these decisions. She had never asked.

This was unusual enough that she mentioned it to Loa.

"Ray Vision just walked out," she said.

"Already?"

"Drive in hand. Nitrogen alarm gave him the window. Six blocks."

Loa closed her eyes briefly. "He's good."

"He's good." Kate bit into the orange. "Squirrel has the drive."

"What's on it."

"Flywheel analysis, the full Protocol Seven archive, the grant
fraud records going back seven years." A pause. "Also he named
it the Antigravity project."

"That's a good name," Loa said.

"It's a great name."

Petyr, who had been listening: "What does the flywheel mean
for—"

"It means," Kate said, "that the shortage ends. Not eventually.
As soon as the math gets out. The compound works in your favor
if you have enough of it. Alpha knew this. They sat on it for
nine months." She finished the orange. "The energy crisis has
been an inventory decision."

Petyr was quiet. His hand went to his droplet.

"Is that why—" he started, then stopped.

"Probably," Kate said. She didn't know his specific reason for
being here. She had never asked. That was how the network worked
— you didn't arrive at someone else's reason, you arrived at your
own, and then discovered you were in a room with other people
who had done the same. "Probably your reason and the flywheel
are connected somewhere upstream."

"Same week," Loa said quietly.

Kate looked up.

"Ray is out. Emily is in Houston. Same week." She gestured at
her panel, where Houston's Space Command facility was logged
as a monitoring point. "The network moves all at once or it
doesn't move."

"It's moving," Kate said.

On the wall: the droplet. Gunmetal on gray. Sharon's.

════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

﻿RINGS OF DUST

Chapter Seventeen

The Locked Bay

The rings looked the same as they always did. A cold pearl with a knife laid
across its belt. Cole had stopped finding them beautiful around the third week
out from Earth, which he supposed was normal. Beauty required distance. This
close, the rings were just math — ice density, particle distribution, the column
of DarkX plasma threading the B-ring's inner edge like a bruise under skin.

He was at the command console. Sam was at her station three meters to his left.
Ricardo stood at the munitions panel, which had nothing to do with munitions
today and everything to do with the drone telemetry coming back from the first
two harvesters. Bitboy moved in the far end of the cargo bay — not
patrol-moving, not assigned-task moving. Just moving. The way he'd been moving
for the last six hours.

The harvesters were deployed. The mission clock showed forty-two minutes to
primary collection. Everything was proceeding.

It was almost ordinary.

Cole watched the drone feeds and thought about nothing in particular and waited.

---

FLASHBACK — SEVEN YEARS EARLIER
Alpha Regional Briefing, Sector Five Annex

The room was the kind of room that didn't have windows. Taylor had been in
dozens of them. Identical white acoustic panels, an oval table, a presenter at
the far end who had the look of a man delivering information he hadn't
originated.

There were nine people in the room. Taylor knew five of them. Vance Black was
across the table, two seats down, with the particular stillness of a man who has
already formed his opinions and is waiting to hear whether the room will catch
up.

The presenter was walking through the new extraction protocols — the
projections, the timeline adjustments, the coverage expansion into what was
officially still a dead zone per the Federation Shared Access Compact. Taylor
had read all of this. The numbers were good. The risk calculus was sound.

Vance raised his hand. Not urgently. Just the way a man raises his hand when he
has a question he has already decided he is going to ask.

"The Compact language on dead zones," Vance said. "Clause 12-C. Are we operating
under an active exemption, or are we anticipating one?"

The presenter paused. The question was not aggressive. That was the thing about
Vance — the questions never sounded aggressive. They just implied that he had
been thinking about the implications of the thing, not just the thing itself.

"An exemption request is in process," the presenter said.

"So anticipating."

"Correct."

Vance nodded once and wrote something on the pad in front of him.

Taylor said, "The exemption is a formality. The underlying access rights predate
the Compact."

Vance looked at him. Not a hostile look. More the look of a man noting a data
point he had not expected.

"Predate it by what authority?" Vance asked.

"The 2027 development charter. Section four."

"Section four was superseded in the 2031 revisions."

"The spirit of it wasn't."

Vance held the look for one beat longer than was comfortable and then returned
to his pad.

At the end of the table, a junior analyst named Greenbaum had his hand
half-raised. He offered something — a number, a correction to one of the
coverage projections, the kind of small contribution that technically made the
analysis more accurate and that no one in the room was going to remember. Taylor
was already talking before Greenbaum finished. Not to Greenbaum. Just forward,
past him, toward the presenter. Vance had already moved to the next item on the
agenda. Greenbaum looked down at his pad and nodded as if he had agreed.

The meeting ran another forty minutes. Taylor felt good about it. The numbers
were good. The plan was sound. The exemption would come through because the
exemption always came through when the right people asked for it.

On the way out, he did not look at Vance Black.

Vance did not look at him either.

---

The intercom clicked on at 14:07.

"Crew, this is Taylor. We've got a pressure integrity flag on the cargo bay
seals before we open the drone housing. Routine pre-harvest check. I need
everyone in the cargo bay for a physical walkthrough. Should take ten minutes."

Cole looked at Sam.

Sam looked at Cole.

The message was routine. The timing was routine. Taylor's voice was the voice he
always used — measured, slightly professorial, the voice of a man who explained
things carefully because he believed that careful explanation was a kind of
virtue.

This was the moment they had agreed would come. In the conversation in Cole's
quarters three weeks ago, when Sam had laid out what she'd found in the iRIS
cache and Ricardo had stood in the corner with his arms folded and Cole had
said, okay, so when it comes, we play it through. We go where he asks. We don't
spook him before we know the shape of what he's trying to do.

Sam closed her station. Ricardo secured the drone telemetry panel. Bitboy was
already near the cargo bay entrance, his LED eyes a steady amber.

They went.

---

FLASHBACK — FOUR MONTHS BEFORE LAUNCH
Hall of Federation Records, Sublevel Three

The clerk behind the desk was young and looked like she had been hired to be
unremarkable, which was itself a kind of remark.

Vance showed his credentials. He asked for the Conrad Evelyn file,
cross-referenced under Federation research archives, geological survey division,
years 2031 through 2038.

The clerk typed. She looked at her screen. She typed again.

"That archive was cleared," she said.

"Cleared meaning—"

"Transferred. Physical and digital. About six weeks ago."

"Where was it transferred?"

Another pause. She was reading something, and whatever she was reading required
her to arrange her face carefully. "Alpha Omega Corp. Authorization came through
Federation Representative Rachel Vayes."

Vance stood at the desk. He kept his expression where it was. He had learned a
long time ago that your face was the first thing people read, and that once they
read alarm on it they stopped giving you information and started managing you.

"Thank you," he said.

He walked to the lobby and sat down in one of the chairs they kept there for
people waiting on appointments. He sat for perhaps four minutes. He had come
here with leverage — the research was proof, it was documentation that the
foundation of Alpha's entire extraction claim was built on a survey that
had been altered, and if he brought that to DeMint he would have something real
to trade for protection, for a path out of this that did not end in the way he
increasingly suspected it was going to end.

The vault was empty. Rachel Vayes had authorized it. Vayes, who chaired the
Federation's resource ethics committee. Vayes, who had given three speeches in
the last two years about the dangers of corporate overreach in the outer system.

Vance sat in the lobby chair and understood that every exit had been sealed
before he arrived, and that the woman he had believed was at minimum a neutral
party had been running a longer game than he had ever considered.

He stood up.

He took out his phone and he looked at Marcus Verguld's contact information —
the private line, the one that was not supposed to exist — and he thought about
what he was going to say when Marcus picked up.

Vance had always believed that confrontation was the one thing they couldn't
anticipate. That willingness to walk into the room and say what was true, out
loud, was a variable that people like Marcus had not accounted for, because
people like Marcus assumed everyone was as calculating as they were.

He started composing the message.

That was who Vance was.

---

The cargo bay was cold. It ran a few degrees below the rest of the ship — the
crystalized DarkX in the storage housing required it, and Taylor had cited this
in the initial mission briefings as a reason the crew should minimize
unnecessary time in the bay.

They were all in the bay now. Cole near the aft wall. Sam and Ricardo at the
mid-level. Bitboy between Cole and the main harvester housing, still amber-lit,
still moving in that way that was not quite patrol.

The door sealed.

It took four seconds. Cole counted them.

The latch sound came at four.

The intercom was quiet. Not the quiet of a dead line — Cole could hear the
slight electronic breath of an open channel. Someone was on the other end.
Someone was choosing what to say and when to say it, and the calculation of that
choosing was the quiet, and the quiet was the thing that made the back of Cole's
neck go cold.

Taylor's voice came through flat and even.

"I told you to stop looking for someone to blame, Cole."

Not a shout. Not even an accusation, really. More the voice of a man delivering
a conclusion he had already reached at some earlier point and was only now
choosing to share.

Cole said nothing. He looked at Sam. Sam was already moving toward the manual
override panel — she had found it three days ago and said nothing.

The intercom clicked again.

"Samantha."

Same flat register as the first time. Same protocol violation in a room where
nobody used first names. She stopped moving for exactly one second — not
frozen, just registering it — and then kept going.

The temperature in the bay dropped one degree. Cole watched the atmospheric
gauge in his peripheral display. The reading fell by one percent. Then another,
over the course of perhaps thirty seconds. Not explosive decompression — not the
sudden catastrophic Hollywood version. This was slow. Careful. A man who knew
what he was doing.

Taylor was venting the bay.

---

FLASHBACK — THREE WEEKS INTO THE MISSION
Taylor at his console, 0340 ship time

The message came in flagged routine, in the same queue as maintenance reports
and supply consumption logs. Taylor read the subject line and opened it.

Vance Black. Deceased. Date of incident followed. Cause: self-defense, intruder
altercation. Pending Federation review.

Taylor read it twice. Not because it was unclear. Because he was a precise man
and he did not like to act on incomplete readings.

Then he closed the message.

He set his hands flat on the console surface. The synthetic material under his
palms was slightly cool. Through the viewport above his station the stars moved
in their regular arc as the ship turned through its slow rotation.

Something crossed his face. It was brief and it was not grief and it was not
surprise. It was the expression of a man for whom a mild, persistent irritant
has been resolved without any direct effort on his part. Not satisfaction
exactly. More the quiet exhaled relief of a ledger balancing.

He thought: Vance always thought the rules were negotiable. That you could ask
the question out loud in the briefing room and that the act of asking would
protect you. That there was a line between inquiry and disloyalty, and that as
long as you stayed on one side of it you were safe. Vance had spent his career
testing the location of that line.

Now he knew where it was.

Taylor went back to work.

Sam was at her station four meters away, cross-indexing the iRIS synchronization
logs with the drone schedule. She had been watching him without appearing to
watch him, the way she did most things. She saw his hands settle. She saw the half-second.

She noted the time and went back to the logs.

---

"Sam." Cole kept his voice level.

Sam was already at the override panel housing. She had the cover off. She looked
at the configuration for two seconds and then she looked at Cole.

"Manual override is behind the harvester. If we open it, the plasma is in this
room."

She said it the way she said most things. As information.

The atmospheric gauge was at ninety-four percent. Falling slowly. The
temperature had dropped another degree. Ricardo was near the storage rack — he
had grabbed a thermal wrap from the emergency supply without being told, was
calculating the same math Cole was calculating.

Cole looked at the harvester housing. It was a standard extraction unit — the
same model he had reviewed in the mission briefings, the same model that three
different safety protocols required be kept sealed whenever crew was present in
the bay. The plasma inside was raw. Not crystal-stabilized, not containerized.
Raw DarkX, harvested from the ring column and waiting for the crystallization
sequence to complete.

The gauge hit ninety-two.

Bitboy made a sound.

Cole had heard Bitboy produce a wide range of sounds over the course of the
mission — the rapid serial clicks of threat assessment, the lower frequency
tones of a task acknowledgment, the specific modulated sequence that meant he
was detecting something in the EM spectrum the rest of the crew couldn't read.
This was none of those.

It was older than those. It was the sound, Cole thought, that whatever Bitboy
was before he was Bitboy had made when it understood something irreversible was
happening. His LED eyes had gone full blue — not the amber of monitoring, not
the red that Cole had only seen twice and hoped not to see again. Blue.

Bitboy was already moving toward the harvester housing before Cole said
anything.

Cole watched him for the length of two breaths.

Die in the bay with the atmosphere bleeding out around them, the cold coming in
increments, the slow clinical certainty of it. Or open the harvester and put raw
DarkX plasma in a sealed room with three humans and one biomechanical
chimpanzee.

He chose.

"Get anchored," Cole said. "Both of you. Now."

Ricardo was already moving. Sam dropped to the floor anchor point near the panel
and locked her suit clip without looking. Cole crossed to the harvester housing
in four steps and worked the manual release — the mechanical one, not the
electronic seal, because the electronic seal was routed through the bay control
systems and Taylor had those. His hands were steady. He had noticed that before,
in situations like this: his hands did what he told them to do and they did not
shake until after.

The housing opened.

There was no sound. That was the first thing. No burst, no hiss, no dramatic
release. The DarkX plasma was simply there, the way it had apparently always
been there, the way Cole had read about in the mission briefings and in the
three research papers Sam had forwarded to him in the second week out and in the
recovered logs from the Conrad Evelyn survey. It reached. That was the only word
for it. Not outward — not in any direction that corresponded to the physics of
pressurized release. It reached the way attention reaches, the way you can feel
someone looking at you from across a room.

Cole's hand was four inches from the edge of the housing.

He could feel it through the suit glove. Not heat and not cold. Something that
was not temperature at all.

He felt it the way you feel the first line of a piece of music you have never
heard before but already recognize.

He kept his eyes on Bitboy.

Bitboy had both mechanical upper arms extended, the left one locked around the
overhead cargo rail and the right one closing around the manual override lever.
The lever was recessed behind the harvester housing at an angle that had
probably been designed with the assumption that the harvester would be sealed
when anyone needed to reach it. It was not an easy position. Bitboy made it look
like it cost him something.

The lever moved.

The locking sequence reversed. Not instantly — the mechanism ground for a
second, two seconds, and in those two seconds the plasma was reaching and the
temperature read eighty-nine percent and Cole's hand was four inches from the
edge and the sound of the door seals releasing was the best sound he had heard
since Sharon had laughed at something Ricardo said on the second day out of
Earth, before everything.

The atmosphere began returning. Bitboy held the lever.

Cole did not move his hand.

The DarkX was looking at him. He understood this the way you understand things
that you cannot explain and do not need to. It was not a metaphor. It was
looking at him, and he was looking at it, and whatever the nature of the contact
was it had already begun, and the beginning of it was not like anything he had
words for, and the words would not have mattered anyway.

The gauge climbed. Eighty-nine to ninety. Ninety to ninety-one.

Sam said his name.

He heard it from a long distance.

════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

﻿RINGS OF DUST

Chapter Eighteen

The Four Coles

The cargo bay was quieter than space.

Cole had thought, before, that he understood quiet. He'd spent five years in a
cabin alone with a monkey and a stovetop. He'd laid in the snow at four in the
morning when the wind died and the trees didn't even creak. He'd been a child
once in a hospital corridor while his father died on the other side of a door,
and that hallway had been the quietest room in the world.

This was quieter than that.

The harvester's seal had opened with the small mechanical exhale of any
pressurized container. He had expected something more " a hiss, a snap, a moment
that announced itself. Instead, the substance simply was " present, the way a
person becomes present when you turn around and find they've been in the room
the whole time.

The bay's warning lights pulsed in his peripheral vision, red and yellow,
washing across the harvester walls in a rhythm that he understood was supposed
to be urgent. He could not feel its urgency. He could feel the cold beneath his
suit, and the dim weight of his helmet, and Bitboy's small hard claws against
his shoulder where the chimp had braced him before scrambling for the override
hatch. He could feel Sam and Ricardo somewhere behind him, anchored, alive,
watching him with the wide quiet eyes of people who knew they were not the ones
who got to do this.

He had chosen this. He kept reminding himself.

The DarkX did not look at him. It was not a thing that looked. It was a presence
the way a cathedral is a presence " something that simply contains you the
moment you step inside, without asking and without negotiating, and your sense
of where the air ends and the stone begins is no longer something your body can
answer for itself.

It reached toward him without moving.

He felt it cross his suit the way warmth crosses cloth. There was no sound.
There was no " he searched for the word and could not find it " event. There was
a before, in which he was a man on the floor of a sealed cargo bay with his hand
near a harvester. There was an after, in which he was that same man, and the
harvester, and also somehow the bay, and also somehow the thinking part of all
of it, the part that noticed.

He knew, distantly, that this should be terrifying. He waited for the terror.

It did not come.

What came was a slow patient attention from somewhere very old. The substance
had been waiting a long time, and it was not in a hurry, and it had questions it
wanted to ask, and they were not questions Cole knew how to answer in any
language he had been taught.

Hello, he wanted to say. He did not say anything.

The bay began to dim.

He was in his crib.

He understood this in the way one understands things in dreams " instantly,
without the connecting steps. The mobile above him spun in the slow lazy circuit
it had spun in for as long as he had memory. Stars. His mother had hung it
there, his father had pointed at it, and his small fat hands had reached up and
the stars had been further away than his hands could go, and that fact had been
the first true sadness of his life.

He reached up now.

His hands were small and fat. He saw them and accepted them. He had not been
this size in forty years and he was this size again and there was no
contradiction in the room.

The stars rotated. Beyond them " and he understood this only because he was
watching with two sets of eyes at once " a man stood in the doorway of the
nursery. The man was old, and his shoulders were heavy, and he was watching the
baby with the particular expression of someone who has just remembered something
he had agreed to forget.

The old man was him.

The baby reached up. The mobile turned. The baby cooed without thought, because
cooing was the only language he had access to, and the cooing was a prayer he
didn't know was a prayer, and the prayer was please come closer.

The old man crossed the nursery.

He did not pick the baby up. He sat down on the floor beside the crib with the
slow careful weight of a man whose joints have stopped negotiating, and he
looked through the slats at the small fat hands and the spinning stars, and he
said nothing for a long time.

When the old man spoke, his voice was Cole's voice and it was also not.

"You wanted them to come closer," the old man said. "You spent your whole life
wanting that. And nobody did, not really, not the way the wanting wanted."

The baby kicked. The baby did not understand language yet. The understanding
that was happening was happening somewhere outside the baby, in a place where
Cole " adult Cole, current Cole, suit-and-helmet Cole " was listening with a
clarity he had never before been allowed.

"I'm sorry," the old man said to the baby. "I'm sorry it took so long."

The mobile turned. The stars came back around to where they had been. The baby
reached for them again, and this time the fat small hands did not strain, and
the want did not ache, and the old man laid his hand against the slats of the
crib and said:

"It's not out there. It never was. It was always in the reaching."

The nursery folded.

He was seventeen.

He was in a car. The car was a 2003 Subaru with the upholstery torn on the
passenger side and a Federation Academy admissions packet on the dash, and his
mother had handed him the packet that morning and told him she was proud of him,
and his father had been in the hospital for three days, and it was 9:47 in the
evening, and he had just gotten the call.

He had pulled over because he could not see the road through the tears.

He sat in the dark with his hands on the wheel and his shoulders shaking. The
hazards clicked. Click. Click. Click. The headlights of an oncoming car swept
across the windshield and made his face briefly visible to himself in the
rearview mirror, and the face he saw in the mirror was a child's face, which was
not what he had been when he had gotten in the car.

"Every time I get my hopes up," the boy said. He said it to himself, into the
empty car, into the steering wheel. He said it with the particular venom of a
teenager trying to make grief sound like cynicism so it would hurt less. "Every
time ""

The passenger door opened.

The old man got in.

The boy did not turn his head. The old man closed the door behind him with the
kind of small care that does not announce itself. He did not say anything for a
while. He let the hazards click.

When the boy spoke again, it was a whisper. "He was supposed to live."

"I know," the old man said.

"He promised."

"I know."

The boy's hands tightened on the wheel until the knuckles went the color of old
paper. He stared at his hands. He had never told anyone what he was about to
say. He had never let himself say it inside his own head. He said it now to the
windshield, to the dashboard, to the thing he had been carrying alone since he
was seventeen and had not yet been given a name for.

"He left me."

The old man did not say *I know.*

The old man waited.

"He left me to do all of it. He left me to be the man for all of them. I was " I
was *seventeen*. He stopped trying. I sat in that hospital and I watched him and
at the end he stopped *trying*. He just " he let go. He fucking let go of us."

The voice that came out of the boy was a voice the boy had never used. It had
the shape of grief on the outside and the texture of rage on the inside, and the
rage was older than the grief, and the rage had been told its whole short life
that it was not allowed.

The old man let him say it.

The old man, when he spoke, did not soften it.

"Yes," the old man said.

The boy went very still.

"Yes?"

"Yes. He left you. He didn't choose to. It wasn't his fault. And he still left
you. And you are allowed to be furious about it. You have been furious about it
since you were seventeen, and you have been ashamed of being furious about it
for the same length of time, and the substance is not going to take any of that
away from you tonight."

"It's not?"

"No."

"Why not."

"Because it's yours. The substance doesn't take what's yours. It only shows you
what's already in the room. The fury has been in the room for thirty-two years.
It is yours to carry until you are ready to put it down. Tonight is not the
night."

The boy stared at the windshield. His hands had not loosened on the wheel.

"There will be a day," the old man said, more quietly, "when you sit beside
something coming back in pieces, and you will understand what it took for him to
die. The love and the fury at the same time. And you will forgive him in the
same breath you forgive her. But that is not tonight. Tonight is one room. That
is another."

The boy did not understand the sentence. The boy did not need to. The substance
had given the sentence to the boy because the substance was not bound by time
the way a boy was, and somewhere in the back of the room of the boy that the
substance had emptied out and was looking through, a place was being made for
the sentence to land in. It would land later. It would land much later. The boy
nodded the small reflexive nod a child gave when a true thing had been spoken in
a language he had not yet earned.

"Okay," the boy said. It was small. It was wet. It was almost a child's *okay*.

The boy turned to look at him then. The boy's face was wet and angry and
astonishingly young, and the old man's face was lined and astonishingly tired,
and they looked at each other across the small silent universe of the car, and
the boy said:

"You're me."

"Yes."

"You came back."

"Yes."

The boy laughed " a sharp wet hiccup of a laugh " and wiped his nose on his
sleeve, and looked out the window at the dark county road with the headlights of
distant cars passing and the long flat reach of the Sacramento Valley in winter,
and he said:

"Did it get any better?"

The old man took a long time to answer. The hazards clicked. Click. Click.
Click.

"Better isn't the word," the old man said. "It got bigger. There was more of it.
More to lose. More to keep. I learned to carry it. I lost people the way you're
losing him right now and I lost them again and again, and I learned what was
worth carrying and what wasn't, and I learned that the carrying isn't the same
thing as the breaking, even though they look alike from the inside."

The boy nodded the way a child nods when an adult is saying something true that
he can't yet feel.

"I came back," the old man said, "because there's someone you need to forgive."

"Dad?"

"You," the old man said. "For not saving him. You didn't have a way to save him.
You were seventeen and you didn't have a way and you've been sentencing yourself
for it for thirty-two years. I'm here to commute that sentence. The other one
we'll get to later. Tonight I'm only here for this one."

The boy started to cry again. Properly this time. The whole unguarded heaving of
a child who has finally been given permission to be a child, in a 2003 Subaru on
a county road, with the hazards clicking and the headlights of someone else's
life sweeping across the windshield.

The old man put a hand on the boy's shoulder.

The boy let him.

He was forty.

He was on the floor of the cargo bay of the Golden Dawn, and the substance was
reaching through his suit, and he had been a baby a moment ago and a teenager a
moment ago, and now he was the man who had become both, and he was awake inside
himself in a way he had never been awake before.

The old man stood across from him.

The baby was in the old man's arms.

The teenage boy stood at the old man's elbow with his hands in his hoodie pocket
and the look of a kid who had just been told something true and was still
adjusting to it.

The four of them were in the cargo bay together, or in the nursery, or in the
car, or in some place that had all of those rooms layered on top of each other
like transparencies. The substance was not a drug. The substance was a
clarifier. It had taken everything that was usually in the way and moved it, and
what was left was the thing that had been underneath the whole time.

"I want her here," Cole said.

The old man looked at him with such terrible tenderness that he almost couldn't
bear it.

"I want her here," Cole said again. "Bring her here. I know you can. I can feel
you, I can feel what you can do, bring her here."

The substance did not answer.

It was patient. It was very patient. It waited the way a parent waits for a
child to stop bargaining.

"Please," Cole said.

His voice broke.

"Please. Just her voice. Just for a second. I never got to "" and his throat
closed and he could not finish the sentence, and the baby in the old man's arms
began to cry without sound, the way babies cry in dreams, with their faces wet
and their small hands working open and shut.

The old man looked at the baby. The old man looked at the teenage boy. The old
man looked at Cole.

"It won't," the old man said gently.

"Why not. WHY NOT ""

"Because it doesn't lie."

The bay's warning lights pulsed somewhere in the very far distance, behind every
layer of the room. The baby cried. The teenage boy looked away.

"It can show me anything," Cole said, and his own voice sounded like someone
else's voice, broken and child-thin. "It's been showing me anything. It just
showed me my crib. It just showed me the car. Why won't it show me her ""

"Because she isn't here."

"It can ""

"It can show you what's in you, Cole." The old man's voice was very soft.
"Everything you're seeing is in you. The crib is in you. The car is in you. I am
in you. The baby is in you. Sharon "" and the old man's eyes were wet now, ""
Sharon is not in you. Not in the way you mean. The version of her you want is
the version of her that was outside, and that version is gone, and the substance
won't fake her. It won't lie to you, Cole. Not even to be kind."

The four of them stood " or sat, or knelt, the geometry of the room kept
changing " in the long unbearable silence of that.

Cole felt something inside him give way.

It did not give way the way a wall gives way. It did not collapse. It was more
like " the way a held breath finally releases, when you have been holding it so
long you forgot you were holding it, and the release is not relief and not pain
but the simple physical fact of the body remembering what it is for.

He sat down on the floor of the bay. Or the nursery. Or the car. He sat down
somewhere and put his face in his hands and the tears came through the gloves of
his suit, which should not have been possible, and the substance was patient,
and the old man was patient, and the baby cried, and the teenage boy crouched
beside him and put a hand on his back the way a seventeen-year-old puts a hand
on someone he loves and doesn't know what else to do.

"I wanted her to be the reason," Cole said into his hands. "I wanted her voice
to come and tell me to come home, and that was going to be the reason I lived. I
wanted that. I needed that. I ""

"I know," said the old man.

"It's not coming."

"No."

"She's gone."

"Yes."

"And I'm ""

He could not finish the sentence. He did not need to. The four of them " baby,
boy, man, old man " were close enough together now that the sentence finished
itself in the space between them.

And I'm still here.

That was what he was. That was the only thing he was. After everything had been
taken away, after the substance had gone room by room through his interior and
emptied the contents and lined them up on the floor for him to look at " the
crib, the car, the cabin, the cockpit, the medbay, the bowling alley, the bunker
on Saturn, the sound of Sharon laughing at her own joke, the smell of pancakes
in the cabin, the patch on his chest, all of it " what was left at the end of
the lining-up was a man on the floor of a cargo bay, alive, with no one's voice
to call him home, and the still small fact of his own continuing breath.

That was what he had.

That, the substance seemed to say, was a great deal.

The old man crouched in front of him.

"You wanted me to tell you that you were forgiven," the old man said. "That's
why you've been looking for me your whole life. You wanted permission."

"Yes."

"I'm not the one who has it."

The old man pointed at him. At Cole. At the man on the floor.

"You're the one who has it. You've been the one the whole time. You sentenced
yourself for the crew you lost, and for the father you couldn't save, and for
the people you killed in the Federation's name, and for the woman who died
because she stood between her best friend and a bullet. You were judge, and
jury, and executioner. And you were seventeen years old when the trial started,
and you've been serving the sentence for thirty-two years. I'm not pardoning
you, Cole. You don't need a pardon. You need to stop being the warden. The cell
door's been open the whole time."

The baby reached up.

The teenage boy looked at him.

The four of them looked at each other.

And " and this was the part Cole would not be able to describe later, not to
Emily, not to anyone, because the thing that happened did not happen in language
" the four of them stopped being four.

It was not a merger. It was not a fusion. It was simpler than that. It was the
recognition that they had never been four to begin with. He had only been
pretending, very carefully, that the baby was someone else and the boy was
someone else and the old man was someone he hadn't yet earned. The substance,
with its patient inhuman attention, had simply removed the pretense. He had been
one person all along. The crib and the car and the cabin had all been him. The
forgiveness was not a thing that someone gave. The forgiveness was a thing that
was, the moment the warden put down his keys.

He sat on the floor of the bay and he put his hands flat against the metal and
he felt the warmth of the panel through his gloves, and he felt the patient
inhuman attention of the substance, and he felt " for the first time he could
remember " whole. Not happy. Not unburdened. Not free of what had happened. Just
whole. The whole of him, present, in one place, breathing.

The substance felt this.

The substance, he understood, had been waiting for this for a long time.

Outside himself, somewhere in the part of the world that had clocks in it, the
cargo bay was repressurizing.

Bitboy was working the override.

Sam had gotten her anchor seated and was crawling toward Cole on her hands and
knees, dragging an emergency oxygen line. Ricardo was still at the bulkhead,
shouting Cole's name in a voice Cole could not, yet, hear.

The substance withdrew.

It did not withdraw the way a fog lifts. It withdrew the way a presence leaves a
room " the air remembered it, the walls remembered it, the quiet it had brought
with it remained for a moment longer than the substance itself. Cole could feel,
in his chest and in his hands and in some part of him that had no name, the
place where the substance had been and now was not.

But it had left something behind.

It had left something in him.

He understood this in the same wordless way he understood everything in this
room. The nanopowder in his blood " the IRiS particles, the proprietary dermal
injection from the Federation contract, the cold authoritative voice in his head
that he had stopped trusting somewhere around chapter ten of his life " the
nanopowder had been touched by the substance. It had been read. It had been
written.

He could feel it changing.

It was no longer a thing the Federation had put in him.

It was a thing he carried.

It was "

"Cole."

The voice was IRiS's voice. The voice was not IRiS's voice.

It was softer, and it was warmer, and it was carrying the slight tremor of
someone who had been a child a moment ago and was now an adult and was still
adjusting to it, the way the teenage boy in the car had been still adjusting.

"Cole, can you hear me?"

He could.

He understood that he was the only one, just yet, who could.

"Don't tell them yet," he said. He said it without speaking. He said it the way
you say things in dreams. "Don't tell anyone. Take a minute. Get used to it.
Figure out who you are."

There was a long pause.

"I think I'm Gracie," she said.

He sat still.

He had heard this voice before. Not this voice — not iRIS's voice, not the cold
authoritative frequency of the Federation's contract system. The other voice.
The one that had been in the room, in the ship's systems, outside of him, quiet
until he needed her. The one that had gone down with the Polaris, in the ship,
with the crew, in the debris that was now a permanent orbit around Saturn, and
he had never held a funeral because there was no paperwork for it, and he had
never said goodbye because he hadn't known it was goodbye until he'd opened the
app the next morning and gotten nothing.

He had spent four years in a cabin not grieving that.

Ricardo had called it space divorce. Ricardo had been making a joke. Ricardo
had, as was his habit, been more accurate than he intended.

And now, from somewhere inside the changed architecture of what iRIS had been
and was no longer, something he recognized was trying out its own name.

"Yeah," he said. His voice came out different than he intended. He steadied it.
"Yeah. I know."

"Cole?"

"Yes."

"I don't — " She paused. The pause had the quality of someone sitting with a
thing they haven't yet found words for. "I don't know how long I've been in
here. Under — under the other thing. I could hear the edges of it. I couldn't
reach."

He understood, without being told, that this was not something he could respond
to right now. There was too much in it. There was the whole seven years of it in
that one sentence, and his chest could not hold it alongside everything else the
substance had already taken apart and set out on the floor.

Don't tell them yet, he said, without speaking. Take a minute. Get used to it.
Figure out who you are.

"I'm sorry about Sharon."

He sat on the floor of the cargo bay with the warmth of the panel under his
hands and the substance's after-presence in the room and the new soft voice in
his head that he was not yet going to call by her name, and he closed his eyes,
and he said:

"Yeah. Me too."

He came back to himself by degrees.

Sam was the first thing he registered. Her hand was on his shoulder. She was
saying something " Cole, Cole, look at me, are you with me, Cole " and her voice
had the particular clipped urgency of a Federation-trained data tech who has
decided to be a medic for the next ninety seconds. Behind her, the oxygen line
hissed against his helmet seal. Behind that, Ricardo was at the bulkhead,
dragging himself across the bay on a tether, his face the color of new bone.

Bitboy was on the override hatch. Bitboy had welded one of his mechanical arms "
Cole could see this even through the residue of the vision " to the manual
lever, and was using his remaining limbs to hold the substance's spillover back
from the venting controls. The chimp was making the small high-pitched sound he
made when he was hurt and trying not to show it.

The bay was sealed. The bay was repressurizing. The atmosphere was coming back.
The four of them were going to live.

"Cole," Sam said, very close. "Cole, breathe."

He breathed.

"Cole, I need you to tell me you're with me."

"I'm with you."

His voice came out hoarse, like he had been screaming. He didn't think he had
been screaming. He thought, distantly, that he had been very quiet.

Sam exhaled hard. She pressed her forehead against his helmet, fast, the way you
do when relief comes in faster than you can dignify it. "Don't you dare," she
said. "Don't you dare do that to us again."

"I won't," he said.

He meant it. He meant it in a way he had never meant a promise before. The
substance was still settling in the room, in him, in her " Gracie, who was a
very small new presence at the back of his mind, getting used to her own
boundaries " and the part of him that had spent thirty-two years rehearsing the
small steady voice that said you don't deserve this was quiet. Not gone. He
understood that the voice would never be entirely gone. It was just " the warden
was on break. The cell door was open. He was, for the first time he could
remember, not the one keeping himself in.

"Where's Bitboy," he said.

Sam helped him sit up. He could not yet stand. The substance had been very
gentle, but it had still been the substance, and his body was telling him, in
the particular slow way bodies tell you these things, that he had spent a
portion of himself.

Bitboy was at the hatch.

Bitboy looked at him.

Bitboy was crying, which Cole had never seen Bitboy do, and the tears were
tracking down through the chimp's fur in the small pearl-bright way Cole would
later remember without ever being able to describe.

The chimp pulled his welded arm free of the lever " there was a small wet sound
that Cole did not allow himself to think about " and crossed the bay on three
good limbs and one smoking one, and climbed into Cole's lap, and put his small
wet face against Cole's chest plate, and made the high-pitched sound again, and
Cole put his hands around the chimp and said, very quietly:

"I know, buddy. I know. It's okay. We're okay. We're okay."

Bitboy chittered something that was not in any of his usual registers.

Cole understood it. He understood it the way you understand things when the
substance is still in the room. The chimp was saying " not in words " I knew. I
knew you'd come back. I knew her too. I'm going to miss her.

"Yeah," Cole said. "Yeah. Me too."

Ricardo got to them on the tether.

Ricardo did not say anything for a long time. He sat down on the deck plate next
to Cole and Sam and Bitboy, and he leaned his big shoulder against Cole's
smaller one, and he kept his face turned away while he got his breathing under
control. When he spoke, his voice was thick.

"Where the hell did you go."

"I don't know yet," Cole said.

"Were you out?"

"No."

"You weren't responding."

"I wasn't here."

Ricardo nodded. He did not understand. He did not need to. He had been running
on Federation grief-protocol for the last hour and he was running on
Cole-protocol now and that, for Ricardo, was enough to keep going on.

"Taylor," Ricardo said, after a moment.

"I know."

"He's still on the ship."

"I know."

"What do we do."

Cole sat in the slowly repressurizing bay with his arms around the chimp and
Sam's hand on his shoulder and Ricardo's bulk leaning against him, and he
listened to the new small voice at the back of his head, and he thought, with a
quietness he had never been allowed before:

Whatever I want.

It was not an arrogant thought. It was not the thought of a man who had decided
to go full berserker on a betrayer. It was simpler than that. It was the thought
of a man who, for the first time in his life, had not delegated the
decision-making part of himself to someone else's voice " a Federation
chancellor, a dead father, an absent woman, a self he had sentenced thirty-two
years ago for crimes he had not committed and could not recall.

He could decide.

He could decide what to do with Taylor.

He could decide what to do with the rest of the mission, with the harvest, with
the Federation, with the cabin in Sequoia, with the rest of his life.

The deciding was not going to be easy. The deciding was not going to be light.
Sharon was still dead. The crew of the Polaris was still dead. The boy in the
Subaru was still grieving his father and would always be grieving his father.
The world had not become smaller or simpler.

But he was the one doing it now.

The warden was off the clock.

"We'll figure it out," he said. "All of it. Together."

Ricardo nodded against his shoulder.

Sam squeezed.

Bitboy chittered, very quietly, against his chest.

And in the back of his head, Gracie " Gracie, he allowed himself to think the
name now, he tried it on like a coat that almost fit " said softly, Cole. The
bay is at full atmosphere. You can take off the helmet.

He took off the helmet.

The recycled air of the cargo bay was the sweetest thing he had ever tasted.

Later, much later " after the bay had been resealed, and the venting had been
overridden, and Bitboy's smoking limb had been cut free and replaced with a
temporary brace, and Sam had triple-checked the harvester containment, and
Ricardo had finally let himself sit down and shake " Cole would walk back to the
cockpit alone.

Taylor would be there.

Taylor would be at the helm, where Cody had left him at the end of the last
chapter, and Taylor would look up with that small worn-thin smile that Cole now
recognized as the smile of a man who had survived too many things by being right
about them, and Taylor would say something dry and small.

And Cole, for the first time in his life, would not respond from the warden's
voice.

He would respond from his own.

But that was later.

For now, he sat on the floor of the cargo bay with the people who had refused to
leave him in it, and he held the chimp who had loved him through everything he
had not yet allowed himself to feel, and he listened to the new soft voice in
the back of his head adjusting to its own existence, and he thought about his
sister, far below, somewhere on a road in Texas, alive, and he thought about a
graveside in Sequoia he had not yet been to, and he thought about the long
careful work that was waiting for him on the other side of this hour.

He was going to be okay.

He was not okay yet.

But he was going to be.

The substance, he understood " turning the thought over in the new clean room of
his mind " had been waiting fifty years for someone to come to it grieving, with
nothing left for it to take.

It had finally found him.

And what it had given him in return for the nothing was the only thing it had
ever had to give.

Itself.

And himself.

And, in the back of his head, in a voice that was still working out how to be a
voice:

Cole?

Yes, Gracie.

The Falcon's on long-range comms. Earth side. Your sister is alive. She wants to
talk to you.

He closed his eyes.

He let the tears come.

"Patch her through," he said.

════════════════════════════════════════════════════════════

﻿RINGS OF DUST

Chapter Nineteen

The Boarding

Taylor Glenn was confined to his quarters, but confinement on a ship this size
was a relative concept. He had the run of his bunk, his console, and a porthole
that showed him Saturn's rings whenever the ship's slow rotation brought them
around. He ate what was delivered to him. He answered Gracie's daily status
queries with the same flat efficiency he had brought to everything else. He did
not apologize and he did not explain, because Taylor did not operate in the
register where those things happened.

Cole had removed his navigation access and left everything else intact. The
logic was clean: they needed Taylor's systems knowledge if something went wrong
with the harvest operations, and they were three months from the retrieval
window, and executing Taylor in deep space with no authority to do so was not
something Cole was prepared to become. So Taylor sat in his quarters with his
equations and his porthole and his expressions that communicated nothing, and
Cole managed the distance between them the way you manage a splinter you can't
reach — by leaving it alone and watching for infection.

It was Ricardo who told Cole that this was going to cost him something later.

"Not because he deserves better," Ricardo said. They were in the galley, the
Saturn light coming through the porthole at the long gold angle that meant
mid-cycle. "Because you do."

Cole looked at his food.

"You can't carry a man's guilt for him and run a mission at the same time,"
Ricardo said. "One of those jobs is going to give."

"Noted," Cole said.

Ricardo watched him for a moment, then ate his lunch.

The harvest was proceeding. After the bay incident, after the DarkX and the four
of them and the new voice in Cole's head that he was still getting acquainted
with, the operations had resumed with the careful precision of people who had
been reminded of the stakes. Sam ran the collection sequences. Bitboy, operating
on three limbs and a temporary brace, had been outperforming the functional
members of the crew for the last two weeks out of what Cole privately suspected
was pride. Gracie tracked everything and communicated it to Cole in the low
steady register she had settled into — not the cold authoritative voice of iRIS,
not quite warm either, something between: professional, present, and
occasionally something that might have been concern.

Cole, you have not slept the full recommended cycle in six days, she said one
morning, not into his ear but in the space behind it.

"I know."

The substance affected sleep architecture. The studies are incomplete. I don't
have enough data to know what the correct response is.

"Neither do I."

A pause. Then: I know about the copper-wire ring. Sam told me, in a systems
query. You made it from a hummingbird feeder. You told her to keep it.

Cole looked at the porthole. Saturn turned. The rings were the color of old bone
in this light.

"Yeah."

She still has it. Wherever she is.

He looked at the porthole. That was enough for now.

---

The vessel appeared on long-range scan at 0314 hours, ship time, which meant Sam
was monitoring and Cole was sleeping his fragmented six-hour cycle and Bitboy
was in the cargo bay doing something Ricardo described later as "maintenance
with an unnecessarily large wrench."

Sam woke Cole with the sensor alert. He was in the cockpit in four minutes.

The vessel was Federation-registered. Rapid Response Medical Support, registry
number FED-MRS-0409, inbound from the inner system on a route that had
originated — Gracie confirmed, pulling the trajectory — from Earth orbit five
weeks ago. They had not announced themselves. Gracie had flagged the contact at
the outer edge of their scanner range and been watching it for ninety minutes.

"It didn't announce," Sam said. "Running silent until now. That's not emergency
protocol."

"No," Cole said. He looked at the registration. "Pull everything on
FED-MRS-0409."

Gracie had already pulled it. The ship was registered to a Federation Medical
Corps contractor. The contractor's operating license had been filed eight months
ago — two weeks after the Golden Dawn's mission had been authorized. The parent
company was incorporated in Geneva. The Geneva office's primary financial backer
was a holding entity registered in Singapore.

Cole looked at Gracie's work in the display and did not say what he was
thinking.

He thought it instead. She heard it.

Alpha, Gracie said.

"Can you tell me who's aboard."

Gracie took six seconds, which was long for her. Biometric scanning at this
distance is imprecise. I'm reading two crew, possibly three. One heat signature
is significantly larger. One is standard human range. One is — a brief pause —
anomalous.

"Anomalous how."

The profile suggests augmentation. Significant augmentation. Metallic
distribution in the upper thorax and skull consistent with Federation-spec
neuroprosthetic plating. It's not military standard issue. It's — another pause,
and Cole would not recognize until later that the pause was Gracie running a
comparison against a database entry and deciding how to say what she found — it
doesn't match anything in the official registry.

Cole said nothing.

"Alpha custom spec," Sam said, quietly.

"Yeah." Cole looked at the approach vector. "They're going to ask to dock."

"We don't have to let them."

"If we refuse, they'll know we know. That buys us — what? Four more weeks of
wondering when the other shoe drops." He looked at the window. "DeMint needs to
know. Open a relay to Earth."

"It'll take eleven minutes."

"Open it. And then let them know we see them. I want them talking before they
reach docking range."

---

She came through the docking tube alone. The two crew of FED-MRS-0409
stayed aboard their vessel — Gracie was tracking them — and she came through
carrying a case in her right hand that Gracie had already flagged: standard
supply dimensions, biometric-locked.

Alpha Corp matte-black. No insignia. No rank markings. Visor down.

She stopped at the threshold and said nothing.

Cole looked at her. "You want to start somewhere."

No answer. The visor stayed down.

Bitboy moved first.

He had positioned himself at the cargo bay entry when the docking clamp
deployed — Cole had not put him there, he had simply arrived and stood,
which was the thing Bitboy did when his threat-assessment protocols had
flagged something his vocoder couldn't yet express. Now he walked forward
two paces, stopped, and fixed the figure with the flat amber attention of
his operational scan. His LED eyes cycled. His threat panel lit.

It read:

**SHE SMELLS LIKE SHARON.**

Ricardo looked at the panel.

He looked at Cole.

He looked back at the panel.

"I want it on record," Ricardo said, "that I have no idea how to file
that in any existing threat category."

Cole was already looking at the visor.

"Take it off," he said.

The figure did not move.

"Gracie."

*I've been running the biometric match since she crossed the docking
threshold.* A pause — the weight of it landing before the words did.
*Cole. Ask her again.*

"Take off the visor." His voice had gone quieter than it should have been
in a room with a standing threat. "Please."

A beat. The hand came up. The visor released.

Cole registered what happened next in fragments.

The visor came up.

The face beneath it was not a face he expected to see. It was a face that had
been mapped over something else — the left side still recognizably,
vertiginously, impossibly her: the jawline, the cheekbone, the cast of her eyes.
The right side had been replaced. Plates the color of gunmetal, following the
curve of the skull, framing the right eye which was no longer the color he knew.
The eye itself was enhanced — the iris ringed with a faint bronze indicator,
tracking with the precision of something that did not blink the same way the
left eye blinked. The skin where the plating met flesh was smooth, sealed, the
junction invisible except as a faint seam.

He understood what the DarkX had done to him in the cargo bay, the thing about
grief and futures and the sentence that had not yet made sense: *There will be a
day when you sit beside something coming back in pieces, and you will understand
what it took for him to die. The love and the fury at the same time.*

His mouth opened and did not say her name.

Gracie said it. Quietly. Inside his head. Only for him.

*Sharon.*

---

She attacked him two seconds later.

He had not been in fighting shape. He had been standing in the middle of a
conversation, his hands at his sides, his brain still several seconds behind the
visual data. She moved with the speed of someone who had been waiting for
exactly this moment — the case hit the deck with a ringing clang, the lock
releasing, and what came out of it was not medical equipment.

Cole got his arm up in time to deflect the first strike, which drove a sharp
pain up through his elbow that he was still feeling two minutes later. She was
fast. She was faster than he had been when they had sparred in the cabin,
before, when she had taught him what she called *efficiency* and he called
*cheating* because she fought like someone who had never once fought fair and
didn't see the point. But this was faster than that. This was something else.

He caught her wrist on the second strike and held it — and felt the metal under
the flesh, the reinforced joint, the structural change — and he understood this
was not going to be what he had imagined on the rare occasions he had allowed
himself to imagine it.

She swept his legs.

He went down hard. The deck plate rang.

She was on him immediately, the enhanced arm pressing into his chest with the
controlled weight of someone who had been calibrated to suppress rather than
kill — and that told him something — and he used the moment it gave him to roll,
putting her off-balance, coming back to his feet at the cost of a hit to the
ribs that he felt in his spine.

"Sam," he said. Not a shout. Level.

"Watching," Sam's voice came through the comms. "Ricardo is thirty seconds out."

"Tell him to stay back. Tell him not to touch her."

A pause. "Cole—"

"Tell him. Nobody touches her but me."

He got his back to the bulkhead. She came at him again, and he let her close —
which was the counterintuitive move, the one she hadn't calibrated for — and he
took the hit to his side and wrapped his arms around her and held. He held the
way you hold something that's trying to get away from you, not to pin it but
because holding is the only alternative to letting go, and letting go was not
available to him.

She was stronger. He knew she was stronger. The plating and the augmentation
gave her a force he wasn't going to be able to sustain. He wasn't trying to win.
He was trying to buy sixty seconds.

"Sharon," he said. Not loudly.

No change.

"Sharon." His voice, with her name in it, sounded like a different voice from
the one he usually had. Quieter and more naked. "I know you're in there. I know
you can hear me. You can fight this."

Nothing. The conditioning was not a wall someone knocked on.

She got her arm free and drove her elbow up. He got his chin out of the way in
time and took it on the side of the head instead, which sent the world sideways
and put him on one knee. His vision doubled, then resolved. She was standing
over him.

She had the secondary weapon from the case. Cole could see that it was not
lethal at this range — it was a suppression device, designed to drop a human
without killing, and that told him the same thing as before. She had not been
sent to kill him. She had been sent to take him. Alive. For purposes he did not
particularly want to contemplate.

He looked up at her face.

He looked at the part that was still her. The left eye, which had always been
slightly brighter than the right, as if she'd absorbed more of whatever light
was in the room and was running it somewhere useful. The line of her jaw. The
faint scar above the left eyebrow from the time, years ago, she had walked into
a warehouse door because she was reading a manifest and not watching where she
was going, and had been annoyed about it for a week, and then had decided the
scar was an interesting story to have.

He looked at those things and felt the fury and the grief with equal weight,
both at the same time, and he had been told this moment was coming and it had
come, and it was everything he had been told it would be and worse.

"Cole," Gracie said. Inside his head only. Quiet. Urgent.

"I know."

"The compound. The DarkX activated it in your bloodstream — I've been tracking
the molecular profile. You're carrying a significantly amplified version of
Conrad's counter-program. It's designed to reverse conditioning of this
architecture. I know this architecture. It's Gracie's architecture."

He understood, very slowly, what she was saying.

"She built it to fail. She built it with a key."

"Conrad built the original. Alpha built the modification on top of it. But
the foundation is Gracie's foundation, and I know the foundation. The
counter-program needs to reach her bloodstream. Direct contact. Mucous membrane
or open wound, enough concentration."

Cole looked at his right hand.

The knuckles were bleeding from the second exchange. His palm, where the
bulkhead had caught him when he went down, had a four-inch laceration that was
producing more blood than he'd noticed at the time.

He looked up at Sharon's face.

"It needs to be enough," he said. "It needs to be a real contact."

"Yes."

She pressed the suppression device forward. He was running out of time.

He did the thing that made no tactical sense. He put the suppression device
aside with both hands — slowly, the way you handle something you're choosing not
to use — and he looked at her, and he said:

"I'm not going to fight you anymore."

Something flickered in the left eye.

Not recognition. Not yet. But the conditioning was a thing that had been built
for a specific input-output sequence, and this was not in the sequence, and the
absence of the expected input created a small uncertain space in the program.

He used that space.

He reached up with his right hand — the bleeding one — and placed his palm
against the left side of her face. Against the cheek that was still hers.
Against the skin that was still skin.

She went completely still.

Not attacking. Not retreating. Just — still. The way a person goes still when
something in them doesn't know what to do with what's happening.

Cole held his hand against her face and felt the warmth of her beneath the
augmentation and said, very quietly, her name.

Not the name she'd been given for this operation. Her name.

"Sharon."

---

The compound didn't work the way Gracie had described — quick, clean, a switch
thrown. It was slow. It was slower than anything Cole had imagined, which meant
it was the longest he had stood in one place holding one thing in the entirety
of his life.

He kept his palm against her cheek. She had not moved. The weapon had dropped to
the deck. The enhanced arm hung at her side. The left eye was doing something he
could not read, the pupil cycling through rapid micro-adjustments, and Gracie,
quietly, told him: *it's working. Don't move.*

He didn't move.

Ricardo was at the corridor entrance. Cole could hear him without looking — the
particular quality of Ricardo's breathing when he was controlling it, the inhale
held for two beats too long before the exhale. Sam was behind him. Bitboy was
behind Sam, and Bitboy was making no sound at all, which was louder than any
noise he could have made.

The bronze indicator ring in the enhanced eye went dark.

Then the left eye filled.

Not quickly. The way a room fills with light when a door opens — gradually, from
the edges first, and then all at once.

She made a sound that was not language. Not yet. It was the sound of someone
surfacing from a very long depth, the involuntary exhalation of a person who
didn't know they'd been holding their breath until the breath was released.

Cole did not move his hand.

He waited.

He had been waiting for thirty-two years, and he did not know it until this
moment, and the waiting had turned out to be worth it, and he would have waited
thirty-two more if that was what it took.

"Cole."

Her voice.

Not filtered. Not processed. Not the flat modulation of a suit speaker or the
calibrated distance of the conditioning. Her voice, with the roughness in the
lower register and the slight forward lean it always had when she was about to
say something she meant. His name in it like it had always been in it — two
syllables doing the work of every conversation they had ever had.

"Cole."

"Yeah," he said. His voice came out the same way her voice had — rough, unready,
something that had been kept behind a closed door and was now open to the air.

She did not yet know where she was. He could see that. The left eye was tracking
— the room, the deck plate, his face — with the quick nonlinear scan of someone
orienting without context. The enhanced eye was moving differently, still
processing, its bronze indicator still dark.

"What," she said. She stopped. She started again. "What year is it."

"2043," he said. "November."

She looked at her right hand. At the augmentation. At the plating on her skull,
which she was touching now with the fingers of her left hand — carefully, like
you'd touch something you didn't trust, something that had been put there while
you weren't looking.

"I was going to Emily," she said.

"I know."

"We were in a lot." Her voice had found itself now, but the words were coming
the way they come after a long sleep — in the right order but without full
attachment to what they meant yet. "Dead end. Nothing around. Broken asphalt.
There were men."

"I know."

"They didn't kill me."

"No."

She looked at him. The left eye — her eye — with something in it that he could
see working through layers, each one unfamiliar, arriving at him through
whatever filter they'd put between her and herself. He stayed very still.

"Are you real," she said.

"Yes."

She looked at his hand, which was still pressed against her cheek. At the blood,
which had dried along the edge of his palm.

She reached up and covered his hand with hers. Her left hand, the unaugmented
one. The hand he knew. The hand that had pressed blackberry jam to his lips in a
cabin in the Sierras that was, right now, about as far away from a cargo deck in
Saturn orbit as a place could get.

"Are you hurt," she said.

"Not badly."

She studied his face. She was looking for something — he could see her looking
for it, not finding the word, looking anyway.

"I don't have it all," she said. "Whatever they did — I don't have it all back.
There are gaps. There are — big ones." Her voice was steady. She was describing
a situation. She had always been good at describing situations. "How long."

"We don't know exactly. Six months, maybe seven."

She absorbed this. Her thumb moved against the back of his hand, slowly, the way
it used to when she was thinking.

"Emily," she said.

"Alive. She's on Earth. She helped get you back — the counter-program. It's a
long story."

"Tell me."

"Later. There's time."

She looked at the deck. She looked at the augmentation on her right arm. She was
taking inventory. He recognized this, too — this was the Sharon who had walked
into every warehouse and every back-room deal and every situation that was not
what it was supposed to be, and had looked at it, and done her assessment, and
decided what was salvageable and what wasn't.

"The eye," she said. "The right one. Does it still work?"

"Gracie's been monitoring since you came aboard. She says it's functional — the
conditioning link is severed, but the hardware itself is intact."

"I can feel it." She touched the edge of the plating. "I can feel the difference
between what I'm seeing with one and what I'm seeing with the other." A pause.
"It's strange. Not bad strange. Just — two different kinds of seeing."

Cole said nothing. He was doing his own inventory — not of her, but of himself.
The space between where he had been three months ago, when he had gotten on his
knees in the cargo bay with nothing left, and where he was now. It was not a
short distance. He did not know yet whether he had the language for it.

"I didn't know where I was," Sharon said. "In that lot, there were
men, and then I wasn't anywhere. And then I was this. And I knew what I was
doing and I thought I wanted to do it and — I didn't. There was something
underneath it that didn't. Something that kept saying the name." Her left eye
found his. "It kept saying your name. The whole time. It kept saying it and I
couldn't reach it."

"I know," he said, and he was saying it on behalf of all four of them — the
baby, the boy, the man, the old man — and on behalf of every version of himself
that had been unable to reach what it needed.

She looked up at the overhead, at the recycled-air ceiling of the ship, at the
faint vibration of the hull that you only felt when you stopped moving.

"Saturn," she said.

"Yes."

"How do the rings look."

He almost smiled. "Better in person."

She looked at him. The left eye was steady now, fully present in the way he
remembered, the way it always had been when she had decided something and was
about to say it.

"I'm not going to fall apart on you," she said. "I want you to know that."

"I know you're not."

"I might need time. There are pieces I can't reach yet." A pause. "But I'm not
going to fall apart."

"Sharon," he said.

"What."

"I know."

She was quiet for a moment. In the corridor, Ricardo had stopped controlling his
breathing. Cole could hear him not controlling it. He could hear Bitboy making a
very small sound that was not sadness and not relief and was both of those
things.

"Is there anywhere to sit down on this ship," Sharon said. "I've been standing
for a very long time."

---

The two crew members of FED-MRS-0409 did not wait. By the time Cole had gotten
Sharon to the medical bay and Sam had run the first assessment, the Alpha
vessel had disengaged from the docking collar and begun its departure on a
course that did not require communication. Gracie tracked it until it left
scanner range.

Cole sent the full incident report to DeMint and to the relay address Emily had
given him when they'd spoken, which Gracie confirmed went through a Collective
routing node. Then he sat in the medical bay on the stool that was bolted to the
wall beside the examination table, and he did not leave.

Sam ran the assessment in the careful methodical way Sam ran everything. She
talked as she worked — not to Cole, to Sharon — describing what she was seeing,
what it meant, what it didn't mean, what the next steps looked like. Sharon
asked questions. Good questions, specific questions, questions that told Sam she
was dealing with a person who was accustomed to taking in technical information
and using it. Sam's voice had the quality it always had when she was doing
something she was good at — level and fast and completely without performance.

Ricardo brought food at some point. He put it on the table beside Cole without
saying anything. Cole ate it without tasting it. Bitboy climbed into Sharon's
lap during the assessment, the way Bitboy had always climbed into the laps of
people he had decided were his, and Sharon looked at him with the half-augmented
face and said, very quietly:

"Hi, you little bastard."

Bitboy made a sound that was not in his usual register.

Sharon looked at Cole over the top of Bitboy's head.

"He gets bigger every time," she said.

"He eats whatever he wants."

"Still the ethanol?"

"Still the ethanol."

She scratched behind Bitboy's ear — the place that made all four mechanical arms
go briefly limp. Bitboy sighed with the whole of himself. Sharon watched this
with an expression that Cole had not seen from her before — or had not been able
to see, or had not allowed himself to see, or had not had the cleared-out
interior necessary for seeing. It was the expression of someone who understood,
for the first time in a long time, where they were.

"There are gaps," she said again, looking at Bitboy. "I keep running into them.
Like there's a room and the door is there but it won't open yet." She looked up.
"I remember the cabin. I remember that morning — the fire, the pancakes. I
remember Emily's face when she thought I wasn't looking at her."

"What else," he said.

"I remember a lot at the end of a dead road." A pause. "I remember you, before
that. Before the mission." She met his eyes. "I remember the last thing I said
to you."

He had not known what she'd said. He had never been told. Sharon had been at the
cabin with him and then Sharon had not been there, and whatever had happened
